The Ontological Status of Universals
Al-Taftazani discusses the existence of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between absolute and specific existence and the intermediate status of universals.

Core Idea
Universals are neither fully existent nor non-existent.

In this passage, al-Taftazani discusses a nuanced issue: the ontological status of universals. He begins with an example of a statement about animals and then clarifies that absolute existence is non-existent, while specific existence is real and additional to the essence. This distinction helps avoid confusion about whether universals are merely mental constructs or have some reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the phrase 'الحيوان ذو لا حيوان' which is unusual; it may refer to a paradoxical statement.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage contrasts absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) with specific existence (al-wujud al-khas). Notice that absolute existence is said to be non-existent (ma'dum), while specific existence is existent (mawjud) and additional to the essence.
3
Understand the warning against regress
Al-Taftazani warns against infinite regress by stating that the universal has no other existence to cause a chain. Then he clarifies that if 'existent by an existence' means the existence is identical to the essence, that is acceptable; but if it means the essence itself is existence, then no distinction remains between existent and non-existent.
This leads to the conclusion that the intermediate status is a strong doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولنا الحيوان ذو لا حيوان هو السواد أو البياض وسائر ما يقوم به من الأعراض. والأقرب أنه إن أريد الوجود المطلق فمعدوم أو الخاص كوجود الواجب، ووجود الإنسان فموجود ووجوده زائد عليه، عارض له هو المطلق أو الحصة منه، وليس له وجود آخر ليتسلسل، فإن أريد بكونه موجوداً بوجود هو نفسه هذا المعنى فحق. وإن أريد بمعنى أنه نفسه وجود فلا يدفع بين المعدوم والموجود بمعنى ماله الوجود هذا والحق أن الواسطة هذه لشبهة قوية.
Our saying 'the animal which is not animal' is blackness or whiteness and the rest of the accidents that subsist in it. And the closest is that if absolute existence is intended, it is non-existent, or specific existence like the existence of the Necessary, and the existence of man is existent and its existence is additional to it, an accident that is the absolute or the portion of it, and it has no other existence to cause regress. If it is intended that it is existent by an existence that is itself this meaning, it is correct. And if it is intended that it itself is existence, then there is no distinction between the non-existent and the existent in the sense of that which has existence. This and the truth is that the intermediary is this due to a strong doubt.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the ontological status of universals. The author begins with an example: the statement 'the animal which is not animal' refers to accidents like blackness or whiteness that subsist in a subject. He then analyzes the concept of existence as applied to universals. He distinguishes between absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) which is non-existent, and specific existence (al-wujud al-khas) like the existence of the Necessary Being (wajib) or the existence of man. For man, existence is additional to the essence. He warns against infinite regress and clarifies that if 'existent by an existence' means the existence is identical to the essence, that is acceptable, but if it means the essence itself is existence, then there is no difference between the existent and non-existent. The conclusion is that the intermediate position (the universal being neither fully existent nor non-existent) is a strong doubt.

Key Terms
الوجود المطلق
الوجود الخاص
الواسطة
المعدوم
الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that universals are either fully existent or fully non-existent.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that universals have an intermediate status: absolute existence is non-existent, but specific existence is real and additional to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq)?
A) It is existent
B) It is non-existent
C) It is both existent and non-existent
D) It is the same as specific existence

Key Takeaway
The ontological status of universals depends on whether existence is considered absolutely or specifically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about abstract concepts like 'justice' or 'mercy', do you assume they have an independent reality? This lesson teaches that their existence is nuanced—neither fully existent nor non-existent. How does this affect the way you relate to Allah's attributes? Will you now examine whether you treat them as absolute or specific existences?
?
The author warns against confusing existence itself with the existent. In your own moments of doubt or pride, have you ever claimed something exists simply by defining it? Identify one belief you hold that might collapse if you applied this distinction. What will you do to correct it?
?
This lesson shows that even scholars grapple with the 'strong doubt' of intermediate existence. Reflect on a theological doubt you have—perhaps about predestination or divine attributes. Are you willing to accept an intermediate position based on this reasoning, or does certainty demand a simpler answer? What step will you take today to deepen your understanding rather than dismiss the nuance?

intermediate
6 min
The Universal Is Neither Existent Nor Non-Existent
Arguments that the universal is neither existent nor non-existent using three examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the universal (kulli) is neither existent nor non-existent in the external world, and how it functions as a conceptual part of its particulars.

Core Idea
The universal is neither existent nor non-existent; it exists only as a concept in the mind.

In this passage, al-Taftazani tackles a fundamental puzzle: if universals like 'man' are not found as separate entities outside the mind, are they then non-existent? He argues they are not, because they are part of the reality of particulars. However, they are also not existent in themselves, as that would make them individuated. This careful balancing act is essential for understanding how universals relate to their instances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the three examples al-Taftazani uses.
2
Identify the three examples
The passage presents three cases: the species 'man', the genus 'animal', and the genus of accidents (blackness-color). For each, note the reasoning why it is not existent and not non-existent.
3
Understand the key reasoning
For each example, al-Taftazani argues that if the universal were existent, it would be individuated and lose its universality. If it were non-existent, it could not be a part of its particulars. Reflect on why both extremes are impossible.
The phrase 'امتناع تقدم الموجود بالمعدوم' is crucial for the non-existence argument.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: what does this argument imply about the ontological status of universals? They are not separate entities, yet they have a conceptual reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن الكلي الذي له جزئيات متحققة مثل الإنسان ليس بموجود، وإلا لكان مشخصاً، فلا يكون كلياً، ولا معدوماً، وإلا لما كان جزءاً من جزئياته الموجودة، كزيد مثلاً، لامتناع تقدم الموجود بالمعدوم، وأيضاً الجنس كالحيوان ليس بموجود لكليته، ولا معدوم لكونه جزءاً من الماهية الحقيقية كالإنسان، وأيضاً جنس الماهيات الحقيقية من الأعراض، كلونية السواد ليس بمعدوم لما ذكر ولا موجود لاستلزامه قيام العرض بالعرض. والكل فاسد لأن جزء المركب سيما المحمول عليه لا يكون عرضاً قائماً به ولا بالعكس، وكذا المحمول الأعم، والنعت لا يلزم أن يكون عرضاً للموضوع بل يمتنع لأن العرض لا يكون محمولاً على المحل إلا بالاشتقاق، وكذا المقوم للشيء بمعنى كونه داخلا في قوامه كالجنس للنوع، أو بمعنى كونه علة لتقومه وتحصله ماهية حقيقية كالفصل للجنس، لا يقتضي كون ذلك الشيء عرضاً قائماً به سيما إذا كان محمولاً، ألا ترى أن الحيوان محمول على الإنسان مقول له، ومحمول على الناطق خارج، والناطق مقوم له علة لتحصله، وكان الغلط من اشتراك لفظ العروض والقيام، وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار في المتن بقوله على أن مثل هذا القيام ليس من قيام العرض بالمحل في شيء، ولما كان ههنا به تحقيق به يخرج الجواب عن هذا الوجه بالكلية، جعلناه العمدة، وهو أن ليس في الخارج تمايز بين الكلي والتشخص يحصل من تركيبهما الشخصي، ولا بين الجنس والفصل يحصل من تركيبهما النوع، لظهور أن ليس في الخارج شيء هو الإنسان الكلي، وآخر هو خصوصية زيد يتركب منهما زيد.
And the second: that the universal which has realized particulars, like man, is not existent, otherwise it would be individuated, so it would not be universal, and it is not non-existent, otherwise it would not be a part of its existent particulars, like Zayd for example, because the existent cannot precede the non-existent. Also, the genus like animal is not existent due to its universality, and not non-existent because it is part of the true quiddity like man. Also, the genus of true quiddities from accidents, like the blackness-color is not non-existent for the mentioned reason, and not existent because it entails the subsistence of accident by accident. And all are corrupt because a part of the compound, especially the predicate, cannot be an accident subsisting in it nor vice versa, and likewise the more general predicate, and the attribute is not necessarily an accident of the subject, rather it is impossible because an accident is predicated of the subject only by derivation. Likewise, the constituent of a thing, in the sense of being a part of its constitution like genus for species, or in the sense of being a cause for its constitution and realization as a true quiddity like differentia for genus, does not imply that that thing is an accident subsisting in it, especially if it is predicated. Do you not see that animal is predicated of man as a predicate, and predicated of 'natiq' externally, and 'natiq' is a constituent of it, a cause for its realization. And the mistake arose from the shared usage of the terms 'urud and qiyam. And to what we have mentioned, the text alluded by saying: 'That such a predication is not at all like the subsistence of an accident in a subject.' And since here we have an investigation by which the answer to this aspect is completely eliminated, we made it the main point: that there is no distinction outside between the universal and the individuation that results from their personal composition, nor between genus and differentia that results from their specific composition, because it is evident that there is nothing outside that is the universal man, and another that is the particularity of Zayd from which Zayd is composed.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the ontological status of the universal (kulli). The author presents a second point: the universal, such as the species 'man', is not existent in a way that would make it an individuated entity, because then it would not be universal. But also it is not non-existent, because then it could not be a part of its particulars. He gives similar reasoning for genus: animal is not existent due to its universality, but not non-existent as it is part of the true quiddity. Then he addresses the specific case of the genus of accidents (like blackness-color) and refutes the view that such a genus would entail an accident subsisting in another accident. He argues that the positions claiming the predicate or constituent is an accident are all corrupt. He explains that the predicate (mahmul) is not necessarily an accident, and that being a constituent (muqawwim) does not imply being an accident. The mistake (ghalat) comes from the ambiguity of the terms 'urud (accidentalness) and qiyam (subsistence). He then refers to the text's statement that such predication is not like the subsistence of an accident. Finally, he presents the main point (al-umda): there is no external distinction between the universal and the individuation, nor between genus and differentia, because there is no separate universal man and particular Zayd; rather, the particular is composed of both in the mind.

Key Terms
الكلي
الجزئيات
الجنس
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universals are either fully existent separate entities or completely non-existent.
Correction: Universals are neither existent as separate entities nor non-existent; they are conceptually real as parts of quiddities.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Taftazani argue that the universal 'man' is not non-existent?
A) Because it has realized particulars
B) Because it would entail an infinite regress
C) Because it is an accident
D) Because it is a differentia

Key Takeaway
Universals are neither existent nor non-existent in the external world; they are conceptual components of quiddities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a universal concept you rely on daily, like 'justice' or 'mercy'. How does realizing that this universal is neither existent nor non-existent change the way you seek to manifest it in your next interaction? Will you treat it as a mere abstraction or as something real that must be embodied in particular acts?
?
If the universal 'man' is not existent on its own, what does that mean for your identity? Are you simply a particular instance of a concept, or do you have a deeper connection to the quiddity? How will this affect your sense of purpose today?
?
Consider your belief in the attributes of God (e.g., 'Knowledge', 'Power')—are they universals or unique? This lesson shows that universals cannot be existent as separate entities. How does this shape your understanding of God's attributes? Will you now be more careful not to treat them as independent realities?

Refuting That the Predicate or Constituent Is an Accident
Refutation of the claim that the predicate or constituent is an accident, and identification of the semantic mistake.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the predicate or constituent of a thing cannot be an accident, and how ambiguity in terminology ('urud and qiyam) led to this error.

Core Idea
Being predicated or being a constituent does not make something an accident; the mistake comes from ambiguous terms.

In the previous lesson, we saw that universals are neither existent nor non-existent. But someone might argue that because universals are predicated of particulars or are constituents of quiddities, they must be accidents. Al-Taftazani now refutes this, explaining the logical relationship between predicate, constituent, and accident. He also identifies the root cause of the confusion: the ambiguity of the terms 'urud (occurrence) and qiyam (subsistence).

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation carefully
Read the passage and its translation. Notice the distinction between predicate and accident, and the discussion of the constituent (muqawwim).
2
Identify the two cases refuted
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that the predicate is an accident, and then the claim that the constituent is an accident. Note the reasoning for each.
3
Understand the example of animal and natiq
He uses the example: animal is predicated of man and of natiq, and natiq is a constituent of man, yet neither is an accident. Trace this example to see why.
4
Recognize the root mistake
The error arises from the shared usage of 'urud and qiyam for both logical predication and accidental inherence. Understand this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن الكلي الذي له جزئيات متحققة مثل الإنسان ليس بموجود، وإلا لكان مشخصاً، فلا يكون كلياً، ولا معدوماً، وإلا لما كان جزءاً من جزئياته الموجودة، كزيد مثلاً، لامتناع تقدم الموجود بالمعدوم، وأيضاً الجنس كالحيوان ليس بموجود لكليته، ولا معدوم لكونه جزءاً من الماهية الحقيقية كالإنسان، وأيضاً جنس الماهيات الحقيقية من الأعراض، كلونية السواد ليس بمعدوم لما ذكر ولا موجود لاستلزامه قيام العرض بالعرض. والكل فاسد لأن جزء المركب سيما المحمول عليه لا يكون عرضاً قائماً به ولا بالعكس، وكذا المحمول الأعم، والنعت لا يلزم أن يكون عرضاً للموضوع بل يمتنع لأن العرض لا يكون محمولاً على المحل إلا بالاشتقاق، وكذا المقوم للشيء بمعنى كونه داخلا في قوامه كالجنس للنوع، أو بمعنى كونه علة لتقومه وتحصله ماهية حقيقية كالفصل للجنس، لا يقتضي كون ذلك الشيء عرضاً قائماً به سيما إذا كان محمولاً، ألا ترى أن الحيوان محمول على الإنسان مقول له، ومحمول على الناطق خارج، والناطق مقوم له علة لتحصله، وكان الغلط من اشتراك لفظ العروض والقيام، وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار في المتن بقوله على أن مثل هذا القيام ليس من قيام العرض بالمحل في شيء، ولما كان ههنا به تحقيق به يخرج الجواب عن هذا الوجه بالكلية، جعلناه العمدة، وهو أن ليس في الخارج تمايز بين الكلي والتشخص يحصل من تركيبهما الشخصي، ولا بين الجنس والفصل يحصل من تركيبهما النوع، لظهور أن ليس في الخارج شيء هو الإنسان الكلي، وآخر هو خصوصية زيد يتركب منهما زيد.
And the second: that the universal which has realized particulars, like man, is not existent, otherwise it would be individuated, so it would not be universal, and it is not non-existent, otherwise it would not be a part of its existent particulars, like Zayd for example, because the existent cannot precede the non-existent. Also, the genus like animal is not existent due to its universality, and not non-existent because it is part of the true quiddity like man. Also, the genus of true quiddities from accidents, like the blackness-color is not non-existent for the mentioned reason, and not existent because it entails the subsistence of accident by accident. And all are corrupt because a part of the compound, especially the predicate, cannot be an accident subsisting in it nor vice versa, and likewise the more general predicate, and the attribute is not necessarily an accident of the subject, rather it is impossible because an accident is predicated of the subject only by derivation. Likewise, the constituent of a thing, in the sense of being a part of its constitution like genus for species, or in the sense of being a cause for its constitution and realization as a true quiddity like differentia for genus, does not imply that that thing is an accident subsisting in it, especially if it is predicated. Do you not see that animal is predicated of man as a predicate, and predicated of 'natiq' externally, and 'natiq' is a constituent of it, a cause for its realization. And the mistake arose from the shared usage of the terms 'urud and qiyam. And to what we have mentioned, the text alluded by saying: 'That such a predication is not at all like the subsistence of an accident in a subject.' And since here we have an investigation by which the answer to this aspect is completely eliminated, we made it the main point: that there is no distinction outside between the universal and the individuation that results from their personal composition, nor between genus and differentia that results from their specific composition, because it is evident that there is nothing outside that is the universal man, and another that is the particularity of Zayd from which Zayd is composed.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the ontological status of the universal (kulli). The author presents a second point: the universal, such as the species 'man', is not existent in a way that would make it an individuated entity, because then it would not be universal. But also it is not non-existent, because then it could not be a part of its particulars. He gives similar reasoning for genus: animal is not existent due to its universality, but not non-existent as it is part of the true quiddity. Then he addresses the specific case of the genus of accidents (like blackness-color) and refutes the view that such a genus would entail an accident subsisting in another accident. He argues that the positions claiming the predicate or constituent is an accident are all corrupt. He explains that the predicate (mahmul) is not necessarily an accident, and that being a constituent (muqawwim) does not imply being an accident. The mistake (ghalat) comes from the ambiguity of the terms 'urud (accidentalness) and qiyam (subsistence). He then refers to the text's statement that such predication is not like the subsistence of an accident. Finally, he presents the main point (al-umda): there is no external distinction between the universal and the individuation, nor between genus and differentia, because there is no separate universal man and particular Zayd; rather, the particular is composed of both in the mind.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is predicated of another, or is a part of its constitution, then it must be an accident.
Correction: Predication and constitution do not entail accidentalness; the relationship is different from inherence of an accident.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the predicate not necessarily an accident?
A) Because it is a part of the compound
B) Because it is predicated by derivation
C) Because it is a constituent
D) Because it is a genus

Key Takeaway
Predication and constitution do not turn something into an accident; the confusion arises from ambiguous terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever confused what is essential to you (like your nature) with something accidental, assuming a characteristic defines you when it is only a passing attribute? How can you distinguish between the two in your own life?
?
When you describe others, do you sometimes treat predicates (like 'he is a writer') as if they are accidents that could be stripped away, when in fact they are constituents of their identity? How does this passage challenge your assumptions?
?
Reflect on a time you misunderstood a concept because of ambiguous language. How does being precise about terms like 'urud and qiyam protect you from theological error? Will you commit to studying terms carefully before drawing conclusions?

intermediate
4 min
The Main Point: No External Distinction Between Universal and Particular
Conclusive argument that there is no external distinction between universal and particular, genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's main contention: that in the external world, there is no real distinction between the universal and the particular, or between genus and differentia.

Core Idea
Universals and particulars are not separate existents outside the mind; they are mental distinctions.

After refuting the misunderstandings about predicates and accidents, al-Taftazani presents his main point: the universal and the particular do not exist as separate entities in the external world. Nor do genus and differentia exist separately. They are only distinguished in the mind. This is a key insight in Islamic philosophy about the nature of universals.

Guided Path
1
Read the main point passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the reference to the text and the final conclusion.
2
Understand the text allusion
Al-Taftazani refers to the text's statement: 'such a predication is not at all like the subsistence of an accident in a subject.' This summarizes the previous refutation.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The main point is that there is no external composition of universal and particular. Zayd is not composed of a separate universal man and a separate particularity. They are one entity in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن الكلي الذي له جزئيات متحققة مثل الإنسان ليس بموجود، وإلا لكان مشخصاً، فلا يكون كلياً، ولا معدوماً، وإلا لما كان جزءاً من جزئياته الموجودة، كزيد مثلاً، لامتناع تقدم الموجود بالمعدوم، وأيضاً الجنس كالحيوان ليس بموجود لكليته، ولا معدوم لكونه جزءاً من الماهية الحقيقية كالإنسان، وأيضاً جنس الماهيات الحقيقية من الأعراض، كلونية السواد ليس بمعدوم لما ذكر ولا موجود لاستلزامه قيام العرض بالعرض. والكل فاسد لأن جزء المركب سيما المحمول عليه لا يكون عرضاً قائماً به ولا بالعكس، وكذا المحمول الأعم، والنعت لا يلزم أن يكون عرضاً للموضوع بل يمتنع لأن العرض لا يكون محمولاً على المحل إلا بالاشتقاق، وكذا المقوم للشيء بمعنى كونه داخلا في قوامه كالجنس للنوع، أو بمعنى كونه علة لتقومه وتحصله ماهية حقيقية كالفصل للجنس، لا يقتضي كون ذلك الشيء عرضاً قائماً به سيما إذا كان محمولاً، ألا ترى أن الحيوان محمول على الإنسان مقول له، ومحمول على الناطق خارج، والناطق مقوم له علة لتحصله، وكان الغلط من اشتراك لفظ العروض والقيام، وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار في المتن بقوله على أن مثل هذا القيام ليس من قيام العرض بالمحل في شيء، ولما كان ههنا به تحقيق به يخرج الجواب عن هذا الوجه بالكلية، جعلناه العمدة، وهو أن ليس في الخارج تمايز بين الكلي والتشخص يحصل من تركيبهما الشخصي، ولا بين الجنس والفصل يحصل من تركيبهما النوع، لظهور أن ليس في الخارج شيء هو الإنسان الكلي، وآخر هو خصوصية زيد يتركب منهما زيد.
And the second: that the universal which has realized particulars, like man, is not existent, otherwise it would be individuated, so it would not be universal, and it is not non-existent, otherwise it would not be a part of its existent particulars, like Zayd for example, because the existent cannot precede the non-existent. Also, the genus like animal is not existent due to its universality, and not non-existent because it is part of the true quiddity like man. Also, the genus of true quiddities from accidents, like the blackness-color is not non-existent for the mentioned reason, and not existent because it entails the subsistence of accident by accident. And all are corrupt because a part of the compound, especially the predicate, cannot be an accident subsisting in it nor vice versa, and likewise the more general predicate, and the attribute is not necessarily an accident of the subject, rather it is impossible because an accident is predicated of the subject only by derivation. Likewise, the constituent of a thing, in the sense of being a part of its constitution like genus for species, or in the sense of being a cause for its constitution and realization as a true quiddity like differentia for genus, does not imply that that thing is an accident subsisting in it, especially if it is predicated. Do you not see that animal is predicated of man as a predicate, and predicated of 'natiq' externally, and 'natiq' is a constituent of it, a cause for its realization. And the mistake arose from the shared usage of the terms 'urud and qiyam. And to what we have mentioned, the text alluded by saying: 'That such a predication is not at all like the subsistence of an accident in a subject.' And since here we have an investigation by which the answer to this aspect is completely eliminated, we made it the main point: that there is no distinction outside between the universal and the individuation that results from their personal composition, nor between genus and differentia that results from their specific composition, because it is evident that there is nothing outside that is the universal man, and another that is the particularity of Zayd from which Zayd is composed.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument about the ontological status of the universal (kulli). The author presents a second point: the universal, such as the species 'man', is not existent in a way that would make it an individuated entity, because then it would not be universal. But also it is not non-existent, because then it could not be a part of its particulars. He gives similar reasoning for genus: animal is not existent due to its universality, but not non-existent as it is part of the true quiddity. Then he addresses the specific case of the genus of accidents (like blackness-color) and refutes the view that such a genus would entail an accident subsisting in another accident. He argues that the positions claiming the predicate or constituent is an accident are all corrupt. He explains that the predicate (mahmul) is not necessarily an accident, and that being a constituent (muqawwim) does not imply being an accident. The mistake (ghalat) comes from the ambiguity of the terms 'urud (accidentalness) and qiyam (subsistence). He then refers to the text's statement that such predication is not like the subsistence of an accident. Finally, he presents the main point (al-umda): there is no external distinction between the universal and the individuation, nor between genus and differentia, because there is no separate universal man and particular Zayd; rather, the particular is composed of both in the mind.

Key Terms

التشخص
الجنس
الفصل
الخارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universals and particulars exist as separate components that are externally compounded (like a Platonic form plus a particular).
Correction: There is no external composition; the universal and particular are not separate entities outside the mind.

Checkpoint
What does al-Taftazani mean when he says 'ليس في الخارج تمايز بين الكلي والتشخص'?
A) The universal and the particular are identical in the external world
B) There is no real distinction between the universal and the particular outside the mind
C) The universal is not a part of the particular
D) The particular is an accident of the universal

Key Takeaway
The universal and particular are not externally distinct; their composition is only in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you treat abstract categories (like 'Muslim', 'human', 'believer') as if they were more real than the actual individuals you interact with? What would it mean to let go of that and see each person as a single, indivisible reality before God?
?
You are not a combination of a universal 'human' and a particular 'you' — you are one being in God's sight. Does this truth change how you understand your own uniqueness and accountability? What if you truly believed that your identity is not a composite of labels but an indivisible soul?
?
Think of a concept you hold dear, like 'justice' or 'truth'. Have you ever acted as if the abstract concept were more important than the real, messy particulars of a situation? How can this lesson's principle — that universals have no external reality apart from particulars — reshape your daily judgments?

Proof from Ijad and Its Refutation
Presents the third argument for al-hal and refutes it using the example of blindness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for al-hal based on bringing into existence (al-ijad) and its refutation through the principle that predication does not require the existence of the predicate's basis in external reality.

Core Idea
Predication does not require the existence of the attribute in external reality.

This lesson presents a key argument for the existence of states (al-ahwal) based on the act of bringing into existence (al-ijad). The argument claims that al-ijad cannot be classified as existent or non-existent, so it must be a state. However, the refutation shows that predication can be valid even when the attribute is non-existent, using examples like 'Zayd is blind'. Understanding this debate clarifies the relationship between language, reality, and theological attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for al-hal
Read the initial proof: al-ijad cannot be existent (else infinite regress) nor non-existent (else agent not bringer). Therefore it must be a state.
Notice the structure: disjunctive syllogism.
2
Examine the initial response
The response (qulna) points out that non-existence of the predicate's basis does not negate predication, using Zayd is blind as an example.
This challenges the assumption that predication requires external existence.
3
Follow the restated argument
The argument is repeated more fully: if al-ijad were non-existent, no new attribute would accrue to the agent, so he wouldn't be called bringer.
This sharpens the original point.
4
Understand the final answer
The answer chooses that al-ijad is non-existent and denies the consequence. It explains that affirmative predication is valid even when the attribute is non-existent externally.
The examples of blindness and impossibility illustrate the principle.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation undermines the proof for al-hal from ijad, and think about what it means for the theory of states.
This connects to the broader debate on attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أدلة إثبات الحال: الإيجاد ليس بموجود، وإلا احتاج إلى إيجاد آخر وتسلسل ولا معدوم وإلا لما كان الفاعل موجداً. قلنا: عدم مبدأ المحمول لا يستلزم عدم الحمل، فزيد أعمى، مع أن العمى معدوم واجتماع الضدين ممتنع، مع أن الامتناع معدوم. أي من وجوه إثبات الحال: إن الإيجاد ليس بموجود، وإلا احتاج إلى إيجاد له محتاج إلى آخر، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له، ولا معدوم، وإلا لما كان الفاعل موجداً، لأنه بعد صدور المعلول عنه، لم يحصل له صفة وكما لم يكن قبل الصدور موجداً، فكذا بعده لا يقال إيجاد الإيجاد عينه، لأنا نقول: مثل هذا لا يصح في الأمور الموجودة، لأن ذلك إيجاد للمعلول، وهذا إيجاد للوصف الذي هو إيجاد. والجواب إنا نختار أنه معدوم، ولا نسلم لزوم أن لا يكون الفاعل موجداً، فإن صحة الحمل الإيجاب لا تنافي كون الوصف، الذي أخذ منه المحمول معدوماً وما في الخارج كما في قولنا زيد أعمى في الخارج، واجتماع الضدين ممتنع في الخارج، مع أن كلاً من العمى والامتناع معدوم في الخارج.
Among the proofs for affirming the state: Bringing into existence (al-ījād) is not existent, otherwise it would need another bringing into existence and an infinite regress; nor is it non-existent, otherwise the agent would not be a bringer into existence. We said: The non-existence of the basis of the predicate does not necessitate the non-existence of the predication. Thus Zayd is blind, even though blindness is non-existent; and the conjunction of two opposites is impossible, even though impossibility is non-existent. That is, among the ways of affirming the state: Bringing into existence is not existent, otherwise it would need a bringing into existence that itself needs another, and so on ad infinitum; nor is it non-existent, otherwise the agent would not be a bringer into existence, because after the effect proceeds from him, no attribute accrues to him, and just as he was not a bringer into existence before the procession, so too afterwards. It cannot be said that the bringing into existence of the bringing into existence is the same [as the first], because we say: such does not hold for existent things, for that is the bringing into existence of the effect, and this is the bringing into existence of the description which is bringing into existence. The answer: We choose that it is non-existent, and we do not accept the necessary consequence that the agent is not a bringer into existence. For the validity of affirmative predication does not contradict the fact that the description from which the predicate is taken is non-existent in external reality, as in our saying 'Zayd is blind in external reality', and 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible in external reality', even though both blindness and impossibility are non-existent in external reality.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of al-ḥāl (the state) based on the concept of 'bringing into existence' (al-ījād) and then provides a refutation. The argument runs: al-ījād is neither existent (otherwise it would require another ἵjād ad infinitum) nor non-existent (otherwise the agent would not be called 'bringer into existence'). Therefore, al-ījād must be a state—something between existence and non-existence. The initial response ('قلنا') points out that the non-existence of the basis of the predicate does not invalidate predication: we can say 'Zayd is blind' even though blindness is non-existent. Then the argument is restated more fully: if al-ījād were non-existent, the agent would not acquire any new attribute after causing the effect, so he would not be called 'bringer into existence'. The answer ('والجواب') directly chooses that al-ījād is non-existent and rejects the consequence. It explains that affirmative predication (e.g., 'Zayd is blind') is valid even though the attribute (blindness) is non-existent in external reality. Similarly, the impossibility of opposites is a true predication despite impossibility being non-existent. Thus, the agent can be called 'bringer-into-existence' even if al-ījād is non-existent, because predication does not require the predicate's basis to exist externally.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a true predicate requires the existence of its basis in external reality.
Correction: The passage shows that we can validly predicate blindness of Zayd, even though blindness is non-existent. Thus, predication does not require the attribute to exist externally.

Checkpoint

B) By showing that affirmative predication can hold even when the attribute is non-existent in external reality.
C) By denying that the agent is the cause.
D) By using an infinite regress argument.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that al-ijad can be considered non-existent without making the agent not a bringer-into-existence, because predication does not require external existence of the attribute.

Will you now examine each self-description for whether it is an excuse or a true predication that does not diminish your responsibility?
?
Reflect on a time you said 'I have no choice' to justify an action. According to this lesson, even if 'no choice' (necessity) is non-existent, the statement can still be true or false. Does this thought make you feel more accountable for your choices? How will you respond to that accountability starting today?
?
The passage uses the example 'Zayd is blind' to show that blindness is non-existent yet the predication is valid. Consider an attribute you often ascribe to yourself that you consider 'just the way I am'—like impatience or laziness. If that attribute is non-existent in reality, what does that mean for your ability to change it? Majority Proof on Generic Attributes of Non-Existents
Sanusi presents the majority's proof and its refutation regarding attributes of non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority's proof that non-existent essences possess generic attributes and the reply that equality in essentiality does not entail identity.

Core Idea
Equality in essentiality does not imply identity; realities can differ while sharing essentiality.

This passage is part of a larger discussion on whether non-existent entities are things. Here, the author presents the majority argument that non-existent essences have generic attributes (like substantiality) in the state of non-existence, and then provides a refutation. Understanding this exchange clarifies the relationship between essence, existence, and attributes in classical kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the three-part structure of the proof and the concise reply.
2
Identify the three premises of the proof
The majority's proof has three distinct points. Find each one and note how they build toward the conclusion that non-existents have generic attributes.
The three premises are: (1) equality in essentiality, (2) similarity/difference argument, (3) basis of distinction.
3
Examine the reply's counterexample
The reply uses the example of realities sharing in existence to show that equality in essentiality does not prevent difference in reality. Consider how this example refutes each premise.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Think about why the majority argument fails. The reply demonstrates that sharing a common property (essentiality) does not force identity, as long as there is another level of differentiation.
This lesson underscores a key principle in kalam: coextensive properties do not imply essential identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من تفاريع القول بكون المعدوم شيئاً : اختلافهم في أن الذوات المعدومة هل تتصف بصفة الجنس، كالجوهر بالجوهرية والسواد بالسوادية إلى غير ذلك، وبما يتبع صفة الجنس، كالحلول في المحل التابع للسوادية مثلاً . فقال الجمهور: نعم لأنها متساوية في الذاتية، فلو لم تتخالف بالصفات لكانت واحدة ولأنها إما (۳) متماثلة، فتكون متماثلة في الوجود، لأن ما بالذات لا يزول بالعرض وإما متخالفة فتكون بالصفات ضرورة اشتراكها في الذاتية، ولأن التميز اللازم للجوهر حالة الوجود، ليس لأنه ذات، ولا لأنه موجود، وإلا لكان لازماً للعرض، فتعين أن يكون لصفة يتصف بها في العدم. وأجيب بأن التساوي في الذاتية لا يمنع الاختلاف بالحقيقة، كالحقائق المتشاركة في الوجود، وحينئذ لا يرد شيء مما ذكر (4)
From the ramifications of the statement that the non-existent is a thing: their disagreement concerning whether non-existent essences are characterized by the attribute of genus (sifat al-jins), like the substance by substantiality and blackness by blackness and so on, and by what follows the attribute of genus, like inherence in a locus which follows blackness for example. So the majority said: Yes, because they are equal in essentiality (al-dhatiyya), so if they did not differ by attributes they would be one; and because they are either similar, in which case they would be similar in existence — for what is essential does not vanish by accident — or different, in which case they are so by attributes necessarily due to their commonality in essentiality; and because the distinction (al-tamayyuz) necessary for the substance in the state of existence is not because it is an essence, nor because it is existent, otherwise it would be necessary for the accident, so it must be due to an attribute it is characterized by in non-existence. And it was answered that equality in essentiality does not prevent difference in reality (al-haqiqa), like realities sharing in existence, and in that case none of what was mentioned is valid.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the majority (al-jumhur) that non-existent essences (al-dhat al-ma'duma) possess generic attributes (sifat al-jins) in the state of non-existence, and then provides the response to their argument. The passage begins by identifying this as one of the ramifications of the thesis that the non-existent is a thing (shay'). The majority's proof consists of three points: First, all essences are equal in essentiality (al-dhatiyya) in non-existence, so without differing by attributes they would be identical—a reductio ad absurdum. Second, either they are similar (mutamathila) or different (mukhalifa) in non-existence; if similar, they must be similar in existence because essential properties cannot change accidentally; if different, that difference must be due to attributes, since they share essentiality. Third, the distinction (al-tamayyuz) necessary for a substance in existence is not due to its essence nor its existence, otherwise it would be necessary for an accident (which it is not), so it must be due to an attribute that it possesses in non-existence. The answer (al-jawab) counters that equality in essentiality does not prevent difference in reality (al-haqiqa), giving the example of realities that share existence yet differ in their essential natures. Thus the majority's argument fails because it wrongly assumes that equality in essentiality entails identity.

Key Terms
الذوات المعدومة
صفة الجنس
الذاتية
التميز
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equality in essentiality implies identity of essences.
Correction: The reply shows that equality in essentiality does not prevent difference in reality (haqiqa); multiple realities can share existence yet differ essentially.

Checkpoint
Which premise is NOT part of the majority's proof that non-existents have generic attributes?
A) Essences in non-existence are equal in essentiality.
B) If essences were not distinguished by attributes, they would be one.
C) Distinction in existence is due to an attribute present in non-existence.
D) Attributes in non-existence are caused by an external agent.

Key Takeaway
The majority's proof that non-existents have generic attributes fails because equality in essentiality does not force identity; realities can differ in their true natures while sharing essentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you procrastinate on an action—like praying on time or fulfilling a promise—that action is a non-existent essence in your intention. According to this lesson, does it have any attributes in that state? What does the reality (haqiqa) of that action demand from you now, before you perform it? Examine one specific responsibility you are delaying and commit to acting on it today.
?
The majority assumed that equality in essentiality forces identity. Where in your relationships do you assume that because people share a common label (like 'family' or 'co-worker') they are interchangeable, ignoring their unique realities? How can you break that assumption starting with one relationship this week?
?
The refutation teaches that realities differ despite shared essentiality. How does that apply to your understanding of divine names? Two attributes of Allah, like Al-Rahman and Al-Rahim, share essentiality as mercy, but differ in reality. How does that distinction affect the way you call upon Allah in your personal supplications? Write down a specific request you will address with a particular name of Allah, conscious of its unique reality.

Ibn 'Ayyash's Objection to Attributes of Non-Existents and the Answer
Ibn 'Ayyash's objection to attributes of non-existents and the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn 'Ayyash's argument that non-existent entities cannot have attributes and the resolution based on the distinction between absolute and particular essence.

Core Idea
Attributes of non-existents are possible if grounded in the particular essence, not the absolute essence.

Abu Ishaq ibn 'Ayyash presented a powerful objection against the Ash'arite view that attributes can exist in non-existent entities. He argued that all non-existents are equal, so any specific attribute would lead to absurdities. The answer, however, reveals a key distinction that resolves the dilemma. Let's examine his reasoning and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage for the argument.
Pay attention to the flow of alternatives that Ibn 'Ayyash rejects.
2
Identify the four rejected alternatives
Ibn 'Ayyash systematically rejects four ways an attribute might be assigned to a non-existent essence. Find each alternative and his reason for rejection.
These are: essence itself, another attribute, a separate cause (mubayin), and a necessitating cause (mujib).
3
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer (al-jawab) introduces a crucial distinction between the absolute essence (al-dhat al-mutlaqa) and the particular essence (al-dhat al-mukhtassa). Notice how this solves the problem.
The attribute can be grounded in the particular essence without regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب (٥) أبو إسحق بن عياش : (٦) إلى أنها في العدم عارية عن جميع الصفات، لأنها لما كانت متساوية في الذات فاختصاص بعضها بصفة معينة لا تكون لذاته وهو ظاهر ولا لصفة أخرى وإلا تسلسل، بل لمباين ولا يجوز أن يكون موجباً لأن نسبته إلى الكل على السواء فيكون مختاراً. وفعل المختار حادث فيلزم كون المعدوم مورداً للصفات المتزايلة وهو باطل بالاتفاق (۱) ، فتعين أن يكون ذلك حالة الوجود . والجواب : أنه يجوز أن يكون لذاته المخصوصة، فظهر أن مبنى كلام الطرفين على عدم التفرقة بين العارض الذي هو الذات المطلقة، والمعروض الذات المخصوصة
And Abu Ishaq ibn 'Ayyash held that they in non-existence are devoid of all attributes, because since they are equal in essence, the specification of some of them with a particular attribute cannot be due to its essence — that is obvious — nor due to another attribute, otherwise there would be infinite regress (tasalsul), nor due to something separate (mubayin), and it cannot be necessitating (mujib) because its relation to all is equal, so it would be a free agent (mukhtar). And the act of a free agent is originated (hadith), so it would follow that the non-existent is a recipient of multiple attributes, which is invalid by consensus. Therefore that must be in the state of existence. The answer: it is possible that it is due to its particular essence (dhat mukhtassa). So it became clear that the foundation of the two parties' discourse is the failure to distinguish between the accidental (al-'arid) which is the absolute essence (al-dhat al-mutlaqa) and the substrate (al-ma'rud) which is the particular essence (al-dhat al-mukhtassa).
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of Abu Ishaq ibn 'Ayyash, who denies that non-existent entities have any attributes at all. He argues that since all essences are equal in non-existence, a specific attribute cannot be due to the essence itself (that would be circular), nor due to another attribute (infinite regress), nor due to an external cause (mubayin) that would necessitate it, because such a cause would have equal relation to all, so it would have to choose freely, and the act of a free agent is originated (hadith), implying that the non-existent entity becomes the recipient of multiple originated attributes, which is universally rejected. Therefore attributes can only exist in the state of existence. The answer (al-jawab) resolves the dilemma by suggesting that the attribute could be due to the particular essence (al-dhat al-mukhtassa) of that entity, not the absolute essence (al-dhat al-mutlaqa). Thus the author shows that both parties failed to distinguish between the absolute essence (al-'arid) and the particular substrate (al-ma'rud), which is the key to solving the dispute.

Key Terms
عارية عن الصفات
الذات المطلقة
الذات المخصوصة
المختار
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribute of a non-existent necessarily leads to infinite regress.
Correction: The attribute can be grounded in the particular essence (al-dhat al-mukhtassa), avoiding regress; the regress only arises if one tries to attribute it to another attribute or the absolute essence.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn 'Ayyash, what would happen if a non-existent had an attribute due to another attribute?
A) Infinite regress
B) The attribute would be eternal
C) The essence would be destroyed
D) The attribute would be impossible

Key Takeaway
The key to resolving the debate about attributes of non-existents is the distinction between the absolute essence (al-dhat al-mutlaqa) and the particular essence (al-dhat al-mukhtassa).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a quality you have—perhaps intelligence, patience, or wealth—that you tend to attribute to your 'self'. Is this quality grounded in your absolute existence (you think you deserve it) or in your particular essence (a specific gift from Allah)? How does acknowledging it as a particular gift change your sense of ownership and gratitude?
?
Ibn 'Ayyash argued that attributes in non-existence would require an 'originated act' from a free agent, which he found problematic. But the answer says attributes can be grounded in the particular essence. Reflect: Are there attributes you claim for yourself—perhaps in your thoughts or actions—that you assume are self-generated? Where does the 'originated act' truly come from? What would it mean to see even your best qualities as divine gifts rather than your own merits?
?
The author says both parties failed to distinguish between the accidental (the absolute essence) and the substrate (the particular essence). Consider a personal challenge: When you judge yourself or others, do you tend to confuse temporary attributes (accidents) with the core person (substrate)? How might this distinction help you avoid incorrect judgments and cultivate humility?

Distinction and Substantiality: Two Views
The author presents a dispute on whether distinction differs from substantiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among kalam scholars on whether distinction (al-tamayyuz) and substantiality (al-jawhariyya) are distinct or identical concepts.

Core Idea
Distinction and substantiality: two sides of the same coin or separate attributes?

In this lesson, we examine a key debate in kalam metaphysics: Is a substance's substantiality the same as its spatial distinctness? The passage contrasts the majority view that substantiality accompanies the substance even in non-existence, while distinction only attaches when it exists, with an alternative view that equates the two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
Read the passage and locate the majority position and the minority position. Note the key terms used for each.
2
Analyze the majority's argument
The majority distinguishes substantiality from distinction. Understand how they define each and their relationship to the substance's existence.
Note the terms al-kawn and al-ka'iniyya.
3
Analyze the minority's argument
Al-Shahham, al-Basri, and Ibn 'Ayyash argue that substantiality is itself spatiality. Consider why they reject the distinction.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this debate affects the understanding of substance and accidents in kalam. What is at stake?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أن التميز هل يغـايـر الذي هو الجوهرية؟ فالجمهور على أن الجوهرية صفة تابعة للجوهر حالتي الوجود والعدم، والتميز وهو اقتضاء الجوهر حيزاً ما صفة تابعة ثابتة للجوهر الموجود أي صادرة عن الجوهر بشرط الحدوث ويسمونه الكون وحصول الجوهر في الحيز المعين ويسمونه الكائنية معلل بالتحيز بمعنى الكون، وذهب الشحام والبصري (۲) وابن عياش (۳) إلى أن الجوهرية نفس التحيز، إذ لا معنى للجوهرية إلا المتحيز بالذات
And among them is their disagreement concerning whether distinction (al-tamayyuz) is different from that which is substantiality (al-jawhariyya). The majority hold that substantiality is an attribute adhering to the substance in both states of existence and non-existence, while distinction—which is the substance's requirement of some space (hayan)—is an attribute adhering to the existent substance, i.e., emanating from the substance under the condition of origination, and they call it al-kawn, and the substance's occurrence in a specific space they call al-ka'iniyya, which is caused by al-tahayyuz in the sense of al-kawn. And al-Shahham, al-Basri, and Ibn 'Ayyash held that substantiality is itself al-tahayyuz, for there is no meaning of substantiality except being essentially spatial (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat).
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the dispute over whether distinction (al-tamayyuz) is different from substantiality (al-jawhariyya). The majority opinion: substantiality is an attribute that accompanies the substance in both existence and non-existence, whereas distinction—which is the substance's requirement of a space (hayan)—is an attribute that only attaches to the existent substance. They call this attribute 'al-kawn' (the existence of the substance in a space) and the actual occurrence in a specific space 'al-ka'iniyya', which is caused by al-tahayyuz (spatiality) in the sense of al-kawn. In contrast, al-Shahham, al-Basri, and Ibn 'Ayyash hold that substantiality is identical to al-tahayyuz, because to be a substance means nothing other than to be essentially spatial (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat). Thus the disagreement is about whether substantiality is separable from spatiality or whether they are the same.

Key Terms
التميز
الجوهرية
التحيز
الكون
الكائنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distinction and substantiality are always distinct concepts.
Correction: Some scholars argue that substantiality is essentially spatiality, making distinction and substantiality identical.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, what is the status of substantiality (jawhariyya) in relation to existence and non-existence?
A) It exists only in non-existence.
B) It exists only in existence.
C) It exists in both existence and non-existence.
D) It exists only when distinction occurs.

Key Takeaway
The central debate is whether substantiality is an independent attribute or identical to spatiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between substantiality (present in both existence and non-existence) and distinction (only present in existence). In what sense does your own 'substance'—your soul—exist even when you are not acting? How does this reality reframe your accountability for every moment of existence?
?
Al-Shahham and others claimed that substantiality is nothing but being spatial. If your very essence is tied to your location, then where is your true 'place' that defines you? Does your heart reside in the dunya or in Allah's presence? What change does this demand of you today?

Does Non-Existence Have an Attribute?
Argument against non-existence as an attribute via causal dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether non-existence (al-ma'dumiyya) is an attribute of non-existent entities and the causal dilemma used to deny it.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be an attribute because it leads to paradoxes of perpetuity or origination.

This passage presents a nuanced debate in kalām about the status of non-existence. Al-Basrī affirmed that non-existence is an attribute of non-existent things, but others argued that such an attribute would entail problematic causal consequences. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a denial, and an argument.
The argument uses a dilemma: two possible causes, both leading to impossibility.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage mentions two parties: Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri (who affirmed) and others (who denied). Understand what each claims about non-existence as an attribute.
Al-Basri's position is stated, but his reasoning is not given. The focus is on the denial.
3
Unpack the causal dilemma
The deniers argue that if non-existence were an attribute, it must have a cause. If the cause is the essence itself or a necessitating agent, the attribute would be perpetual; if it is a free agent, it would be originated. Both conclusions are deemed impossible.
Perpetuity would mean the essence never comes to exist; origination would mean the attribute is created, which is absurd for non-existence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since both causal scenarios lead to impossibility, the original claim that non-existence is an attribute must be false. Understand why this reasoning is considered valid in kalām.
This argument highlights the importance of causality and logical consistency in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أن المعدومية هل هي ثابتة (٤) للمعدومات حالة العدم، فأثبته أبو عبد الله البصري (ه)، ونهاه غبره لأنها لافتقارها إلى الذات ممكن، فإن كان علتها الذات أو الفاعل الموجب من توسط الاختيار أصلاً لزم دوامها ، فلا توجد الذات وإن كان هي الفاعل بالاختيار ابتداء أو انتهاء لزم حدوثها وهو محال
And among them is their disagreement concerning whether non-existence (al-ma'dumiyya) is established for non-existent entities in the state of non-existence. Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri affirmed it, and others denied it because, due to its dependence on an essence, it is possible. If its cause is the essence or the necessitating agent without mediation of choice at all, then it would be perpetual, so the essence would not exist; and if it is the agent by choice, whether initially or finally, then it would be originated, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the dispute over whether non-existence (al-ma'dumiyya) is an attribute of non-existent entities in the state of non-existence. Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri affirmed it, but others denied it with an argument based on causality. Since the attribute of non-existence depends on an essence (dhat), it is a possible entity. If its cause is the essence itself or a necessitating agent (mujib) without any choice, then the attribute would be eternal (da'ima), so the essence would never come into existence (since it would always have the attribute of non-existence?). If its cause is a free agent (mukhtar) acting by choice, either initially or finally, then the attribute would be originated (hadith), which is impossible because non-existence is not something that can be originated. Therefore the attribute cannot be established. The argument assumes that any attribute must have a cause, and that a cause either necessitates without choice (leading to perpetuity) or acts by choice (leading to origination), both of which are problematic for the attribute of non-existence.

Key Terms
المعدومية
الذات
الفاعل الموجب
المختار
دوام
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that non-existence as an attribute is obviously true because things are non-existent before coming to be.
Correction: The argument shows that if non-existence were an attribute, it would require a cause leading to either perpetual non-existence or a created attribute, both of which are impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the argument against non-existence as an attribute, why can't the cause be the essence or a necessitating agent?
A) Because non-existence would then be eternal, so the essence would never exist.
B) Because the essence is not a real cause.
C) Because a necessitating agent does not exist.
D) Because the attribute would be originated.

Key Takeaway
The claim that non-existence is an attribute is rejected because it leads to a logical dilemma where both possible causal explanations result in impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you define yourself by what you lack (the non-existence of certain qualities) rather than by the positive existence of what Allah has given you? How does this lesson's logical argument expose the flaw in such self-definition? Commit today to reframing your identity based on the existence granted by the Agent by Choice.
?
The argument forces you to consider that every possible entity requires a cause. Have you ever pondered that your own existence is a possible entity, and its cause is not a blind necessity but a personal, willing Creator? How should that accountability shape your daily choices?
?
If non-existence cannot be an attribute, then nothingness itself has no describable quality. How does this deepen your awe for Allah's power to create existence from nothing, without any pre-existing attribute? What act of worship today can reflect that gratitude?

Non-Existent Substances and the Cause of States
Debate on non-existent bodies and division of state into caused/uncaused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether non-existent substances are bodies and the foundational division of the state into caused and uncaused.

Core Idea
States can be caused or uncaused; non-existence complicates the picture.

The author begins by referencing a debated issue: are non-existent substances bodies? The majority denied it, but some affirmed. Then he introduces a key division of the 'state' (al-ḥāl) that is central to the ontology of the kalām school: some states are caused by an existing attribute, others are not. This sets the stage for understanding how states relate to attributes and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the debate on non-existent bodies
Identify the two positions: majority negation vs. Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat's affirmation.
This debate is a backdrop for the coming classification.
2
Identify the two types of state
Find the contrast between 'worldliness' caused by knowledge and 'blackness' which is not caused by an attribute.
This distinction is fundamental to the doctrine of al-ḥāl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواهر المعدومة هل هي أجسام في العدم؟ فنفاه الجمهور، وأثبته أبو الحسين الخياط .

أدلتهم على ثبوت الحال تقسيمه

قال : ومثل تقسيم الحال إلى العلل بصفة موجودة، كالعالمية المعللة بالعلم وغير المعلل كلونية السواد، وتعطيل اختلاف الذوات بالأحوال إلى غير ذلك .

من تفاريع القول بالحال تقسيم إلى حال هو معلل بصفة موجودة في الذات كالعالمية المعللة بالعلم والقادرية المعللة بالقدرة، وإلى حال ليس كذلك كلونية السواد فإنها لا تعلل بصفة في السواد، وكذا وجود الأشياء، ومنها تعليل اختلاف الذوات في العدم بالأحوال فإن القائلين بكون الذوات المعدومة متخالفة بالصفات جعلوا تلك الصفات أحوالاً، ودل ذلك على تلك أن الحال عندهم لا يجب أن يكون صفة لموجود، ومنها تقسيمهم الصفات في الجواهر إلى ما يعود إلى الجملة أعني مجموع ما يتركب عنه البنية كالحيثية وما هو مشروط بها كالعلم والقدرة، وإلى ما يعود إلى التفصيل، أي الافراد كالجوهرية والوجود والكون، والكائنية. وفي الأعراض إلى الصفة النفسية كالسوادية، والصفة الحاصلة بالفاعل كالوجود وإلى ما يتبع العرض بشرط الوجود كالحلول في المحل.
Non-existent substances: are they bodies in non-existence? The majority negated it, and Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat affirmed it. Their proofs for the existence of the state: its division. He said: Among examples of the division of the state (al-hal) is into what is caused by an existing attribute, like 'worldliness' caused by knowledge, and what is not caused, like 'blackness' (lawaniyya al-sawad), and the nullification of the difference of essences by states, etc. Among the ramifications of the doctrine of the state is a division into a state that is caused by an existing attribute in the essence, like 'worldliness' caused by knowledge and 'powerfulness' caused by power, and a state that is not like that, like 'blackness' for it is not caused by an attribute in blackness, and likewise the existence of things. Among them is the explanation of the difference of essences in non-existence by states, for those who hold that non-existent essences differ by attributes made those attributes states, and that indicates that the state according to them is not necessarily an attribute of an existent. Among them is their division of attributes in substances into what pertains to the whole—that is, the sum of what composes the structure, like 'whereness' (al-haythiyya) and what is conditioned by it like knowledge and power—and into what pertains to the detail, i.e., the individuals, like substantiality (jawhariyya), existence, spatial location (al-kawn), and 'being located' (al-ka'iniyya). And in accidents, [they divide] into the essential attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya) like blackness (al-sawadiyya), and the attribute produced by the agent like existence, and into what follows the accident on condition of existence, like inherence in the substrate (al-hulul fi al-mahal).
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage in which the author discusses the concept of al-Ḥāl (the state) and its divisions, as well as the debated issue of non-existent substances. The passage begins by referencing the debate on whether non-existent substances are bodies in the state of non-existence: most theologians negate this, but Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat affirmed it. Then the author turns to the divisions of al-Ḥāl as presented by its proponents. The first division distinguishes between a state that is caused by an existing attribute in the essence (e.g., 'worldliness' caused by knowledge) and a state that is not so caused (e.g., 'blackness' in blackness itself). Second, the author mentions that among the ramifications of the doctrine of the state is explaining the difference of non-existent essences by states: those who hold that non-existent essences differ by attributes regard those attributes as states, implying that the state does not have to be an attribute of an existent. Third, the author classifies attributes in substances into those pertaining to the whole (like 'whereness' and what is conditioned by it, such as knowledge and power) and those pertaining to the detail (like substantiality, existence, spatial location, and 'being located'). Fourth, in accidents, attributes are divided into the essential attribute (like blackness), the attribute produced by the agent (like existence), and what follows the accident on condition of existence (like inherence in the substrate). The passage thus systematically presents how the proponents of al-Ḥāl categorize states and attributes, highlighting the subtlety of their ontology.

Key Terms
الحال
المعلل
غير المعلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Worldliness (ʿālimiyya)
B) Powerfulness (qādiriyya)
C) Blackness (lawniyyat al-sawād)
D) Existence (wujūd)

Key Takeaway
The state can be either caused by an existing attribute or uncaused, as seen in the example of blackness.

Non-Existent Essences and States as Differentiators
Ramification: states differentiate non-existent essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will grasp that according to some proponents of al-ḥāl, states can differentiate non-existent essences and do not require an existent subject.

Core Idea
A state need not belong to something that exists.

The previous lesson introduced the division of states into caused and uncaused. Now the author delves into a specific ramification: the use of states to explain how non-existent essences can differ. This leads to the conclusion that a state (ḥāl) is not necessarily an attribute of an existent entity.

Guided Path

This is a key point in the ontology of al-ḥāl.
2
Extract the implication
Those who hold that non-existent essences differ by attributes treat those attributes as states, showing that states can attach to non-existents.
This challenges the more common view that attributes require an existing subject.

Classification of Attributes in Substances and Accidents
Detailed classification of attributes in substances and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proponents of al-ḥāl classify attributes in substances and accidents.

Core Idea
Attributes divide into whole-dependent and individual, and in accidents into essential, agent-produced, and conditional.

The author concludes this discussion with a detailed classification of attributes as they apply to substances (jawāhir) and accidents (aʿrāḍ). This shows how the doctrine of al-ḥāl is applied across categories of existents.

Guided Path
1
Note the two-part division for substances
Attributes in substances are divided into those pertaining to the whole (like whereness) and those pertaining to the detail (like substantiality).
Whole-dependent attributes are conditioned by the structure.
2
Note the three-part division for accidents
Accident attributes are divided into essential (like blackness), produced by an agent (like existence), and conditional (like inherence).
These divisions reflect the subtlety of al-ḥāl theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواهر المعدومة هل هي أجسام في العدم؟ فنفاه الجمهور، وأثبته أبو الحسين الخياط .

أدلتهم على ثبوت الحال تقسيمه

قال : ومثل تقسيم الحال إلى العلل بصفة موجودة، كالعالمية المعللة بالعلم وغير المعلل كلونية السواد، وتعطيل اختلاف الذوات بالأحوال إلى غير ذلك .

من تفاريع القول بالحال تقسيم إلى حال هو معلل بصفة موجودة في الذات كالعالمية المعللة بالعلم والقادرية المعللة بالقدرة، وإلى حال ليس كذلك كلونية السواد فإنها لا تعلل بصفة في السواد، وكذا وجود الأشياء، ومنها تعليل اختلاف الذوات في العدم بالأحوال فإن القائلين بكون الذوات المعدومة متخالفة بالصفات جعلوا تلك الصفات أحوالاً، ودل ذلك على تلك أن الحال عندهم لا يجب أن يكون صفة لموجود، ومنها تقسيمهم الصفات في الجواهر إلى ما يعود إلى الجملة أعني مجموع ما يتركب عنه البنية كالحيثية وما هو مشروط بها كالعلم والقدرة، وإلى ما يعود إلى التفصيل، أي الافراد كالجوهرية والوجود والكون، والكائنية. وفي الأعراض إلى الصفة النفسية كالسوادية، والصفة الحاصلة بالفاعل كالوجود وإلى ما يتبع العرض بشرط الوجود كالحلول في المحل.
Non-existent substances: are they bodies in non-existence? The majority negated it, and Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat affirmed it. Their proofs for the existence of the state: its division. He said: Among examples of the division of the state (al-hal) is into what is caused by an existing attribute, like 'worldliness' caused by knowledge, and what is not caused, like 'blackness' (lawaniyya al-sawad), and the nullification of the difference of essences by states, etc. Among the ramifications of the doctrine of the state is a division into a state that is caused by an existing attribute in the essence, like 'worldliness' caused by knowledge and 'powerfulness' caused by power, and a state that is not like that, like 'blackness' for it is not caused by an attribute in blackness, and likewise the existence of things. Among them is the explanation of the difference of essences in non-existence by states, for those who hold that non-existent essences differ by attributes made those attributes states, and that indicates that the state according to them is not necessarily an attribute of an existent. Among them is their division of attributes in substances into what pertains to the whole—that is, the sum of what composes the structure, like 'whereness' (al-haythiyya) and what is conditioned by it like knowledge and power—and into what pertains to the detail, i.e., the individuals, like substantiality (jawhariyya), existence, spatial location (al-kawn), and 'being located' (al-ka'iniyya). And in accidents, [they divide] into the essential attribute (al-sifa al-nafsiyya) like blackness (al-sawadiyya), and the attribute produced by the agent like existence, and into what follows the accident on condition of existence, like inherence in the substrate (al-hulul fi al-mahal).
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage in which the author discusses the concept of al-Ḥāl (the state) and its divisions, as well as the debated issue of non-existent substances. The passage begins by referencing the debate on whether non-existent substances are bodies in the state of non-existence: most theologians negate this, but Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat affirmed it. Then the author turns to the divisions of al-Ḥāl as presented by its proponents. The first division distinguishes between a state that is caused by an existing attribute in the essence (e.g., 'worldliness' caused by knowledge) and a state that is not so caused (e.g., 'blackness' in blackness itself). Second, the author mentions that among the ramifications of the doctrine of the state is explaining the difference of non-existent essences by states: those who hold that non-existent essences differ by attributes regard those attributes as states, implying that the state does not have to be an attribute of an existent. Third, the author classifies attributes in substances into those pertaining to the whole (like 'whereness' and what is conditioned by it, such as knowledge and power) and those pertaining to the detail (like substantiality, existence, spatial location, and 'being located'). Fourth, in accidents, attributes are divided into the essential attribute (like blackness), the attribute produced by the agent (like existence), and what follows the accident on condition of existence (like inherence in the substrate). The passage thus systematically presents how the proponents of al-Ḥāl categorize states and attributes, highlighting the subtlety of their ontology.

?
