The Necessity of Simple Quiddity from Composite and Its Critique
Author critiques two proofs for simple quiddity from composite necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of composite quiddity does not straightforwardly prove simple quiddity, and how the author critiques two arguments for that claim.

Core Idea
Composite quiddity does not necessarily imply simple quiddity; the arguments are sophistical.

In this investigation, the author tackles a foundational question: does the existence of composite quiddity force us to accept simple quiddity? The claim seems plausible—every composite requires simple parts—but the author shows that the two common arguments for this are weak. By understanding his critique, you'll sharpen your ability to spot logical fallacies in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim and definitions
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the author's initial claim that rational necessity judges the existence of composite quiddity, so it must end in the simple. Then study the definitions of simple and composite quiddity with examples.
Pay attention to the examples: for simple, the Necessary, point, unit, existence; for composite, body, man, blackness.
2
Analyze the two arguments for simple quiddity
The author presents two arguments: the absolute argument (every number contains the one) and the intellectual composite argument (the intellect cannot grasp infinite parts). Understand why each is supposed to prove simple quiddity.
3
Understand the author's critique of each argument
The author declares both arguments weak. For the first, he identifies a sophistry: confusion of subject with accident. For the second, he notes that intellectual distinction does not entail conceptualization by parts. Study his reasoning carefully.
The first critique is a classic fallacy of composition/division.
4
Grasp the alternative reliance on necessity
After rejecting both arguments, the author recommends instead relying on necessity (ḍarūra) to prove the simple, like existence. Reflect on why this alternative is considered more sound.
This ties back to the beginning: the necessity of composite is known, but the inference to simple is not straightforward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث: الضرورة قاضية بوجود الماهية المركبة فلا بد من انتهائها إلى البسيطة. الماهية : إما بسيطة لا جزء لها أصلاً كالواجب والنقطة والوحدة والوجود وإما مركبة لها أجزاء كالجسم والإنسان والسواد ووجود المركبة معلوم بالضرورة، ويلزم منه وجود البسيطة، إما مطلقاً فلأن كل عدد ولو غير متناه فالواحد موجود فيه بالضرورة، وإما في المركب العقلي فلأنه لو لم ينته إلى البسيط امتنع تعقل الماهية لامتناع إحاطة العقل بما لا يتناهى، وكلاهما ضعيف. أما الأول : فلأنه مغالطة من باب اشتباه المعروض بالعارض، فإن وجود الواحد بمعنى جزء له أصلا إنما يلزم في العدد الذي هو العارض وأما في معروض العدد فلا يلزم إلا معروض الواحد الذي هو أحد أجزائه، فعلى تقدير عدم الانتهاء إلى البسيط تكون الماهية مركبة من مركبات غير متناهية مراراً غير متناهية، ويلزمه وجود المركب الواحد بالضرورة وهو لا يثبت المدعي. أما الثاني : فلأن معنى المركب العقلي أن لا يكون تمايز أجزائه إلا بحسب العقل، وهذا لا يستلزم كونه معقولاً بأجزائه، فالأولى التمسك في إثبات البسيط أيضاً بالضرورة كالوجود.
The third investigation: Rational necessity judges the existence of composite quiddity, so it must end in the simple. Quiddity: either simple, having no part at all, like the Necessary, the point, the unit, and existence; or composite, having parts, like body, man, and blackness. The existence of the composite is known necessarily, and from it follows the existence of the simple, either absolutely because every number, even infinite, has the one existing in it necessarily; or in the intellectual composite because if it did not end in the simple, comprehension of the quiddity would be impossible due to the impossibility of the intellect encompassing what is infinite. Both are weak. As for the first: it is a sophistry (mughalata) from the confusion of the subject with the accident, for the existence of the one in the sense of a part having no part only follows in the number which is the accident, but in the subject of the number it only follows the subject of the one which is one of its parts. So on the assumption of not ending in the simple, the quiddity would be composed of infinite composites indefinitely, and the existence of a single composite necessarily follows, which does not prove the claim. As for the second: the meaning of the intellectual composite is that the distinction of its parts is only mental, and this does not entail its being conceptualized by its parts. So it is better to rely on necessity in proving the simple as well, like existence.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage begins a new investigation (al-mabḥath al-thālith) on simple and composite quiddity. The author first states the claim: rational necessity judges that composite quiddity exists, and therefore it must terminate in the simple. He then defines simple quiddity as having no parts, giving examples like the Necessary (al-wājib), the point, the unit, and existence; and composite quiddity as having parts, like body, man, and blackness. The existence of composite quiddity is known by necessity, and from it the existence of the simple is allegedly entailed in two ways: (1) absolutely, because every number, even infinite, contains the one necessarily; (2) in the intellectual composite, because if it did not end in the simple, the intellect could not comprehend the quiddity due to its inability to grasp infinite parts. The author then declares both arguments weak (ḍaʿīf). He explains the weakness of the first: it is a sophistry (mughālaṭa) that confuses the subject (al-maʿrūḍ) with the accident (al-ʿāriḍ). The existence of the one as a part only applies to the number as an accident, not to the subject of number. On the assumption of no termination, the quiddity would be composed of an infinite series of composites, and the existence of a single composite would follow, but that does not prove the claim that a simple part exists. The weakness of the second argument: the intellectual composite means its parts are conceptually distinct, but this does not entail that the quiddity is actually conceptualized by its parts. Therefore, the author recommends instead relying on necessity (ḍarūra) to prove the simple, just as existence is known necessarily.

Key Terms
الماهية
بسيطة
مركبة
الضرورة
المغالطة
المركب العقلي
الواحد
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the necessity of composite quiddity directly proves simple quiddity.
Correction: The author shows this is a fallacy: the arguments for that inference are weak, and the correct approach is to rely on direct necessity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the first argument for simple quiddity weak?
A) Because it relies on a false definition of simple.
B) Because it confuses the subject with the accident.
C) Because the intellect cannot grasp infinite parts.
D) Because composite quiddity does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Composite quiddity does not necessarily imply simple quiddity; arguments for this are fallacious, and the sound approach is to rely on rational necessity.

Proof of Composition Through Participation and Difference
Proof of composition via participation and difference, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how participation and difference in essential attributes prove the composition of quiddities, and how a key objection is resolved.

Core Idea
Participation in essence plus difference in essence or concomitants proves composition.

This passage presents a foundational proof in metaphysics: if two things share an essential attribute but differ in another essential or a necessary concomitant, they must be composite. The author then addresses an important objection about the scope of the proof, clarifying when composition is and is not implied.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once without pausing on details. Then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the objection begins with 'فإن قيل' and the answer with 'قلنا'.
2
Identify the proof principle
Find the core statement: participation in an essential (dhati) with difference in another essential or a concomitant (lazim) proves composition. Understand why the shared essential cannot be the whole quiddity.
The proof relies on the impossibility of difference in concomitants if the quiddity were simple.
3
Examine the objection and answer
Read the objection carefully: it challenges whether 'essential' means part of the quiddity or non-accidental. The answer refocuses the conclusion to the distinguished quiddity (al-mumtaza).
The example of substance (jawhar) and body (jism) illustrates that only one quiddity may be composite.
4
Understand the exceptions
The author then lists cases that do NOT prove composition: mere participation in essential with difference in accidents, and difference in essential with participation in accidents. Note the examples of man and his individuals, and unity with point.
These exceptions prevent overgeneralization of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدل على التركيب الاشتراك في ذاتي مع الاختلاف في ذاتي أو شيء من لوازم الماهية لا مجرد الاشتراك أو الاختلاف في ذاتي يعني إذا اشتركت الماهيات في ذاتي مع الاختلاف في ذاتي، دل ذلك على تركب كل من الماهيتين مما به الاشتراك وما به الاختلاف، وكذا إذا اشتركا في ذاتي مع الاختلاف في عارض هو من لوازم الماهية لأن ذلك الذاتي المشترك لا يكون تمام ماهيتهما، وإلا امتنع الاختلاف في لوازمها، فيكون جزءاً وفيه المطلوب. فإن قيل : إن أريد بالذاتي جزء الماهية كان هذا لغواً من الكلام بمنزلة أن يقال : كل ما له جزء فهو مركب مع الاستغناء عن باقي المقدمات، وإن أريد ما ليس بعرضي جاز أن يكون الذاتي المشترك تمام إحدى الماهيتين، وجزء الأخرى الممتازة عنها بالذاتي الآخر، أو بلوازم الماهية، فلا يلزم تركب الماهيتين جميعاً، كالجوهر مع الجسم المتميز عنه بالذاتي، ولوازم الماهية الجسمية. قلنا : المراد لزوم تركب الماهية الممتازة بالذاتي أو بلوازم الماهية، فإن كانت كلتاهما كذلك كما في الإنسان والفرس، فكلتاهما، وإن كانت إحداهما كما ذكرتم فإحداهما، وأما مجرد الاشتراك في ذاتي مع الاختلاف في العوارض الثبوتية أو السلبية، أو مجرد الاختلاف بالذاتي مع الاشتراك في العوارض، فلا يستلزم التركيب لجواز أن يكون الذاتي المشترك تمام ماهيتهما، ويستند اختلاف العوارض إلى أسباب غير الماهية كما في الإنسان وأفراده، وأن يكون الذاتيات المختلفات تمام الماهيتين البسيطتين المشتركتين في العوارض كالوحدة، والنقطة في العرضية والإمكان ونحو ذلك.
And indication of composition is participation in an essential attribute with difference in an essential attribute or something from the concomitants of the quiddity, not mere participation or difference in an essential. That is, if quiddities participate in an essential with difference in an essential, that indicates composition of each of the two quiddities from that by which there is participation and that by which there is difference. Likewise, if they participate in an essential with difference in an accident that is from the concomitants of the quiddity, because that shared essential cannot be the entire quiddity of both; otherwise difference in their concomitants would be impossible, so it is a part, and therein is the desired. If it is said: If by essential is meant part of the quiddity, this is idle talk, akin to saying everything that has a part is composite, dispensing with the rest of the premises. And if by it is meant what is not accidental, it is possible that the shared essential is the entire one of the two quiddities and a part of the other distinguished from it by the other essential or by the concomitants of the quiddity, so composition of both quiddities is not necessary, like substance with body distinguished from it by the essential and the concomitants of corporeal quiddity. We say: The intention is the necessity of composition of the quiddity distinguished by the essential or by the concomitants of the quiddity. If both are such, as in man and horse, then both; if one is such as you mentioned, then one. As for mere participation in an essential with difference in positive or negative accidents, or mere difference in essential with participation in accidents, it does not entail composition, because it is possible that the shared essential is the entire quiddity of both, and the difference in accidents is attributed to causes other than the quiddity, as in man and his individuals, and that the different essentials are the entire two simple quiddities sharing in accidents, like unity and the point in accidentality and contingency, and so forth.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the composition of quiddities (mahiyyat) based on participation (ishtirak) and difference (ikhtilaf). The author begins by stating the principle: if two quiddities share an essential attribute (dhati) but differ in another essential attribute or in a concomitant (lazim) of the quiddity, then each quiddity is composed of a common part and a differentiating part. He explains: the shared essential cannot be the entire quiddity of both, otherwise they could not differ in their concomitants. Thus it must be a part, proving composition. 

An objection is raised: if by 'essential' is meant a part of the quiddity, the argument is trivial (saying everything with a part is composite). If by 'essential' is meant what is not accidental (i.e., constitutive), it is possible that the shared essential is the entire quiddity of one and only a part of the other, so only one quiddity is composite. The author answers: the intended conclusion is that the quiddity which is distinguished (al-mumtaza) by its essential or by concomitants is composite. If both are distinguished (as in man and horse: both share 'animal' but differ in 'rational' and 'neighing'), then both are composite. If only one is distinguished (as substance and body: substance is simple? Actually the example given: jawhar with jism, where jism is distinguished by the essential and accompaniments, so jism is composite, but jawhar may be simple? The author uses this to show that only the distinguished quiddity is necessarily composite). 

Then the author clarifies what does NOT imply composition: mere participation in an essential with difference in accidental attributes (like individuals of a species sharing the specific essence but differing in accidents) does not prove composition, because the shared essential can be the entire quiddity, and accident differences are due to external causes. Similarly, difference in essential with participation in accidents (like 'unity' and 'point' both participating in 'accidentality' and 'contingency') does not prove composition; each simple quiddity can be entirely its own essential and share in accidental attributes. This refines the proof and guards against overgeneralization.

Key Terms
اشتراك (ishtirak)
ذاتي (dhati)
ماهية (mahiyya)
تركيب (tarkib)
لوازم (lawazim)
عارض (arid)
ممتاز (mumtaz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mere participation in an essential attribute with any kind of difference (including accidents) proves composition of both quiddities.
Correction: The author clarifies that difference must be in another essential or a necessary concomitant, not in mere accidents. Participation in essential with difference in accidents does not prove composition, as in the case of man and his individuals.

Checkpoint
In the author's proof of composition via participation and difference, how does he respond to the objection that the shared essential could be the whole of one quiddity?
A) He concedes and rejects the proof.
B) He says the proof only requires that at least one quiddity is composite.
C) He says the distinguished quiddity is composite.
D) He says the objection is invalid because the shared essential must be a part.

Key Takeaway
Composition of quiddity is proven when two entities share an essential but differ in another essential or necessary concomitant; difference in mere accidents does not prove composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You share the essential of faith (iman) with many believers, yet you differ in actions and states. According to this lesson, does that difference prove that your faith is composite? How does this clarify whether your faith can be both shared and uniquely yours without contradiction?
?
The objection in the passage warns against ambiguity in the word 'essential.' When you discuss religious beliefs with others, how often do misunderstandings arise because you assume shared meanings? What specific term in your own understanding of tawhid might be ambiguous, and how can you clarify it to avoid error?

The Three Properties of a Part in Composition
Taftazani derives and enumerates three properties of a part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three properties of a part in composition and why only the first is essential while the others are relational.

Core Idea
A part has three properties, but only precedence is essential; the other two are relational.

Taftazani begins by stating a necessary truth: a part must precede its whole in mind and existence. From this foundational point, he deduces two further properties, creating a complete taxonomy of the part's characteristics. This passage is crucial for understanding how kalam analyzes composition and the immediate logical relationships between parts and wholes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice that the passage starts with a statement, explains it, then enumerates three properties.
Pay attention to the examples given for each property.
2
Identify the three properties
The passage explicitly lists three properties of a part. Find them: (1) precedence in mind and externally, (2) independence from an intermediary in assent, (3) independence from an intermediary in existence. Note which ones are labeled essential (حقيقية) and relational (إضافية).
3
Understand the examples
Taftazani provides examples for each property. For precedence, he uses man (animal and rational) and house (wall and roof). For independence in assent, he says the mind affirms the part without proof. For independence in existence, he notes that the cause of the whole is also the cause of the part.
These examples help ground the abstract concepts.
4
Analyze the comparison of the two relational properties
At the end, Taftazani compares the second and third properties, showing how they intersect. Note the distinction between making the proposition primary (بديهي) and making the predicate primary (أولي).
This is a subtle but important point for advanced understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ولا بد من تقدم الجزء ذهناً، وخارجاً، فيلزمه الاستغناء عن الوسط في التصديق، والواسطة في الثبوت، إلا أن الخاصة الأولى حقيقية، والأخريان إضافيتان. يعني بأن جزء الشيء يتقدمه وجوداً وعدماً في الذهن الخارج، أما الوجود فبالنسبة إلى كل جزء، وأما العدم فبالنسبة إلى شيء ما من الأجزاء بمعنى أن وجود الإنسان مثلاً في العقل، يفتقر إلى وجود الحيوان والناطق، وعدمه إلى عدم أحدهما، ووجود البيت في الخارج، يفتقر إلى وجود الجدار والسقف، وعدمه إلى عدم شيء منهما ويتفرع على الأول الاستغناء عن الواسطة في التصديق بمعنى أن جزم العقل بثبوت الذاتي للماهية لا يتوقف على ملاحظة وسط واكتساب بالبرهان بل يجب إثباته لها ويمتنع سلبه عنها بمجرد تصورها. وعلى الثاني: الاستغناء عن الوسط في الثبوت، بمعنى أن حصول الجزء للمركب كالجدار للبيت واللون للسواد لا يفتقر إلى سبب جديد فإن جاعل الجدار هو جاعل البيت وجاعل اللون هو جاعل السواد. فظهر أن للجزء خواصاً ثلاثاً: الأولى: التقدم في الذهن والخارج وهي خاصة حقيقية لا تصدق على شيء من العوارض. الثانية: الاستغناء عن الواسطة في التصديق، بمعنى وجوب الثبوت، وامتناع السلب بمجرد إخطار الجزء والماهية بالبال بل بمجرد تصور الماهية وهذه خاصة إضافية لا حقيقية، لصدقها على اللوازم البيئة بالمعنى الأعم، إن اشترط إخطارهما، والأخص إن اكتفى بتصور الماهية. الثالثة: الاستغناء عن الوسط في الثبوت، وهي أيضاً إضافية، لصدقها على الأعراض الأولوية، أعني اللاحقة للشيء لذاته من غير واسطة، سواء كان الجزم بثبوتها للموضوع، محتاجاً إلى وسط كتساوي الزوايا الثلاث للقائمتين بالنسبة إلى المثلث فإنه لازم له لذاته، ويفتقر بيانه إلى وسائط أو غير محتاج كالانقسام بالمتساويين للأربعة والبيض بسطح الجسم الأبيض فالاستغناء عن الوسط يجعل القضية أولية، والاستغناء عن الواسطة يجعل محمولها أولياً، وبينهما عموم من وجه لتصادقهما في انقسام الأربعة، وبياض السطح، وصدق الأولى بدون الثانية في بياض الجسم، وبالعكس في تساوي زوايا المثلث للقائمتين.
He said: 'It is necessary that the part precede in mind and externally, so it follows that it is independent of a middle term in assent and of an intermediary in existence, except that the first property is essential and the other two are relational.' Meaning that the part of a thing precedes it in existence and non-existence in the mind and externally. As for existence, it is relative to each part; as for non-existence, it is relative to some part. For example, the existence of man in the mind depends on the existence of animal and rational, and his non-existence depends on the non-existence of one of them; the existence of a house externally depends on the existence of the wall and roof, and its non-existence depends on the non-existence of one of them. From the first [property] branches independence from an intermediary in assent, meaning that the mind's certainty of the attribution of an essential to an essence does not depend on observing an intermediary or acquiring through demonstration, but rather it must be affirmed for it, and it is impossible to negate it from it merely by conceiving it. And from the second: independence from an intermediary in existence, meaning that the occurrence of the part for the composite, like the wall for the house and color for blackness, does not depend on a new cause, for the maker of the wall is the maker of the house and the maker of color is the maker of blackness. So it becomes clear that the part has three properties: First: precedence in mind and externally, and it is an essential property, not applicable to any accident. Second: independence from an intermediary in assent, meaning necessity of affirmation and impossibility of negation merely by bringing the part and the essence to mind, indeed merely by conceiving the essence; and this is a relational property, not essential, because it applies to obvious concomitants in the general sense if bringing both to mind is required, and in the specific sense if conceiving the essence suffices. Third: independence from an intermediary in existence, and it is also relational, because it applies to primary accidents, i.e., those that follow a thing by itself without intermediary, whether the certainty of their affirmation for the subject is in need of an intermediary, like the equality of the three angles to two right angles for the triangle, for it is a necessary concomitant by itself but its clarification requires intermediaries, or not in need, like the division into two equals for the number four and whiteness for the surface of a white body. So independence from an intermediary makes the proposition primary, and independence from an intermediary makes its predicate primary, and between them there is generality and particularity in respect because they coincide in the division of four and the whiteness of the surface, and the first applies without the second in the whiteness of the body, and vice versa in the equality of the triangle's angles to two right angles.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the properties of a part (جزء) in the context of composition. It begins by quoting a foundational statement: it is necessary for the part to precede the whole both in the mind (as a conceptual component) and in external existence. From this, two immediate consequences follow: (1) independence from a middle term in assent (تصديق), meaning that once the part and the essence are conceived, the mind affirms the part without needing a separate proof; (2) independence from an intermediary in existence (ثبوت), meaning that the part's existence in the composite does not require a distinct cause—the cause of the whole is also the cause of the part. The author then notes that the first property (precedence) is essential (حقيقية), while the other two are relational (إضافية). He explains the first consequence by example: the mind's judgment that animal is part of man does not require a demonstration; it is immediate. The second consequence is explained by the fact that the agent that makes the house also makes its wall—no separate agent is needed. The passage then formally enumerates three properties of the part: (1) precedence in mind and external existence—essential, not shared by accidents; (2) independence from an intermediary in assent—relational, meaning the part's necessity is known directly; (3) independence from an intermediary in existence—also relational, meaning the part's existence does not require a separate cause. The author clarifies that the second property applies to obvious concomitants (اللوازم البيئة) whether in a general sense (if both part and whole must be brought to mind) or a specific sense (if mere conception of the essence suffices). The third property applies to primary accidents that follow by themselves, such as the equality of angles in a triangle (which requires proof) or the divisibility of four (which is immediate). He then compares the two properties: independence from an intermediary in assent makes the proposition primary (بديهي), while independence from an intermediary in existence makes the predicate primary. They overlap in some cases (e.g., division of four, whiteness of surface) but differ in others (e.g., whiteness of body requires existence intermediary but not assent intermediary; triangle's angles require assent intermediary but not existence intermediary). The enumeration follows logically from the initial quotation and explanation, providing a complete taxonomy of the part's characteristics.

Key Terms
جزء
خاصة حقيقية
خاصة إضافية
الاستغناء عن الوسط في التصديق
الاستغناء عن الوسط في الثبوت
اللوازم البيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all three properties are essential to the part.
Correction: Only the first property (precedence) is essential; the other two are relational and can apply to some accidents as well.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an essential (حقيقية) property of a part?
A) Independence from an intermediary in assent
B) Independence from an intermediary in existence
C) Precedence in mind and externally
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
A part in composition has three properties: precedence (essential), independence in assent, and independence in existence (both relational).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect: Is your conviction in the oneness of Allah (tawḥīd) so immediate that it requires no proof—like the part's precedence in the mind? If not, what intermediaries have you allowed to cloud that certainty, and will you remove them today?
?
Consider: The part's existence does not need a separate cause—the cause of the whole is its cause. In your life, do you treat your daily actions, words, and thoughts as parts of a whole that is solely caused by Allah? Or do you behave as if they have independent causes? Repent from attributing any part of your existence to other than Him.
?
Are there truths about your own soul—like your need for Allah—that you have made conditional upon proofs or circumstances, when they should be as self-evident as a part's belonging to its whole? Commit to affirming that need with the same immediacy the mind affirms the part.

Universals Are Mental Distinctions
Arguing that jins, fasl, and naw' are mind-dependent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why genus, differentia, and species are considered mental distinctions with no separate existence in the external world.

Core Idea
Universals exist only in the mind; outside are only individuals.

This lesson presents the core argument that universal concepts like genus and species are not external realities but rather mental abstractions. The author uses blackness as an example to show that what appears as a composite of color and constrictor of sight is actually a single simple thing. Through this, we see why the mind's classification does not reflect separate external parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow.
Pay attention to the logical structure: premise, example, conclusion.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage claims that only individuals exist externally; genus, differentia, and species are forms distinguished in the intellect. Find the sentence that states this directly.
3
Reflect on the blackness example
Consider why blackness cannot be composed of color and constrictor of sight externally. How does this support the claim about mental existence?
The example illustrates that what seems composite in the mind is simple in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لحصولها في الخارج، لأنه لا تمايز بينهما في الخارج، وإلا امتنع حمل أحدهما على الآخر بالمواطأة، ومن البين أن ليس في السواد أمر محقق هو اللون، وآخر هو قابضية البصر يجتمعان فتحصل منهما السواد. بل التحقيق أن ليس في الخارج إلا الأشخاص وإنما الجنس والفصل والنوع صور متمايزة عند العقل، يحصلها من الشخص بحسب استعدادات تعرض للعقل واعتبارات يتعقلها من جزئيات أقل أو أكثر مختلفة في التباين والاشتراك فيدرك من زيد تارة صورة شخصية بحيث لا يشاركه فيها غيره، وأخرى صورة يشاركه فيها عمرو وبكر، وأخرى صورة يشاركه فيها الفرس وغيره، وعلى هذا القياس. فإن قيل : هذا إنما هو في النوع البسيط كالسواد، لظهور أن ليس في الخارج لونية ، وشيء آخر به امتاز السواد عن سائر الألوان، ولهذا لا يصح يقال : جعل لوناً فجعل سواداً. بل جعلاهما واحداً، وأما في غيره، فالذاتيات المتمايزة في العقل، متمايزة في الخارج، وليس جعلاهما واحداً كالحيوان . فإنه يشارك النبات في كونه جسماً، ويمتاز عنه بالنفس الحيوانية، وجعل الجسم غير جعل النفس، حتى إذا زالت عنه النفس بقي ذلك الجسم بعينه موجوداً كالفرس الذي يموت وجسميته باقية ولهذا يصح أن يقال: جعل جسماً فجعل حيواناً . قلنا : الجسم المأخوذ على وجه كونه مادة غير المأخوذ على وجه كونه جنساً بالذاتي . وقد سبق تحقيق ذلك. والحاصل أن الذاتيات المتمايزة بحسب العقل فقط، قد يكون لها مبادىء متمايزة بحسب الخارج كالحيوان من الجسم والنفس الحيوانية والإنسان من البدن والنفس الناطقة، وقد لا يكون كالسواد من اللون، وقابضية البصر؛ وكالسطح من الكم، وقابليته القسمة في الطول والعرض جميعاً، وهو المسمى بالنوع البسيط، ومن ههنا جوز بعض المحققين كون الفصل عدمياً فإن المعنى الجنسي من الكم المتصل، يتحصل بماله من طول وعرض فقط، فيكون سطحاً، وبما له طول فقط فيكون خطاً .
For their occurrence in the external [world], because there is no distinction between them in the external [world], otherwise the predication of one upon the other by agreement would be impossible. And it is evident that in blackness there is no one definite thing that is color and another that is constrictor of sight that come together so that blackness results from them. Rather, the truth is that there is nothing in the external [world] except individuals, and genus, differentia, and species are forms distinguished in the intellect, which it obtains from the individual according to dispositions that occur to the intellect and considerations that it conceives from more or fewer particulars differing in disparity and commonality. Thus, it perceives from Zayd sometimes a personal image such that no one else shares it with him, another time an image in which Amr and Bakr share with him, and another time an image in which the horse and other share with him, and so on. If it is said: This applies only to the simple type like blackness, because it is apparent that in the external [world] there is no 'color-ness' and another thing by which blackness is distinguished from other colors; therefore it is not correct to say: He made it a color then made it blackness; rather they made them one. But as for other [types], the essential [parts] that are distinguished in the intellect are distinguished in the external [world], and they are not made one; like animal: it shares with plant in being a body and is distinguished from it by the animal soul, and the making of the body is other than the making of the soul, such that when the soul departs from it, that same body remains existing, like the horse that dies while its bodyness remains; hence it is correct to say: He made it a body then made it an animal. We say: The body taken as matter is other than the body taken as genus by the essential [part]. And there is no dispute about the distinction of the first from everything by external existence; rather, the dispute is about the second, because it is the predicated part called the essential. And the verification of that has preceded. The conclusion is that essential [parts] distinguished only according to the intellect may have principles distinguished according to the external world, like animal: body and animal soul, and human: body and rational soul, and may not have, like blackness: color and constrictor of sight, and like surface: quantity and its divisibility in length and breadth together; this is called the simple type. From this, some verifiers allowed the differentia to be privative, for the generic meaning of continuous quantity is specified only by having length and breadth, so it becomes a surface, and by having length only, so it becomes a line.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the position that universal concepts (jins, fasl, naw') are mental distinctions with no separate external existence. The author begins by arguing that if genus and species had distinct external existence, then predicating one of the other would be impossible (since they would be separate). Using blackness as an example, he asserts that blackness is not composed of color and 'constrictor of sight' as two separate external entities; rather, it is one simple reality. The truth is that only individuals exist externally; the mind abstracts from them general forms based on similarities and differences. An objection is raised: the above applies only to simple types like blackness, but in composite types like animal, the essential parts (body and soul) are distinct both in the mind and externally. The author answers by distinguishing 'body taken as matter' from 'body taken as genus' — the first is a physical substrate, the second is a logical component. The external distinction only applies to the material substrate, not to the genus as a predicated part. The passage concludes by summarizing that essential parts may have distinct external principles (e.g., animal: body and soul) or not (e.g., blackness: color and constrictor of sight, which are not distinct externally). The latter is the simple type, and it is here that some scholars allow the differentia to be a privation (e.g., surface is quantity limited by length and breadth).

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
نوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since genus and species are distinct concepts in the mind, they must correspond to distinct parts in the external world.
Correction: The author argues that this is false; external reality contains only individuals. The distinctions we make (genus, differentia, species) are mental abstractions based on similarities and differences among individuals.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the truth about external existence?
A) Genus and species exist separately.
B) Only individuals exist externally.
C) Blackness is composed of color and constrictor of sight.
D) The mind and external reality are identical.

Key Takeaway
Genus, differentia, and species are mental distinctions; external reality contains only individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the labels you assign to people (like 'good Muslim', 'sinner', 'friend') as fixed external facts about them, or are you aware that these are mental categories you've constructed? How might this lesson change the way you look at someone you've categorized too quickly?
?
If only individuals exist externally, then your judgments about groups ('those people', 'our community') are mental abstractions, not ultimate realities. What would it mean for you to stop using group labels as excuses for prejudice or indifference?
?
When you think about your own identity, do you cling to a mental 'genre' (such as 'I am a successful person' or 'I am a failure') as if it were an unchanging external reality? What would change if you saw yourself as a unique individual that no category can fully capture?

Simple vs Composite Types and the Nature of Differentia
Objection and answer about simple vs composite types, concluding with allowance of privative differentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between simple and composite types, and how the differentia can be privative in simple types.

Core Idea
Simple types lack external parts for their differentia; composite types have material parts but logical unity.

Having established that universals are mental, the author now addresses an objection: what about composite types like animal? The answer clarifies that external distinction pertains to material substrates, not logical components. This leads to the concept of the simple type, where the differentia can be privative. This lesson unpacks the objection, answer, and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the full passage from 'فإن قيل' to the end. Notice how the objection distinguishes simple and composite types.
The objection uses blackness as a simple type and animal as a composite.
2
Understand the distinction between 'body as matter' and 'body as genus'
The author responds by differentiating the material substrate of body and the logical genus 'body'. The external distinction applies to the material substrate, not the logical component.
This is key to resolving the objection.
3
Analyze the conclusion about essential parts
The conclusion summarizes that essential parts may have distinct external principles (like animal: body and soul) or may not (like blackness: color and constrictor of sight). The latter is the simple type.
4
Grasp how this allows differentia to be privative
From the simple type example (surface from continuous quantity), some scholars allowed the differentia to be a privation (e.g., surface is quantity limited by length and breadth).
This is a notable consequence.

?

?
The objection in the passage assumes that composite types have externally distinct parts. How does that simplify your priorities?

Considerational Unity: The Case of an Army
Explains that some unities are purely conceptual, like an army.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what considerational unity is and why it does not entail real interdependence of parts.

Core Idea
Some unities exist only in the mind, like an army of individual soldiers.

We have seen that some wholes have parts that truly need each other. But not all unities are like that. Sometimes the mind collects several things together and calls them one, even though they are independent. This passage explains that kind of unity with an army as an example.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read carefully to see how the author describes considerational unity. Note the key phrase 'اعتبارياً كالعسكر'.
The translation may help clarify the concept.
2
Understand the key term 'i'tibari'
This term means conceptual or mental consideration. It is the opposite of real unity. The intellect considers several things as one even when they are not joined in reality.
3
Examine the examples: army and ten
Notice that in both cases, the parts (soldiers, units) do not depend on each other. An army is just a collection of individual soldiers; the number ten is just a collection of units.
This contrasts with real parts like the body's organs.
4
Identify the main lesson
Considerational unity does not imply any necessary connection between parts. The earlier rule about parts needing each other does not apply here.
This prevents misunderstanding about all wholes being the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد يكون اعتبارياً كالعسكر فلا يلزم. بأن يكون هناك عدة أمور يعتبرها العقل أمراً واحداً، وإن لم يكن أمراً واحداً في الحقيقة. وربما يضع بإزائه اسماً، كالعشرة من الأحاد، والعسكر من الأفراد، ولا يلزم فيه احتياج بعض الأجزاء إلى البعض .
He said: And it may be considerational, like an army, so [the need] does not apply. That is, there may be several matters that the intellect considers as one thing, even if it is not one thing in reality. And perhaps a name is placed for it, like ten from units, and an army from individuals, and in it there is no requirement of need of some parts for others.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage introduces another type of whole: a considerational (i'tibari) unity. Unlike the essential parts of a definition or the real components of a body, some unities exist only in the mind's consideration. The example given is an army: many individual soldiers are considered as one army, even though they do not form a real single entity. Another example is the number ten, which is a collection of units. In such unities, the parts do not depend on each other for their existence; the unity is purely conceptual. Therefore, the earlier discussion about parts needing each other (like in the case of hyle and form) does not apply to these.

Key Terms
اعتباري
عسكر
عشرة
وحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All wholes have real parts that need each other.
Correction: Some wholes, like an army or the number ten, are purely mental constructs with no real interdependence.

Checkpoint
Why does an army not require a real need between its parts?
A) Because it is a simple substance.
B) Because its unity is purely considerational, not real.
C) Because an army has no parts.
D) Because the parts are indivisible.

Key Takeaway
Considerational unity is a mental construct where parts are independent, unlike real wholes where parts depend on each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something you treat as a single unit in your life—like your family, your work team, or your community. Is it a real unity where parts depend on each other, or a considerational one where they are independent? How should this understanding change how you interact with its individual members?
?
The passage says parts in a considerational whole do not need each other. In what areas of your life have you mistakenly assumed that certain parts (e.g., your spiritual practices and worldly duties) are dependent when they are actually independent? What is one practical change you can make today to act on that truth?
?
If an 'army' is just a mental construct, then what about other groups you identify with—your nation, your ethnicity, your school? Are you giving them too much reality in your heart? How does this lesson call you to realign your attachment and priorities?

Individuation: The Philosophers' Doctrine and Initial Response
The author presents his own position and the philosophers' view on the basis of individuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' theory of individuation (al-taʿayyun) and the author's critical engagement with it, including the key concepts of essence, accident, and locus.

Core Idea
Individuation requires more than existence; it depends on essence or a locus, not on accident or separation.

In this dense passage, the author confronts the philosophers' explanation of what makes an individual distinct. He first counters a potential objection, then lays out the philosophers' systematic view that determination arises either from essence itself or from a locus, never as a separate entity or mere accident. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the complex argumentative flow.
Pay attention to technical terms like al-taʿayyun and al-tashakhkhuṣ.
2
Identify the author's initial position
The author begins by arguing that disregarding something does not negate it. Individuation, like existence, must have a cause.
This sets up the need for a principle of individuation.
3
Understand the philosophers' three possibilities
The philosophers consider three bases for determination: essence itself, something separate, or something in the individual (accident or locus). They reject separate and accident.
Trace why each rejection is logically necessary.
4
Grasp the concept of locus (maḥall)
The locus is the substrate: matter for bodies, relation for souls, quiddity for immaterial intellects. This is the philosophers' positive view.
Note the distinction between accident (ḥāl) and locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: قطع النظر عن الشيء لا يوجب انتفاؤه، فعند الوجود لا بد من ماهية وأسباب فاعلية أو مادية. وبالجملة أمر يستند إليه الوجود، فيجوز أن يستند التشخص أيضاً إليه، ولو سلم فالوجود لا يقتضي إلا تعيناً ما، والكلام في التعينات المخصوصة، فلا يثبت المطلوب ما لم يتبين أن وجود كل فرد يقتضي تعينه الخاص. وذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أن التعين قد يستند إلى الماهية بنفسها أو بلوازمها كمها في الواجب. فينحصر في شخص، وإلا لزم تخلف المعلول عن علته لتحقق الماهية في كل فرد مع عدم تشخص الآخر، وقد يستند إلى غيرها، ولا يجوز أن يكون أمراً منفصلاً عن الشخص، لأن نسبته إلى الأفراد والتعينات على السواء، ولا حالاً فيه - لأن الحال في الشخص لافتقاره إليه يكسبون متأخراً عنه، ولكونه علة لتشخصه المتقدم عليه ضرورة أنه لا هذا الشخص إلا بهذا التشخص فيكون متقدماً عليه، وهو محال، فتعين أن يكون محلاً له، وما ذكرنا من نسبة الحال والمحل إلى الشخص دون المباهية، أو التشخص أقرب وأوفق بكلامهم والمراد بمحل الشخص معروضه في الأعراض، ومادته في الأجسام، ومتعلقه في النفوس على ما ذكروا من حدوث النفس بعد المدن وتعينها به فالعقول المجردة تستند تعيناتها، إلى مباهياتها فينحصر كل في شخص لا إلى مجرد الإضافة كعقل الفلك الأول مثلاً على ما قيل لأن هذه الإضافة متأخرة عن وجود الفلك المتأخر عن وجود العقل وتعينه، والاستناد إلى المادة أعم من أن يكون بنفسها، أو بواسطة ما حالاً فيها من الأعراض. فلا يرد ما قيل: أن غير المنفصل لا ينحصر فيما يكون في التشخص أو محلاً له، لجواز أن يكون حالاً في محله.
We said: disregarding something does not necessitate its non-existence. For upon existence, there must be an essence (māhiyya) and efficient or material causes. In sum, something to which existence is attributed, so it is possible that individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) is also attributed to it. And even if it is granted, existence only necessitates some determination (taʿayyun), while the discussion is about specific determinations. So the desired point is not established unless it is shown that the existence of each individual necessitates its specific determination. And the philosophers held that determination may be attributed to the essence itself or to its concomitants, like its quantity in the Necessary Being; thus it is restricted to one individual, otherwise the effect would lag behind its cause because the essence is realized in each individual without the individuation of the other. And it may be attributed to something else, and it is not permissible that it be something separate from the individual, because its relation to all individuals and determinations is equal; nor can it be an accident (ḥāl) in it, because the accident in the individual, by its need for it, would be posterior to it, and because it would be a cause of its individuation which is prior to it—necessarily there is no this individual except by this individuation, so it would be prior to it, which is impossible. So it is determined that it be a locus (maḥall) for it. And what we mentioned of the relation of accident and locus to the individual rather than to the quiddity or individuation is closer and more in line with their words. And by 'locus of the individual' is meant its substrate in accidents, its matter in bodies, and its relation in souls, according to what they mentioned of the soul’s origination after the body and its determination by it. So the immaterial intellects have their determinations attributed to their quiddities, so each is restricted to one individual, not to mere relation like the intellect of the first sphere, for example, as it is said, because this relation is posterior to the existence of the sphere which is posterior to the existence of the intellect and its determination. And attribution to matter is more general, whether by itself or by means of what is an accident in it. So what is said—that what is not separate is not restricted to what is in individuation or a locus for it—is not a problem, because it may be an accident in its locus.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage begins with the author’s own position on individuation and then presents the philosophers’ doctrine. The author first argues that simply disregarding something does not negate it; existence requires an essence and causes, so it is plausible that individuation also has a cause. Even if existence only gives a general determination, the discussion concerns specific determinations, so the conclusion is not proven without showing that each individual’s existence necessitates its specific determination. Then the author explains the philosophers’ view: determination (al-taʿayyun) may arise from the essence itself or its concomitants (as in the Necessary Being), restricting it to one individual, otherwise the effect would lag. It may also come from something else, but it cannot be separate from the individual (since its relation to all individuals is equal) nor an accident in it (since the accident would be posterior, yet the individuation is prior). Thus it must be a locus (maḥall) for the individual. The author clarifies that the terms 'accident' and 'locus' are used in relation to the individual, not the quiddity. The locus is the substrate for accidents in bodies, matter in bodies, and relation in souls. For immaterial intellects, determination comes from their quiddities, so each is unique. Attribution to matter is broader, either directly or through accidents. The passage concludes by refuting an objection that what is not separate cannot be restricted to being in individuation or a locus, because it can be an accident in its locus.

التشخص (al-tashakhkhuṣ)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)
الحال (al-ḥāl)
المحل (al-maḥall)
الوجود (al-wujūd)
المادة (al-mādda)
العقول المجردة (al-ʿuqūl al-mujarrada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Existence alone suffices to individuate each thing.
Correction: The author shows that existence only yields a general determination; specific determinations require additional principles, as the philosophers argue.

Checkpoint

B) Their quiddities or essences
C) Accidental relations
D) The First Cause

Key Takeaway
Individuation is not a mere consequence of existence; it must be grounded either in essence itself or in a locus, never in a separate or accidental principle.

Circularity Objection and Its Subtle Resolution
Objection, response, and further objection on individuation circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of circularity between matter and accidents is answered by distinguishing two levels of determination.

Core Idea
The determination of matter and accidents are not the same, so circularity is avoided.

This passage tackles a sophisticated objection: if accidents are individuated by matter, and matter is individuated by accidents, we seem to have a vicious circle. The response carefully distinguishes between two kinds of determination, showing that they are not identical. We'll walk through the objection, the answer, and a lingering challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the objection that if accidents are determined by matter, then matter cannot be determined by accidents without circularity. Understand the logical structure of the objection.
The objector grants the earlier premises but sees a circular dependency.
2
Study the response
The answer distinguishes: matter's determination is by the accidents themselves, but the accidents have a different determination. Notice how the response avoids the circle by separating levels.
The upshot: determination is not one-way but two-leveled.
3
Examine the further objection
Finally, note the unresolved objection: if this reasoning works, why can't essence become multiple through its own attributes without matter? Reflect on why this is left open.
This points to a deeper issue about individuation.
4
Connect to learning objective
Review how the response resolves the circularity and what it implies for the theory of individuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على ما ذكروا بعد تسليم مقدماته بأن تعين الأعراض الحالة التي في المادة إنما هو بتعين المادة على ما سيجيء، فلو تعينت المادة بها كان دوراً. وأجيب بأن تعين المادة إنما هو بنفس الأعراض الحالة في المادة المعينة بتعين ما لا بتعيناتها الحاصلة بتعين المادة، وحاصله أن تعينها بتعينها وتعينها مع تعيناتها، فلا يلزم الدور، ولا حصول التشخص من انضمام الكلي إلى الكلي. إلا أنه يرد عليه أنه إذا جاز ذلك، فلم لا يجوز تكثر الماهية وتعين أفرادها بما لها من الصفات المتكثرة العارضة لها من غير لزوم مادة.
And objection was raised against what they mentioned, after conceding its premises: that the determination of the accidents subsisting in matter is only by the determination of matter, as will come, so if matter were determined by them, there would be circularity. And it was answered: that the determination of matter is only by the accidents themselves subsisting in matter which is determined by a determination not by their determinations that arise from the determination of matter. The upshot is that its determination is by its determination and its determination is together with its determinations, so no circularity follows, nor does individuation result from the conjunction of universal to universal. However, it is objected against this: if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible for the essence to become multiple and its individuals to be determined by its multiple accidental attributes without requiring matter?
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection and its response. The objector grants the previous premises but argues: the accidents inhering in matter are determined by matter’s determination (as will be shown later), so if matter were determined by those accidents, there would be circularity. The answer distinguishes two levels: matter’s determination is by the very accidents subsisting in it, but those accidents have a determination that is not the same as the one they acquire from matter. In other words, matter is determined by a prior determination (from the accidents), and the accidents themselves have their own determinations, so there is no circularity—the two determinations are different. Consequently, individuation is not simply the conjunction of two universals. However, an implicit objection remains: if this reasoning is accepted, why can't essence become multiple through its own accidental attributes without needing matter at all? This last point is left as an unresolved challenge.

الأعراض الحالة
المادة
الدور
الكلي
الصفات العارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any mutual determination between matter and accidents necessarily leads to circularity.
Correction: The response shows that the determinations are at different levels, so no vicious circle occurs.

Checkpoint

B) By distinguishing the determination of matter from the determinations of accidents that arise from matter
C) By claiming matter determines accidents and accidents determine matter in the same sense
D) By accepting circularity as harmless

Key Takeaway
The circularity objection is answered by distinguishing two levels of determination, but a further objection suggests that essence might individuate without matter, which remains unresolved.

The Seven Investigations of the Second Method
An enumerated list of the seven investigations of the second method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven key investigations that form the second method of this work, which covers the modal categories of necessity, impossibility, and contingency.

Core Idea
The seven investigations are the structural backbone for proving the need for a Creator.

This passage presents an outline of the second method (al-manhaj al-thani), which focuses on the fundamental modal concepts of necessity, impossibility, and contingency. These categories are essential for kalam arguments about the nature of existence and the need for a cause. The outline lists seven investigations that will be explored in detail, from the formation of concepts to the principle that the contingent is equally open to existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic list to get a sense of the technical terms.
Notice the enumeration from الأول to السابع.
2
Identify the seven investigations
Work through each of the seven mabahith and note their focus.
Each investigation builds on the previous ones.
3
Consider the purpose
Notice how this list sets up the classic kalam argument for a necessary being.
The seventh investigation is the key principle of contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنهج الثاني في الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان وفيه مباحث . الأول المعقولات تحصل من نسبة المفهوم الثاني : تقسيم الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان الثالث: الوجود رابطة وتوابعه مواد القضايا الرابع : في اعتبارية الوجوب وما يجري مجراه الخامس: احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر السادس في موجب احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر السابع : تساوي طرفي الممكن بالنسبة إلى ذاته .
The Second Method: On Necessity, Impossibility, and Contingency. It contains investigations: First, the intelligibles are obtained from the relation of the concept. Second: Division of necessity, impossibility, and contingency. Third: Existence as copula and its corollaries: the materials of propositions. Fourth: On the contingency of necessity and what follows its path. Fifth: The need of the contingent for an efficient cause. Sixth: On the cause of the need of the contingent for an efficient cause. Seventh: The equivalence of the two sides of the contingent with respect to itself.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is an outline of the second method (al-manhaj al-thani) of the work, which covers the modal categories: necessity (wujub), impossibility (imtina'), and contingency (imkan). It lists seven investigations (mabahith). The first investigation concerns how intelligibles (al-ma'qulat) are obtained from the relation of the concept, likely discussing the formation of concepts. The second is the division of the three modal judgments themselves. The third discusses existence as a copula (rabita) in propositions and its corollaries, which are the materials of propositions (mawadd al-qadaya). The fourth investigates the contingency (i'tibariyya) of necessity and related concepts. The fifth establishes the need of the contingent (al-mumkin) for an efficient cause (mu'aththir). The sixth examines the reason why the contingent needs a cause. The seventh states that the two sides (existence and non-existence) of the contingent are equal with respect to itself, which is the principle of contingency that leads to the need for a determinant. This sets the stage for the classic kalam argument for a necessary being.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الامتناع
الإمكان
الممكن
المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the second method is solely about modal logic, but it also includes the proof of the need for a cause and the principle of determination.
Correction: The second method is not just a logical classification; it provides the foundation for the kalam cosmological argument by establishing that contingent things need a cause and that their existence is not self-explanatory.

Key Takeaway

Introduction to the Second Method on Modal Judgments
Author introduces the second method and lists seven investigations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second method's focus on the three modal judgments—necessity, impossibility, and possibility—and the seven investigations that constitute the method's content.

Core Idea
Modal judgments are properties of existence and essence, and the second method unpacks them through seven investigations.

This lesson opens the second method of the work, which centers on the three fundamental modal judgments: necessity, impossibility, and possibility. The author clarifies that impossibility is considered a consequent of both existence and essence, and then outlines seven specific investigations that will be carried out. The accompanying commentary enriches the discussion with lexical and theological notes on the term 'man'' (prevention) and the relationship of modal judgments to existence and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the title
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the title announces the second method concerning necessity, impossibility, and possibility.
The title is 'المنهج الثاني في الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان'.
2
Understand the three modal judgments
The passage builds on the three rational judgments: wajib (necessary), mumtani' (impossible), and mumkin (possible). These are central to Islamic theology and logic.
These judgments are also called modal judgments in logic.
3
Grasp the explanation about impossibility as a consequent
The author explains why impossibility is treated as a consequent of existence and essence. Several reasons are given, showing the depth of the analysis.
This is a nuanced point; note the different perspectives.
4
Review the seven investigations
The passage lists seven investigations that will be covered. Read them carefully to get a preview of the upcoming topics.
The investigations range from the necessity of judgments to the nature of possibility.
5
Reflect on the significance of the second method
Consider why this method is important. It lays the groundwork for discussing God's attributes, creation, and other theological issues.

المنهج الثاني في الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان
(قال : المنهج الثاني في الوجوب والامتناع (۱) والإمكان (۲)) .
وفيه مباحث (۳) جعل الامتناع من لواحق الوجود والماهية. نظراً إلى أن ضرورة سلب الموجود عن الماهية حال لهما أو إلى أنه من أوصاف الماهية المعقولة، أو لكونه في مقابلة الإمكان أو لأن المراد بلواحقهما ما جرت العادة بالبحث عنه بعد البحث عنهما .
(۱) المنع: خلاف الإعطاء يقال : منع يمنع منعاً فهو مانع ومناع. قال تعالى : (مناع للخير معتد أثيم) سورة القلم آية ١٢ . وقال تعالى : (وإذا مسه الخير منوعا) سورة المعارج آية رقم ٢
والمانع من صفات الله تعالى له معنيان أحدهما ما روي في الدعاء الثابت عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم اللهم لا مانع لما أعطيت ولا معطي لما منعت ولا ينفع ذا الجد منك الجد». الثاني : أنه يمنع أهل دينه أي يحوطهم وينصرهم . ومن هذا قولهم فلان في عز ومنعة
راجع بصائر ذوي التمييز ج٤ ص ٥٢٥)
(۲) جعل الامتناع من لواحق الوجود ومعه الإمكان والوجوب، وكون الثلاثة لواحق الوجود فظاهر لأن الوجود ينقسم إلى واجب وممكن وممتنع ، وقد يقال إنها من لواحق الماهية أيضاً نظراً إلى أن
لاحق الوجود لاحق الماهية.
.
(۳) فيه مباحث سبعة الأول في ضروريتها وما يتعلق به الثاني في بيان ذاتيتها وعرضيتها . الثالث: في كونها قد تكون مواد القضايا أو جهاتها وما يتعلق به الرابع : في كونها اعتبارية وما يتعلق به الخامس: في كون الإمكان منها يفتقر معروضه إلى مرجع في وجوده وعــدمـه السادس : في أن موجب احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر هو الإمكان أو الحدوث السابع: في أن طرفي الممكن من وجود وعدم لا أولية لأحدهما به لذاته وما يتعلق به .
The second method concerning necessity, impossibility, and possibility.
(He said: The second method concerning necessity (1) and impossibility (2)).
And it contains investigations (3) that make impossibility a consequent of existence and essence. Considering that the necessity of negating the existent from the essence is a state for both, or that it is among the attributes of the conceived essence, or because it is in contrast to possibility, or because what is meant by their consequents is what is customarily investigated after investigating them.
(1) Al-Man': opposite of giving. It is said: mana'a yamna'u man'an, so he is a mani' and manna'. The Most High said: (A preventer of good, transgressing, sinful) Surah Al-Qalam, verse 12. And the Most High said: (And when good touches him, he is withholding) Surah Al-Ma'arij, verse 2. And Al-Mani' is among the attributes of Allah Almighty; it has two meanings. One is what is narrated in the supplication established from the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'O Allah, there is no preventer of what You give, and no giver of what You prevent, and the wealth of the wealthy does not benefit him from You.' The second: that He prevents the people of His religion, i.e., He protects them and supports them. From this is their saying: So-and-so is in honor and prevention. (Refer to Basa'ir Dhawi at-Tamyiz, vol. 4, p. 525).
(2) Making impossibility a consequent of existence, and with it possibility and necessity. And that the three are consequents of existence is apparent because existence is divided into necessary, possible, and impossible. And it may be said that they are also consequents of essence, considering that the consequent of existence is a consequent of essence.
.
(3) It contains seven investigations. The first: concerning their necessity and what pertains to it. The second: concerning explaining their essentiality and accidentally. The third: concerning that they may be the matter of propositions or their modalities and what pertains to it. The fourth: concerning that they are relational (i'tibariyya) and what pertains to it. The fifth: concerning that possibility among them requires its subject to a reference in its existence and non-existence. The sixth: concerning that the cause of the possible's need for an effect is possibility or origination (huduth). The seventh: concerning that the two extremes of the possible, existence and non-existence, have no primacy of one over the other by its essence, and what pertains to it.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the second method (manhaj) of the work, which deals with the three modal judgments: necessity (wujub), impossibility (imtina'), and possibility (imkan). The author first quotes the original text (matn) stating the title of the method. He then explains that this method includes investigations (mabahith) that treat impossibility as a consequent (lahaq) of existence and essence. He provides several reasons for this: either because the necessity of negating the existent from the essence is a state for both, or because impossibility is one of the attributes of the conceived essence, or because it is the opposite of possibility, or because what is meant by 'consequents of existence and essence' is simply what is customarily investigated after discussing existence and essence. The passage then includes three footnotes. The first footnote (1) gives a lexical definition of 'man' (prevention) and explains its theological usage concerning God's attribute as Al-Mani'. The second footnote (2) clarifies that making impossibility a consequent of existence is clear because existence is divided into necessary, possible, and impossible; and it may also be a consequent of essence. The third footnote (3) lists the seven investigations that will be covered: (1) the necessity of these judgments, (2) their essential vs accidental nature, (3) their role as matter or modality of propositions, (4) their relational (i'tibari) nature, (5) possibility's need for a reference in existence and non-existence, (6) whether the need for an effect is due to possibility or origination, and (7) that the two sides (existence, non-existence) of the possible have no inherent priority.

Impossibility as a Property of Existence and Essence
Author clarifies impossibility as a property of existence and essence with four reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why impossibility is considered a property of both existence and essence according to the author.

Core Idea
Impossibility attaches to both existence and essence for four distinct reasons.

Before listing the seven investigations, the author clarifies the status of impossibility. This passage explains that impossibility is not a property exclusive to essence but belongs to both existence and essence, offering four reasons for this inclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المنهج الثاني في الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان. وفيه مباحث: جعل الامتناع من لواحق الوجود والماهية، نظراً إلى أن ضرورة سلب الموجود عن الماهية حال لهما أو إلى أنه من أوصاف الماهية المعقولة، أو لكونه في مقابلة الإمكان، أو لأن المراد بلواحقهما ما جرت العادة بالبحث عنه بعد البحث عنهما. الأول في ضروريتها وما يتعلق به. الثاني في بيان ذاتيتها وعرضيتها. الثالث: في كونها قد تكون مواد القضايا أو جهاتها وما يتعلق به. الرابع: في كونها اعتبارية وما يتعلق به. الخامس: في كون الإمكان منها يفتقر معروضه إلى مرجع في وجوده وعدمه. السادس: في أن موجب احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر هو الإمكان أو الحدوث. السابع: في أن طرفي الممكن من وجود وعدم لا أولية لأحدهما به لذاته وما يتعلق به.
He said: The second method concerning necessity, impossibility, and possibility. And in it are investigations: making impossibility among the properties of existence and essence, either because the necessary negation of the existent from the essence is a state for both, or because it is among the attributes of the conceived essence, or because it is in contrast to possibility, or because what is meant by their properties is what is customarily investigated after investigating them. The first: concerning their necessity and what pertains to it. The second: concerning the explanation of their being essential and accidental. The third: concerning their possibly being matters of propositions or their modalities and what pertains to it. The fourth: concerning their being relational and what pertains to it. The fifth: concerning that possibility among them requires its subject to have a point of reference in its existence and non-existence. The sixth: concerning that the cause of the possible's need for an effect is possibility or temporal origination. The seventh: concerning that the two extremes of the possible, existence and non-existence, have no priority to either of them by itself and what pertains to it.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration passage. The author opens the second methodological chapter (manhaj) on the three rational judgments: necessity (wujūb), impossibility (imtinā'), and possibility (imkān). He first notes that impossibility is treated as a property (lāḥiq) of both existence and essence, offering four possible reasons: (1) because the necessary negation of the existent from the essence is a state for both, (2) because it is an attribute of the conceived essence, (3) because it stands in contrast to possibility, or (4) because by 'properties' here is meant whatever is customarily investigated after the investigation of existence and essence. After this preliminary, he enumerates seven investigations (mabāḥith) that will be taken up in this manhaj: (1) their necessity and related issues, (2) whether they are essential or accidental, (3) whether they serve as the matters or modalities of propositions, (4) whether they are relational (i'tibārī), (5) that possibility requires its subject to have a point of reference in existence and non-existence, (6) whether the possible's need for an efficient cause is due to its possibility or its temporal origination (ḥudūth), and (7) that the two sides of the possible—existence and non-existence—have no inherent priority for either. The passage thus sets out both a conceptual clarification and a roadmap for the discussion.

The Seven Investigations of the Second Method
Author lists seven investigations of the second method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and briefly describe the seven investigations that constitute the second method.

Core Idea
The second method proceeds through seven investigations, each addressing a key aspect of the three rational judgments.

After clarifying the status of impossibility, the author enumerates the seven investigations (mabāḥith) that will be undertaken in this method. These investigations cover topics like necessity, essentiality, propositional modalities, relationality, the need for a point of reference, the cause of need for an effect, and the nature of possibility's extremes.

Guided Path

Each investigation begins with 'ال... في' and a brief description.
2

The first is about their necessity, the second about essential vs. accidental, and so on.
3
Consider the logical flow
Ask yourself why the author chose this order. How does one investigation build on the previous?
The sequence moves from general properties to more specific issues like modality, relationality, and the cause of need.

Rational Judgments
