Specific and General Possibility: Al-Taftazani's Classification
Al-Taftazani classifies meanings of possibility into specific and general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas) and general possibility (al-imkan al-amm), and how they are defined by the negation of different necessities.

Core Idea
Possibility is not one thing—its meaning shifts depending on what necessity it negates.

Al-Taftazani clarifies that 'possibility' can be understood in multiple ways, leading to confusion if not properly distinguished. In this passage, he classifies three meanings based on whether we negate the necessity of existence, non-existence, or both. This distinction is crucial for precise theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the three definitions of possibility presented by Al-Taftazani.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three meanings
List the three meanings: (1) negation of both necessities, (2) negation of necessity of existence only, (3) negation of necessity of non-existence only. Notice how each applies to different categories.
Specific possibility is the first meaning; general possibility is the third.
3
Contrast specific and general possibility
Observe that specific possibility is opposed to both necessity and impossibility, while general possibility is simply negation of impossibility.
The passage says general possibility aligns with common language.
4
Check your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you've grasped the key distinction.
The answer is based on the passage's explicit statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : قد يؤخذ بمعنى سلب ضرورة الوجود أو العدم، فيعم الإمكان الخاص، وضرورة الطرف الآخر، فيصدق على الممتنع ممكن العدم، وعلى الواجب ممكن الوجود، وقد يتوهم أنه بمعنى سلب ضرورة أحد الطرفين. فيعم الكل . الإمكان بمعنى سلب ضرورة الوجود والعدم، هو الإمكان الخاص المقابل للوجوب، والامتناع بالذات وقد يؤخذ بمعنى سلب ضرورة الوجود، فيقابل الوجوب، ويعم الإمكان الخاص والامتناع فيصدق على الممتنع أنه ممكن العدم وقد يؤخذ بمعنى سلب ضرورة العدم، فيقابل الامتناع ويعم الإمكان الخاص والوجوب فيصدق على الواجب أنه ممكن الوجود. وهذا هو الموافق للغة (4) والعرف، ولهذا () سمي بالإمكان العامي، فإن العامة تفهم منه نفي الامتناع فمن إمكان الوجود نفي امتناع الوجود، ومن إمكان العدم
He said: It may be taken in the sense of negation of necessity of existence or non-existence, thus it includes specific possibility and the necessity of the other side, so 'possible non-existence' applies to the impossible, and 'possible existence' applies to the necessary. And it may be misunderstood that it is in the sense of negation of necessity of one of the two sides, thus it includes all. Possibility in the sense of negation of necessity of existence and non-existence is specific possibility, opposed to necessity and essential impossibility. And it may be taken in the sense of negation of necessity of existence, thus it is opposed to necessity and includes specific possibility and impossibility, so 'possible non-existence' applies to the impossible. And it may be taken in the sense of negation of necessity of non-existence, thus it is opposed to impossibility and includes specific possibility and necessity, so 'possible existence' applies to the necessary. And this is in agreement with language and custom, and therefore it is called general possibility; for the general public understands from it the negation of impossibility, so from possibility of existence they understand negation of impossibility of existence, and from possibility of non-existence they understand negation of impossibility of non-existence.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage classifies the different meanings of possibility (imkan). The author first states that possibility may be taken in the sense of negation of necessity of both existence and non-existence, which includes specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas) and the necessity of the opposite side. For example, 'possible non-existence' applies to the impossible, and 'possible existence' applies to the necessary. Some mistakenly think it means negation of necessity of one of the two sides, which would include all. Then the author distinguishes three meanings: (1) Specific possibility: negation of necessity of both existence and non-existence, opposed to necessity and essential impossibility. (2) Possibility as negation of necessity of existence only, opposed to necessity, and includes specific possibility and impossibility; thus the impossible is said to be 'possible non-existence'. (3) Possibility as negation of necessity of non-existence only, opposed to impossibility, and includes specific possibility and necessity; thus the necessary is said to be 'possible existence'. The author then states that the third meaning (negation of necessity of non-existence) is in agreement with language and custom, and therefore it is called general possibility (al-imkan al-amm), because the general public understands from it the negation of impossibility. For example, from possibility of existence they understand negation of impossibility of existence, and from possibility of non-existence they understand negation of impossibility of non-existence. This explanation is in agreement with linguistic usage. Note that the passage ends abruptly; the discussion likely continues on the next page.

Key Terms
الإمكان الخاص
الإمكان العامي
سلب ضرورة الوجود
سلب ضرورة العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General possibility (al-imkan al-amm) is the same as specific possibility (al-imkan al-khas).
Correction: General possibility is broader; it only negates impossibility, while specific possibility negates both necessity of existence and non-existence.

Checkpoint
What does general possibility (al-imkan al-amm) mean according to language and custom?
A) Negation of both necessity of existence and non-existence
B) Negation of necessity of existence only
C) Negation of impossibility
D) Negation of necessity of one side

Key Takeaway
Specific possibility negates both necessities, while general possibility only negates impossibility, aligning with common language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your existence is purely contingent (specific possibility) and not necessary, does your heart truly depend on Allah every moment, or do you act as if your life is guaranteed? What would change if you lived with constant awareness that you could cease to exist at any instant without His sustaining will?
?
The necessary being is described in general language as 'possible existence'—meaning nothing can prevent His existence. Do you ever limit Allah's power by thinking something is impossible for Him, even though it is not logically contradictory? When making dua, do you hold back because you think the situation is truly impossible, forgetting that for Him it is merely 'possible existence'?
?
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between technical and common meanings of possibility. In your daily life, do you treat the categories of 'possible' and 'impossible' with the precision of a scholar, or do you fall into the 'common misunderstanding' by conflating them? How might this affect your reliance on Allah versus your own assessment of circumstances?

The Three Senses of Possibility
Enumeration of three definitions of possibility with logical conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct logical definitions of possibility (imkan) in Islamic theology and how they differ in scope.

Core Idea
Possibility has three distinct meanings, not just one.

The term 'possibility' (imkan) is used in multiple senses in kalam. In this passage, the author clarifies three precise definitions: specific possibility, possibility of existence, and possibility of non-existence. Each has a unique logical form and range of application, setting the stage for a deeper discussion of general possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying three definitions
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author presents three distinct definitions of possibility, each with its own logical condition.
The first definition is specific possibility (imkan khas), the second concerns existence, the third concerns non-existence.
2
Compare the logical scope of each definition
Observe how each definition includes or excludes certain categories: specific possibility excludes both necessity and impossibility, while the other two include one of them.
3
Understand the relationship to general possibility
Notice that the third definition aligns with common usage and is called general possibility. It is defined as the negation of impossibility, which is different from the other two.
This will be important for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : قد يؤخذ بمعنى سلب ضرورة الوجود أو العدم، فيعم الإمكان الخاص، وضرورة الطرف الآخر، فيصدق على الممتنع ممكن العدم، وعلى الواجب ممكن الوجود، وقد يتوهم أنه بمعنى سلب ضرورة أحد الطرفين. فيعم الكل .
الإمكان بمعنى سلب ضرورة الوجود والعدم، هو الإمكان الخاص المقابل للوجوب، والامتناع بالذات وقد يؤخذ بمعنى سلب ضرورة الوجود، فيقابل الوجوب، ويعم الإمكان الخاص والامتناع فيصدق على الممتنع أنه ممكن العدم وقد يؤخذ بمعنى سلب ضرورة العدم، فيقابل الامتناع ويعم الإمكان الخاص والوجوب فيصدق على الواجب أنه ممكن الوجود. وهذا هو الموافق للغة والعرف، ولهذا سمي بالإمكان العامي، فإن العامة تفهم منه نفي الامتناع فمن إمكان الوجود نفي امتناع الوجود، ومن إمكان العدم نفي امتناع العدم. وقد سبق إلى كثير من الأوهام، أن للإمكان العام مفهوماً واحداً، يعم الإمكان الخاص والوجوب والامتناع هو سلب ضرورة أحد الطرفين، أعني الوجود والعدم وهو بعيد جداً. إذ لا يفهم هذا المعنى من إمكان الشيء على الإطلاق، بل إنما يفهم من إمكان وجوده نفي الامتناع، ومن إمكان عدمه نفي الوجوب . ولهذا يقع الممكن العام مقابلا للممتنع شاملاً للواجب كما في تقسيم الكلي إلى الممتنع ، وإلى الممكن الذي أحد أقسامه أن يوجد منه فرد واحد مع امتناع غيره ،كالواجب، وبهذا ينحل ما يقال على قاعدة كون نقيض الأعم أخص من نقيض الأخص، من هذا أنه لو صح الصدق في قولنا كل ما ليس بممكن عام ليس بممكن خاص لكنه باطل لأن كل ما ليس بممكن خاص، فهو إما واجب أو ممتنع ، وكل منهما ممكن عام ، فيلزم أن كل ما ليس بممكن عام فهو ممكن عام .
He said: It may be taken in the sense of the negation of the necessity of existence or non-existence, so it includes specific possibility and the necessity of the other side, so it is true of the impossible that it is possible of non-existence, and of the necessary that it is possible of existence. And it may be mistakenly thought that it is in the sense of the negation of the necessity of one of the two sides, so it includes all.
Possibility in the sense of negation of the necessity of existence and non-existence is specific possibility, opposite to necessity and essential impossibility. And it may be taken in the sense of negation of the necessity of existence, so it is opposite to necessity and includes specific possibility and impossibility, so it is true of the impossible that it is possible of non-existence. And it may be taken in the sense of negation of the necessity of non-existence, so it is opposite to impossibility and includes specific possibility and necessity, so it is true of the necessary that it is possible of existence. This is what conforms to language and custom, and therefore it is called general possibility ('ammi), for the common people understand from it the negation of impossibility. So from the possibility of existence they understand the negation of the impossibility of existence, and from the possibility of non-existence they understand the negation of the impossibility of non-existence. And many minds have previously fallen into the illusion that general possibility has a single concept, which includes specific possibility, necessity, and impossibility, and that it is the negation of the necessity of one of the two sides, namely existence and non-existence. But this is very far from the truth. For this meaning is not understood from the possibility of a thing absolutely; rather, from the possibility of its existence is understood the negation of impossibility, and from the possibility of its non-existence is understood the negation of necessity. And therefore, the general possible is contrasted with the impossible and includes the necessary, as in the division of the universal into the impossible and the possible, one of whose divisions is that a single individual may exist while others are impossible, like the necessary. And by this is resolved what is said concerning the rule that the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific: namely that if the truth were correct in our statement 'Everything that is not generally possible is not specifically possible,' but it is false because everything that is not specifically possible is either necessary or impossible, and both are generally possible, so it follows that everything that is not generally possible is generally possible.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage distinguishes three main senses of possibility (imkan) and refutes a common misunderstanding about general possibility. The author begins by quoting the matn statement that possibility may be taken in the sense of the negation of the necessity of existence or non-existence (salb darurat al-wujud aw al-'adam). This first meaning covers both specific possibility (imkan khas) and the necessity of the opposite side, such that the impossible is said to be 'possible of non-existence' and the necessary 'possible of existence.' Then a warning is given: one might mistakenly think it means the negation of the necessity of either side, covering all cases. Next, the author clarifies by giving three distinct definitions. First, specific possibility (imkan khas) is defined as the negation of both necessity of existence and necessity of non-existence, opposite to necessity and essential impossibility. Second, possibility can be taken as negation of necessity of existence only, opposite to necessity and including specific possibility and impossibility, so the impossible is 'possible of non-existence.' Third, possibility can be taken as negation of necessity of non-existence only, opposite to impossibility and including specific possibility and necessity, so the necessary is 'possible of existence.' The third sense conforms to language and custom, called general possibility (al-imkan al-'ammi): common people understand from 'possible of existence' the negation of impossibility of existence, and from 'possible of non-existence' the negation of impossibility of non-existence. The author then refutes an error: some think general possibility has a single concept covering specific possibility, necessity, and impossibility, defined as the negation of necessity of either existence or non-existence. This is rejected because the possibility of a thing absolutely does not convey that meaning; rather, it conveys the negation of impossibility for existence and the negation of necessity for non-existence. Therefore, the general possible is contrasted with the impossible and includes the necessary, as seen in the division of the universal into the impossible and the possible—one division of which is a single individual existing while others are impossible, like the necessary. Finally, the author resolves a problem about the rule that the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific. If one says 'Everything not generally possible is not specifically possible,' this seems plausible but is false because what is not specifically possible is either necessary or impossible, both of which are generally possible, leading to a contradiction. The correct understanding resolves this.

Key Terms
إمكان خاص
سلب ضرورة الوجود
سلب ضرورة العدم
ممكن العدم
ممكن الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which definition of possibility is called 'specific possibility' (al-imkan al-khas)?
A) The negation of necessity of existence only
B) The negation of necessity of non-existence only
C) The negation of both necessity of existence and necessity of non-existence
D) The negation of necessity of either existence or non-existence

Key Takeaway
Possibility has three precise logical definitions; only the third (negation of necessity of non-existence) aligns with common usage and is called general possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is possible of existence,' how does this lesson challenge your understanding of the term 'possible'? Are you willing to accept that your everyday use of the word may imply necessity rather than contingency?
?
Examine your own judgments: have you ever dismissed something as 'impossible' when in fact it was only 'not necessary of existence'? How will this precise distinction change the way you evaluate claims about faith and reality from now on?
?
This lesson shows that general possibility includes even the necessary. If you apply this to your own life, what things do you consider 'possible' that are actually necessary for your faith? How will you realign your thinking to recognize the difference?

Refuting a Misconception about General Possibility
Refutation of a misunderstanding about general possibility and resolution of a logical puzzle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common error about general possibility being a single concept and why it leads to a logical paradox.

Core Idea
General possibility is not a single concept but a context-dependent negation of impossibility.

Some scholars mistakenly think general possibility is one concept covering all modes. The author strongly rejects this, showing that general possibility is understood differently for existence and non-existence. He then resolves a logical puzzle that arises from the mistaken view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken view
Read the passage and find the description of the erroneous idea: that general possibility is a single concept defined as the negation of necessity of either existence or non-existence.
2
Understand the author's correction
The author explains that general possibility is not a single concept; rather, it is understood contextually: 'possible of existence' negates impossibility, 'possible of non-existence' negates necessity.
3
Follow the logical resolution
The author shows how the mistaken view leads to a contradiction in the rule about negation of the more general, and how the correct understanding resolves it.
This step involves careful logical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This is rejected because the possibility of a thing absolutely does not convey that meaning; rather, it conveys the negation of impossibility for existence and the negation of necessity for non-existence. Therefore, the general possible is contrasted with the impossible and includes the necessary, as seen in the division of the universal into the impossible and the possible—one division of which is a single individual existing while others are impossible, like the necessary. Finally, the author resolves a problem about the rule that the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific. If one says 'Everything not generally possible is not specifically possible,' this seems plausible but is false because what is not specifically possible is either necessary or impossible, both of which are generally possible, leading to a contradiction. The correct understanding resolves this.

Key Terms
إمكان عام
نفي الامتناع
نقيض الأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General possibility is a single concept defined as negation of necessity of either existence or non-existence.
Correction: General possibility is understood contextually: negation of impossibility for existence, negation of necessity for non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'possible of non-existence' (mumkin al-'adam) mean in the context of general possibility?
A) It means the thing is necessary
B) It means the negation of necessity of existence
C) It means the thing is impossible
D) It means the negation of impossibility of non-existence

Key Takeaway
General possibility is not a single concept; understanding it correctly resolves a logical paradox about the negation of the more general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you first read the statement 'Everything that is not generally possible is not specifically possible,' did you accept it as obviously true? What does your willingness to accept a seemingly logical claim without deep reflection reveal about how you approach matters of faith?
?
The author warns that many minds have fallen into an illusion about a single concept of possibility. Where in your own understanding of aqeedah might you be guilty of oversimplifying complex distinctions because a single-concept definition feels more comfortable?
?
This passage shows that a precise understanding of general possibility resolves a contradiction. What contradiction in your own life—between what you claim to believe and how you act—might be resolved if you were willing to think more precisely about a core Islamic concept, instead of settling for a vague definition?

Possibility with Respect to the Future and the Condition of Non-Existence
The author discusses possibility as futurity and the requirement of present non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility is specifically associated with the future and the debate about whether present non-existence is a condition for possibility.

Core Idea
Possibility is most fitting for the future because its state is unknown.

This lesson builds on the three rational judgments by focusing on possibility (imkan) as it relates to the future. The passage clarifies why the future is the primary domain of possibility and introduces a debate about whether possibility requires present non-existence, along with an objection that challenges that condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument.
2
Identify why the future is most deserving of 'possible'
The passage explains that the future is most deserving of the name 'possible' because the state of existence or non-existence is not yet determined.
This contrasts with the past and present where the state is known.
3
Understand the condition of present non-existence
Some scholars condition present non-existence for the possible with respect to the future, arguing that existence is a necessity and thus must be absent.
4
Examine the objection
The objection states that non-existence is also a necessity, so the condition would also require freedom from non-existence, creating a problem.
The verification of this objection is incomplete in the passage.
5
Reflect on the incompleteness
Note that the text cuts off, leaving the response to the objection unfinished. Consider what a complete verification might entail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد يعتبر بالنظر إلى الاستقبال، ومن اشترط فيه العدم في الحال كأنه أراد به إمكان طريان الوجود في المستقبل، ففي إمكان العدم يشترط الوجود في الحال ولا يلزم الجمع بين النقيضين.
بمعنى جواز وجود الشيء في المستقبل، من غير نظر إلى الماضي والحال، وذلك لأن الإمكان في مقابلة الضرورة، وكلما كان الشيء أخلى عن الضرورة كان أحق باسم الممكن، وذلك في المستقبل، إذ لا يعلم فيه حال الشيء من الوجود والعدم، بخلاف الماضي والحال، فإنه قد تحقق فيهما وجود الشيء أو عدمه، ومنهم من اشترط في الممكن الاستقبال العدم في الحال لأن الوجود ضرورة، فيجب الخلو عنه .
ورد؛ بأن العدم أيضاً ضرورة، فيجب الخلو عنه أيضاً، وتحقيقه أنه ...
He said: And it may be considered with regard to the future. And whoever conditions in it non-existence in the present, it is as if he intended by it the possibility of occurrence of existence in the future. So in the possibility of non-existence, existence is conditioned in the present, and the combination of two contradictories does not follow.
Meaning: the permissibility of existence of a thing in the future, without regard to the past and present. This is because possibility is opposite to necessity, and whenever a thing is more devoid of necessity, it is more deserving of the name 'possible,' and that is in the future, since the state of the thing as to existence or non-existence is not known therein, unlike the past and present, for in them the existence or non-existence of the thing has been realized. And some of them conditioned in the possible with respect to the future non-existence in the present because existence is necessity, so it must be devoid of it.
It was objected: that non-existence is also necessity, so it must also be devoid of it. And its verification is that ...
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage discusses possibility (imkan) specifically with respect to the future (al-istiqbal). The author quotes the matn statement that possibility may be considered with regard to the future. The commentator explains that whoever conditions present non-existence in the possible does so because they intend the possibility of existence occurring in the future. In the case of the possibility of non-existence, they condition present existence, and no contradiction follows. The meaning is that it is the permissibility (jawaz) of a thing's existence in the future, without considering the past or present. This is because possibility is opposed to necessity (darura), and the more a thing is free from necessity, the more it deserves the name 'possible.' This applies particularly to the future because we do not know whether the thing will exist or not, unlike the past and present where existence or non-existence has been realized. Then the author notes that some scholars condition present non-existence for the possible with respect to the future because existence is necessity (if a thing exists, it is necessary, not possible), so the possible must be free from existence. However, an objection is raised: non-existence is also necessity (if a thing does not exist, its non-existence is necessary), so the possible must also be free from non-existence. The commentator then begins to present the verification (tahqiq) of this issue, but the text cuts off at 'wa tahqiqahu annahu...' leaving the response incomplete. This objection points out an inconsistency in the condition of present non-existence, as it would also require freedom from the necessity of non-existence.

Key Terms
استقبال (istiqbal)
جواز (jawaz)
ضرورة (darura)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility requires present non-existence because existence is necessary.
Correction: Both existence and non-existence are necessary in their respective actual states, so a simple condition of freedom from existence is insufficient without distinguishing modal necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is possibility particularly associated with the future?
A) Because the future is unpredictable
B) Because the future is free from necessity
C) Because in the future the state of existence or non-existence is not yet determined, making it most suitable for possibility
D) Because possibility only applies to future events

Key Takeaway
Possibility is most appropriately applied to the future because its state is undetermined, but the condition of present non-existence is problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your future, do you view it as genuinely possible and open, or do you feel locked into what must happen? How does this lesson's distinction between future and past challenge your daily trust in Allah?

The author notes that the future is most deserving of the label 'possible' because we do not know its state. Are you comfortable with not knowing your future? What does your anxiety (or lack of it) reveal about your reliance on Allah's decree?
?

Future Possibility and the Condition of Present Non-Existence
Discussion of future possibility and refutation of the condition of present non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of future possibility in kalam and why stipulating present non-existence as a condition leads to a logical contradiction that is resolved by distinguishing between possibility of existence and possibility of non-existence.

Core Idea
Future possibility is about the unknown future, not a requirement of present non-existence.

This passage from al-Maqasid tackles a subtle point in the logic of possibility. The author explains that possibility concerning the future is defined without reference to the present or past, because possibility is the opposite of necessity. Some scholars, however, insisted that for a thing to be possible in the future, it must not exist now. This leads to a puzzle: if we require present non-existence for possibility of existence, then for possibility of non-existence we would require present existence, leading to contradiction. The author resolves this by clarifying that the condition is only meant for the possibility of the occurrence of existence, not for the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of future possibility
Read the first part of the passage where the author defines future possibility as the permissibility of a thing's existence in the future without regard to the past and present, because possibility is opposite to necessity.
Notice that the author emphasizes the future as the realm of uncertainty.
2
Grasp the objection to the condition of present non-existence
Some stipulate present non-existence for future possibility. The author objects: if existence is necessary in the present, then non-existence is also necessary, leading to a requirement of both, which is impossible.
This objection shows the logical flaw in the condition.
3
See the resolution
The author resolves the objection by distinguishing: the stipulator intends the possibility of the occurrence of existence, not the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence. For the latter, present existence would be required, but that is not the intended meaning.
Thus, the condition does not lead to contradiction when properly understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد يعتبر بالنظر إلى الاستقبال، ومن اشترط فيه العدم في الحال كأنه أراد به إمكان طريان الوجود في المستقبل، ففي إمكان العدم يشترط الوجود في الحال ولا يلزم الجمع بين النقيضين. بمعنى جواز وجود الشيء في المستقبل، من غير نظر إلى الماضي والحال، وذلك لأن الإمكان في مقابلة الضرورة، وكلما كان الشيء أخلى عن الضرورة كان أحق باسم الممكن، وذلك في المستقبل، إذ لا يعلم فيه حال الشيء من الوجود والعدم، بخلاف الماضي والحال، فإنه قد تحقق فيهما وجود الشيء أو عدمه، ومنهم من اشترط في الممكن الاستقبال العدم في الحال لأن الوجود ضرورة، فيجب الخلو عنه . ورد؛ بأن العدم أيضاً ضرورة، فيجب الخلو عنه أيضاً، وتحقيقه أنه ممكن في جانبي الوجود والعدم، وكما أن الوجود يخرجه إلى جانب الوجود كذلك، ويشترط الخلو عنه. كذلك العدم يخرجه إلى جانب الامتناع، فيلزم اشتراط الخلو عنه أيضاً، فيلزم ارتفاع النقيضين بل اجتماعهما، والظاهر من اشترط ذلك أراد بالإمكان الاستقبالي إمكان حدوث الوجود، وطريانه في المستقبل وهو إنما يستلزم إمكان عدم الحدوث، لا إمكان حدوث العدم، ليلزم اشتراط الوجود في الحال، بل لو اعتبر الإمكان الاستقبالي في جانب العدم، بمعنى إمكان طريان العدم وحدوثه، يشترط الوجود في الحال من غير لزوم محال .
He said: And it may be considered with respect to the future. Some have stipulated present non-existence for it, as if they intended by it the possibility of the occurrence of existence in the future. For the possibility of non-existence, existence is stipulated in the present without necessitating a conjunction of two contradictories. Meaning: the permissibility of a thing's existence in the future, without regard to the past and present. This is because possibility is opposite to necessity, and the more a thing is devoid of necessity, the more deserving it is of the name 'possible'. This is in the future, since the state of the thing from existence and non-existence is not known in it, unlike the past and present, for existence or non-existence of the thing has already been realized in them. Some have stipulated for future possibility present non-existence because existence is necessary, so one must be free from it. It was refuted: because non-existence is also necessary, so one must also be free from it. The verification is that it is possible in both sides of existence and non-existence. Just as existence removes it to the side of existence and requires freedom from non-existence, similarly non-existence removes it to the side of impossibility, thus requiring freedom from it as well, leading to the raising of both contradictories, indeed their conjunction. But it appears that whoever stipulated that intended by future possibility the possibility of the occurrence of existence and its arising in the future, which only entails the possibility of non-occurrence, not the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence, so that stipulation of existence in the present would be required. On the contrary, if future possibility were considered with respect to non-existence, meaning the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence and its arising, then existence would be stipulated in the present without necessitating an impossibility.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage discusses the notion of future possibility (al-imkan al-istiqbali). The author first presents a definition: considering possibility with respect to the future, and notes that some scholars stipulate present non-existence as a condition for future possibility. The author explains that this condition is based on the idea that possibility of existence in the future requires that the thing not exist now, but this does not lead to a contradiction because possibility concerns the future. The rationale is that possibility is the opposite of necessity; the future is unknown, so it is more deserving of the term 'possible'. Then the author presents an objection: if one stipulates present non-existence because existence is necessary (i.e., if it existed now, it would be necessary), then one must also stipulate present existence for the possibility of non-existence, because non-existence is also necessary. This would lead to requiring both, which is impossible. The author resolves this by noting that the one who stipulates present non-existence actually means the possibility of the occurrence of existence, not the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence. If one considers the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence, then present existence is required, but that is not the intended sense. So the objection is answered.

Key Terms
الإمكان الاستقبالي
الوجود
العدم
الضرورة
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Future possibility requires present non-existence as a necessary condition.
Correction: Future possibility is defined without reference to present state; stipulating present non-existence leads to contradiction unless understood as only concerning the possibility of existence occurring, not the possibility of non-existence occurring.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct condition for future possibility?
A) Present non-existence is necessary.
B) It is considered without regard to past or present.
C) Both existence and non-existence must be possible.
D) It requires present existence.

Key Takeaway
Future possibility is considered without reference to past or present; stipulating present non-existence for it leads to a false requirement of both existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you fully convinced that the future is truly unknown and that only Allah knows what will be, or do you secretly assume certain outcomes are necessary? How will you cultivate reliance on Allah's knowledge over your own assumptions?
?
This lesson shows that even scholars can make subtle logical errors by treating possibility and necessity symmetrically. Where in your own life do you demand impossible conditions before trusting in Allah's plan?
?
Do you ever dismiss the possibility of something happening simply because it hasn't happened yet, forgetting that future possibility is independent of the present? What would change if you truly embraced that Allah's power makes all non-necessary things possible?

The Definition of Imkan and Its Distinction from Wujub and Imtina'
Al-Taftazani defines imkan as a rational judgment distinct from wujub bi al-ghayr and imtina' bi al-ghayr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how imkan is defined as a rational judgment about essence considered in itself, distinct from necessity-by-other and impossibility-by-other.

Core Idea
Imkan is a conceptual possibility, not a real third state between existence and non-existence.

Al-Taftazani clarifies a subtle but crucial point in kalam: possibility is not a real property that can exist independently. Instead, it is a rational consideration that arises when we think about an essence without regard to its existence or non-existence. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows how imkan relates to necessity and impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three rational judgments
Read the passage and identify how the same essence can be considered necessary, impossible, or possible depending on what is assumed.
Notice that the essence itself doesn't change; only our consideration changes.
2
Grasp the concept of 'by-other'
Necessity and impossibility here are not intrinsic but acquired from external factors (existence or its cause).
This distinguishes 'bi al-ghayr' from 'dhati' judgments.
3
Distinguish conceptual from actual
The passage emphasizes that possibility is separate only in thought, not in reality—every possible thing is either existent or non-existent.
This guards against the misconception of imkan as a real third state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيث هو مقيساً إلى الوجود، وأما مع اعتبار الوجود أو العدم، فيعرض الوجوب أو الامتناع الغيري، فهو ينفك عنهما تعقلاً لا تحققاً. يعني أن الماهية إذا أخذت مع وجودها أو وجود علتها كانت واجبة بالغير، وإذا أخذت مع عدمها أو عدم علتها كانت ممتنعة بالغير، وإنما يعرض لها الإمكان الصرف، إذا أخذت لا مع وجودها أو عدمها أو وجود علتها أو عدمها، بل اعتبرت من حيث هي هي، واعتبرت نسبتها إلى الوجود، فحينئذ يحصل من هذه المقايسة معقول هو الإمكان. فالإمكان ينفك عن الوجوب بالغير والامتناع بالغير بحسب التعقل، بأن لا يلاحظ للماهية ولا لعلتها وجود أو عدم، لا بحسب التحقق في نفس الأمر، لأن كل ممكن فهو إما موجود فيكون واجباً بالغير، أو معدوم فيكون ممتنعاً بالغير، اللهم إلا على رأي من يثبت الواسطة.
Where it is measured against existence. But with consideration of existence or non-existence, there occurs non-essential necessity or non-essential impossibility. It (possibility) is separable from them conceptually, not in actuality. That is, when the essence is taken with its existence or the existence of its cause, it becomes necessary-by-other, and when taken with its non-existence or the non-existence of its cause, it becomes impossible-by-other. Pure possibility only occurs to it when it is taken neither with its existence nor its non-existence, nor with the existence or non-existence of its cause, but it is considered purely in itself, and its relation to existence is considered; then from this comparison arises an intelligible which is possibility. So possibility is separable from necessity-by-other and impossibility-by-other in terms of conception, meaning that no existence or non-existence is noted for the essence or its cause, not in terms of actualization in reality, because every possible entity is either existent (then it is necessary-by-other) or non-existent (then it is impossible-by-other), except according to the view of those who affirm an intermediate.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of possibility (imkan) as a rational judgment distinct from necessity and impossibility, highlighting that it is considered relative to existence and non-existence. The author explains that when an essence is considered with its existence or the existence of its cause, it becomes necessary-by-other (wajib bi al-ghayr). When considered with its non-existence or the non-existence of its cause, it becomes impossible-by-other (mumtani' bi al-ghayr). But possibility (imkan) arises when the essence is considered purely in itself, without regard to existence or non-existence, and its relation to existence is considered. The key point is that possibility is conceptually separable from necessity and impossibility, but in actuality every possible thing is either existent (thus necessary-by-other) or non-existent (thus impossible-by-other), except for those who posit an intermediate state.

Key Terms
Imkan (الإمكان)
Wujub bi al-ghayr (الوجوب بالغير)
Imtina' bi al-ghayr (الامتناع بالغير)
Mahiyyah (الماهية)
Wujub dhati (الوجوب الذاتي)
Imtina' dhati (الامتناع الذاتي)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imkan is a real third state between existence and non-existence.
Correction: Imkan is a rational judgment, not an actual state. In reality, every possible thing is either existent (then necessary-by-other) or non-existent (then impossible-by-other).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does an essence become wajib bi al-ghayr (necessary-by-other)?
A) When considered purely in itself
B) When taken with its existence or the existence of its cause
C) When taken with its non-existence or the non-existence of its cause
D) When its relation to existence is considered

Key Takeaway
Imkan is a conceptual possibility that arises when an essence is considered in itself, apart from any actual existence or non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you recognize that you are contingent—a 'possible' being—and that without a necessary cause you would be nothing? How does this realization affect your daily gratitude to Allah?

The lesson teaches that imkan is a conceptual distinction, not a real property. What would change if you always kept this in mind?

Defining Al-Hal: The Intermediary Attribute
Al-Taftazani defines al-hal with logical and semantic clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-hal (the state) as an attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent, and the logical relations that necessitate this category.

Core Idea
Al-hal is a third rational category between existence and non-existence for certain attributes.

Al-Taftazani begins by clarifying the logical relationship between two types of non-essential attributes (wujub bi al-ghayr and imtina' bi al-ghayr). He then introduces al-hal as an intermediary concept, defined as an attribute of an existent that is itself neither existent nor non-existent. This definition is carefully qualified to avoid confusion with existential or negative attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical preamble
Read the opening lines about the two non-essential attributes sharing the name of necessity when their objects are opposite, and how they relate to one another. This sets the stage for the concept of al-hal.
The 'two non-essential' refer to wajib bi al-ghayr and mumtani' bi al-ghayr.
2
Identify the definition of al-hal
Find the explicit definition: an attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent. Note the examples given: 'being-knowing' and 'being-powerful'.
3
Examine the three qualifications
The definition includes three cautionary phrases: 'of an existent' excludes attributes of non-existents, 'not existent' excludes existential attributes, and 'not non-existent' excludes negative attributes. Understand each.
These qualifications prevent common misunderstandings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والغيريان يتشاركان في اسم الضرورة عند تقابل المضاف إليه، وحينئذ يتصادقان، وعند اتحاده يتنافيان، فبينهما منع الجمع مع جواز الانقلاب، وكذا بين بين الذاتين استحالته كما بين الذاتي وغير الذاتي من الوجوب والامتناع لاستلزامه الإمكان المنافي للذاتي والاستدلال بأن للغير فهو الحال فهو واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، لأنه عبارة عن صفة لموجود لا تكون موجودة ولا معدومة مثل العالمية والقادرية ونحو ذلك. والمراد بالصفة مالا يعلم ولا يخبر عنه بالاستقلال بل بتبعية الغير، والذات بخلافها، وهي لا تكون إلا موجودة أو معدومة، بل لا معنى للموجود إلا ذات لها صفة العدم والصفة لا يكون لها ذات فلا تكون موجودة ولا معدومة. فلذا قيد بالصفة واحترز بقولهم: لموجود عن صفات المعدوم فإنها تكون معدومة لا حالا. وبقولهم: لا موجودة عن الصفات الوجودية مثل السواد والبياض وبقولهم: ولا معدومة عن الصفات السلبية. قال الكاتبي: وهذا الحد لا يصح على مذهب المعتزلة، لأنهم جعلوا الجوهرية من الأحوال مع أنها حاصلة للذات حالتي الوجود والعدم. قلنا: إنما يتم هذا الاعتراض لو ثبت ذلك من أبي هاشم، وإلا فمن المعتزلة من لا يقول بالحال، ومنهم من يقول بها لا على هذا الوجه، ثم قال: وأول من قال بالحال أبو هاشم وفصل القول فيه بأن الأعراض التي لا تكون مشروطة بالحياة كاللون والرائحة لا توجب لمن قامت به حالاً ولا صفة، إلا الكون فإنه يوجب لمحله الكائنية وهي من الأحوال، وأما الأعراض المشروطة بالحياة، فإنها توجب لمحلها أحوالاً للمحل كالعلم للعالمية والقدرة للقادرية. وزعم القاضي وإمام الحرمين أن كل صفة فهي توجب للمحل حالاً، كالكون للكائنية والسواد للأسودية والعلم للعالمية ومنهم من خالف في نفي كون المعدوم ثابتاً وهم أكثر المعتزلة، حيث زعموا أن المعلوم إن كان له كون الأعيان فموجود وإلا فمعدوم فلا واسطة بينهما ـ وباعتبار آخر: المعلوم إن كان له تحقق في نفسه وتقرر فثابت وإلا فمنفي وكل ما له كون في [؟]
He said: The two non-essential (attributes) share the name of necessity when the object to which they are added is opposite; then they are both true together, and when it is the same they are mutually exclusive. So between them is the impossibility of both being present while conversion is permissible. Likewise, between two essential (attributes) its impossibility is like between the essential and non-essential of necessity and impossibility because it entails possibility which is incompatible with the essential. And the argument is that the non-essential is the state (al-hal), so it is an intermediary between existent and non-existent, because it is an expression of an attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent, like 'being-knowing' and 'being-powerful' and so on. And what is meant by attribute is that which is not known or spoken of independently but by way of another, and the essence is different from that; it is only existent or non-existent. Rather, there is no meaning for 'existent' except an essence having the attribute of non-existence, and the attribute does not have an essence, so it is neither existent nor non-existent. Therefore, they restricted it to attribute and took caution by saying 'of an existent' against attributes of the non-existent, for they are non-existent not a state; and by saying 'not existent' against existential attributes like blackness and whiteness; and by saying 'not non-existent' against negative attributes. Al-Katibi said: This definition is not valid according to the Mu'tazila, because they considered substantiality (jawhariyya) as a state, even though it occurs for the essence in both states of existence and non-existence. We say: This objection is only valid if that is established from Abu Hashim, otherwise there are among the Mu'tazila those who do not affirm states, and among them those who affirm them but not in this manner. Then he said: The first to affirm states was Abu Hashim, and he detailed the view that accidents which are not conditional on life, like color and odor, do not necessitate for their locus a state or attribute, except for spatial location (kawn), for it necessitates for its locus 'being-located' (al-ka'iniyya) which is of the states. As for accidents conditional on life, they necessitate for their locus states, like knowledge for 'being-knowing' and power for 'being-powerful'. Al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn claimed that every attribute necessitates a state for its locus, like spatial location for 'being-located', blackness for 'being-black', knowledge for 'being-knowing'. And among them are those who opposed denying the fixedness of the non-existent, and they are the majority of the Mu'tazila, for they claimed that if the object of knowledge has the existence of entities (kawn al-a'yan), it is existent, otherwise it is non-existent, and there is no intermediary between them. And by another consideration: if the object of knowledge has actualization in itself and determination, it is fixed, otherwise it is negated. And everything that has existence in [?]
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of al-hal (the state) as an intermediate category between existent and non-existent, particularly those attributes that are neither actually existent nor non-existent in themselves. The author begins by explaining the logical relationship between the two non-essential attributes (wujub bi al-ghayr and imtina' bi al-ghayr): they share the name of necessity when their objects are opposite, and they are mutually exclusive when the object is the same. Then he introduces the argument that the non-essential attribute is the state (al-hal), defined as an attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent, like being knowing or powerful. The author clarifies the definition, cautioning that the state is an attribute, not an essence, and that it qualifies an existent but is itself neither existent nor non-existent. He then presents al-Katibi's objection that this definition fails according to the Mu'tazila because they consider substantiality (jawhariyya) as a state even though it pertains to an essence in both existence and non-existence. The author responds by noting that the objection relies on a specific Mu'tazili view attributed to Abu Hashim, but not all Mu'tazila hold that view; some do not affirm states at all, and others affirm them differently. The passage then traces the origins of the concept of al-hal to Abu Hashim, explaining his distinction between accidents that are conditional on life and those that are not. It further contrasts the views of other scholars like al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn regarding the extent of states. Finally, the passage ends with a discussion of the Mu'tazila's stance on the non-existent (al-ma'dum), noting that most of them reject an intermediate between existence and non-existence, and they hold that everything is either existent or non-existent based on whether it has the existence of entities (kawn al-a'yan) or not.

Key Terms
Al-hal (الحال)
Sifah (الصفة)
Wujub bi al-ghayr (الوجوب بالغير)
Imtina' bi al-ghayr (الامتناع بالغير)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-hal is a separate entity like a substance that can exist independently.
Correction: Al-hal is an attribute that cannot exist by itself; it depends on an existent subject and is neither existent nor non-existent.

Checkpoint
What definition of al-hal does the passage provide?
A) It is an existent essence that is neither necessary nor contingent.
B) It is an attribute of an existent that is itself neither existent nor non-existent.
C) It is a non-existent attribute that is fixed in the mind.
D) It is a substance that mediates between existence and non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Al-hal is defined as an attribute of an existent that is neither existent nor non-existent, and this definition is carefully qualified to avoid confusion with other categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God’s attributes (like His knowledge or power) as if they are separate things existing on their own? How does the concept of al-hal—that attributes have no independent existence—call you to rethink the way you speak about Allah? What concrete change can you make today in how you describe His attributes to avoid slipping into imagining them as independent entities?
?
Consider your own qualities: your intelligence, your strength, your kindness. Do you ever think of them as things you 'have' rather than as descriptions of you as a person? How does the lesson’s insistence that an attribute is not independent—only known through its subject—change the way you see yourself and others? What would it look like to live with the humility that your attributes are nothing on their own, but only exist in relation to your Creator?

The Debate over Al-Hal: Objections and Sectarian Positions
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes al-Katibi's objection, then discusses diverse Mu'tazili and Ash'ari views on al-hal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Katibi's objection to the definition of al-hal, al-Taftazani's response, and the diversity of views among Mu'tazila and later theologians on the nature of states.

Core Idea
Al-Katibi's objection fails because Mu'tazila disagree among themselves about the existence and definition of states.

The concept of al-hal was not universally accepted. Al-Katibi raises an objection: the definition fails according to the Mu'tazila because they consider substantiality (jawhariyya) a state even in non-existence. Al-Taftazani responds by noting that the Mu'tazila are not unified; some reject states altogether, others define them differently. He then traces the origin of the concept to Abu Hashim and presents various later formulations.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Katibi's objection
Al-Katibi argues that the definition of al-hal does not hold for the Mu'tazila because they treat substantiality (jawhariyya) as a state, even though it belongs to the essence in both existence and non-existence.
Substantiality is the attribute of being a substance (jawhar).
2
Analyze al-Taftazani's response
Al-Taftazani points out that the objection assumes a specific Mu'tazili view from Abu Hashim, but not all Mu'tazila hold that view. Some reject states entirely, others accept them differently.
3
Trace the history of the concept
Learn that Abu Hashim was the first to use the term 'al-hal' systematically. He distinguished between accidents conditional on life and those not, and which ones necessitate states.
Accidents like color and odor do not produce states, except for spatial location (kawn).
4
Compare later views
Al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn held that every attribute produces a state for its locus. Then consider the Mu'tazili majority who deny an intermediate between existence and non-existence, rejecting states for non-existents.

He said: The two non-essential (attributes) share the name of necessity when the object to which they are added is opposite; then they are both true together, and when it is the same they are mutually exclusive. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila held the same view on states.
Correction: The Mu'tazila were divided: some rejected states, others accepted them but not uniformly, and the majority rejected an intermediate between existence and non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does al-Taftazani consider al-Katibi's objection not fully valid?
A) Because al-Katibi misquoted Abu Hashim.
B) Because the objection only applies if Abu Hashim's specific view is taken, but not all Mu'tazila follow it.
C) Because the definition of al-hal is not meant for the Mu'tazila.
D) Because substantiality is not actually a state.

Key Takeaway
The concept of al-hal was debated among theologians, with significant differences between Mu'tazila and Asharites, and even within the Mu'tazila themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological debate as complex as the one on states, are you tempted to side with a camp before understanding the nuances? What would it cost you to instead suspend judgment and examine each view on its own terms? This passage reveals that even within a school, opinions vary widely. How does that challenge your own tendency to label groups simplistically?
?
Al-Taftazani points out that not all Mu'tazila agree on states. In your own pursuit of truth, do you rely on authorities or do you seek to understand the arguments themselves? If you were to defend a position on states, could you, like Abu Hashim, articulate precise distinctions? Or would you rely on slogans?
?
The majority of Mu'tazila reject an intermediary between existence and non-existence. This is not just a theoretical point: it affects how they understand God's knowledge of non-existent things. Reflect: Does your concept of God allow for such precision, or have you settled for vague attributions? What would it mean for your worship to cultivate a more exact understanding of divine attributes?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Negation of Thubut of the Non-Existent and the Intermediate
Ash'ari negation of thubut and intermediate with two proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that the non-existent has no thubut (establishment) and that there is no intermediate state between existence and non-existence, along with the two proofs they provide.

Core Idea
Existence and non-existence are exclusive; no third state is conceivable.

The Ash'ari school firmly denies that the non-existent (ma'dum) has any form of thubut (established reality) and rejects any intermediate between existence and non-existence. This passage presents their core argument based on rational necessity and then provides two proofs to counter the Mu'tazilite position. As you read, note how the author uses the concept of 'thingness' (shay'iyya) to reinforce the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the main claim
Start by reading the opening statement where the Ash'ari position is stated: negation of thubut of the non-existent and negation of the intermediate.
The phrase 'في المقامين' refers to both the negation of thubut and the negation of the intermediate.
2
Understand the necessity argument
The passage claims that reason (darura) judges that no thubut is conceivable except existence (mental or external), and no 'adam except the negation of that. Consider why no intermediate is possible.
'Thubut' here means being established as something, not necessarily physical existence.
3
Analyze the opponent's terminological move
The passage addresses the Mu'tazilite who makes existence more specific than thubut and introduces an intermediate attribute. See how the author dismisses this as mere terminology.
The term 'mukabir' (stubborn) shows the author's strong rejection.
4
Examine the first proof
The first proof argues that if the non-existent had thubut, it would contradict divine power (qudra), because the essence (dhat) is eternal and existence is a state not subject to power.
This connects to the Ash'ari view that only existent things can be objects of power.
5
Examine the second proof
The second proof states that non-existence ('adam) is a negating attribute, so the described (mawsuf) would be negated. Think about how this undermines the idea of a non-existent being 'established'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا) نفي ثبوت المعدوم ونفي الواسطة بينه وبين الوجود في المقامين (۲) الضرورة (٣) فإنه لا يقل من الثبوت إلا الوجود ذهناً أو خارجاً من العدم إلا نفي ذلك، ولا يتصور بينهما واسط. أي في نفي ثبوت المعدوم وشيئيته ونفي الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم الضرورة فإنها قاضية بذلك، إذ لا يعقل من الثبوت إلا الوجود ذهناً أو خارجاً من العدم إلا نفي ذلك، والشيئية تساوق الوجود، فالثابت في الذهن أو الخارج موجود فيه، وكما لا تعقل الواسطة بين الثابت والمنفي، فكذا بين الموجود والمعدوم والمنازع مكابر وجعل الوجود أخص من الثبوت والعدم من المنفي)، وجعل الموجود ذاتاً لها الوجود والمعدوم ذات لها العدم، لتكون الصفة واسطة اصطلاح (٦) لا مشاحة فيه . قال : فاستدل بوجوه (۷) الأول: أن ثبوت المعدوم ينافي المقدورية، لأن الذات أزلية (۷) والوجود حال لا يتعلق به قدرة (۸) الثاني : أن العدم صفة نفي فينتفي الموصوف به.
For us: the negation of the establishment (thubut) of the non-existent (ma'dum) and the negation of the intermediary between it and existence (wujud) in the two stations: necessity (darura). For nothing is conceived of establishment except existence mentally or externally, and of non-existence except the negation of that, and no intermediary between them is conceivable. That is: in the negation of the establishment of the non-existent and its thingness (shay'iyya) and the negation of the intermediary between the existent and the non-existent, necessity judges that, because no establishment is conceivable except existence mentally or externally, and no non-existence except the negation of that. And thingness accompanies existence, so what is established in the mind or externally is existent in it. And just as no intermediary is conceivable between the established (thabit) and the negated (manfi), so too between the existent and the non-existent. And the opponent is stubborn, and he made existence more specific than establishment, and non-existence more specific than negation, and he made the existent a substance (dhat) having existence, and the non-existent a substance having non-existence, so that the attribute becomes an intermediary — a terminological convention with no quarrel. He said: so he adduced proofs: first: that the establishment of the non-existent contradicts being empowered (maqduriyya), because the substance is eternal (azaliyya) and existence is a state not subject to power. Second: that non-existence is a negating attribute, so the described (mawsuf) is negated.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage states the Ash'ari position (lana) and provides two proofs. The first claim is the negation of thubut (establishment) of the non-existent and the negation of any intermediate between existence and non-existence. The justification is necessity (darura): reason sees that the only possible states are existence (mentally or externally) or non-existence (negation of both). There is no third state. Thingness (shay'iyya) is equivalent to existence, so whatever is established is existent. Thus there is no intermediate between thabit and manfi, and likewise between mawjud and ma'dum. The opponent (the Mu'tazila) who posit an intermediate do so only by renaming existence as more specific and calling non-existence as more specific, and then positing an attribute that is intermediate — but this is just a terminological convention (istilah). Then the passage presents two proofs against the thubut of the non-existent: first, if the non-existent had thubut, that would contradict divine power (qudra), because the substance (dhat) is eternal and existence is a state that cannot be the object of power; second, non-existence is a negating attribute, so if something is described as non-existent, the described itself would be negated. These arguments support the Ash'ari rejection of the Mu'tazilite notion of the non-existent as something.

Key Terms
ثبوت
معدوم
واسطة
ضرورة
شيئية
مقدورية
أزلية
مكابر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The non-existent (ma'dum) has some form of thingness (shay'iyya) or thubut independent of existence.
Correction: Thingness accompanies existence; whatever is established (thabit) is existent (mawjud), so the non-existent cannot have any positive reality or thingness.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does thubut of the non-existent contradict divine power (qudra)?
A) Because power can only affect what is existent
B) Because the substance is eternal and existence is a state not subject to power
C) Because non-existence is a negating attribute
D) Because there is no intermediate between existence and non-existence

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that existence and non-existence are mutually exclusive with no intermediate, and the non-existent has no thubut or thingness, supported by rational necessity and two proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that nothing exists except by Allah's will and power—but do you ever act as if your future plans have a 'reality' of their own, independent of His creation? What would change if you truly internalized that non-existence has no thubut?
?
When you imagine something you desire that does not yet exist, does your heart attach to it as if it were already 'established'? How does this lesson demand that you redirect that attachment to Allah alone, the only Giver of existence?
?
If the non-existent has no positive reality, then even your own sins before you commit them have no real existence—only Allah's power brings them into being. Does this truth increase your hope in His mercy or your fear of His justice? How will you live differently knowing that every moment of existence is a fresh gift from Him?

The Ash'ari Position on the Non-Existent: Necessity of No Existence
Sanusi states the Ash'ari position and proves by necessity that the non-existent has no existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari claim that the non-existent has no existence and why this is considered a matter of necessity.

Core Idea
The non-existent has no existence by necessity—there is no middle ground.

Sanusi opens his discussion of the non-existent by stating the Ash'ari position: the non-existent has no affirmation (thubut) and no existence. He argues that this is a matter of necessity—no rational person can conceive of an intermediate state between existence and non-existence. In this lesson, we will unpack his argument and see how he responds to a terminological objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the source Arabic passage carefully. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning. Notice the flow from the claim to the proof to the rebuttal.
Pay attention to the key terms: thubut, wujud, 'adam.
2
Identify the main claim
Sanusi states that we (the Ash'aris) deny that the non-existent has any affirmation (thubut) and deny any intermediate between it and existence. This is the core position.
Remember that 'thubut' in this context means affirmation or reality.
3
Understand the necessity argument
The proof is based on necessity: nothing is understood from affirmation except existence (mental or external), and from non-existence except the negation of that. There is no other possibility. Therefore, the non-existent cannot have any thubut.
If someone claims an intermediate, they are being obstinate.
4
Examine the terminological objection and rebuttal
Opponents distinguish between existence and affirmation, making existence an attribute. Sanusi dismisses this as mere terminology—it doesn't change the reality. The point is that the non-existent has no positive reality.
This shows the importance of clarity in terminology.
5
Reflect on the significance
Why does this matter? It establishes that only existent things have reality. This is foundational for discussing divine attributes and creation.
This lesson sets the stage for further proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا) نفي ثبوت المعدوم ونفي الواسطة بينه وبين الوجود في المقامين (٢) الضرورة (٣) فإنه لا يقل من الثبوت إلا الوجود ذهناً) أو خارجاً من العدم إلا نفي ذلك، ولا يتصور بينهما واسط. أي في نفي ثبوت المعدوم وشيئيته ونفي الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم الضرورة فإنها قاضية بذلك، إذ لا يعقل من الثبوت إلا الوجود ذهناً، أو خارجاً من العدم إلا نفي ذلك، والشيئية تساوق الوجود، فالثابت في الذهن أو الخارج موجود فيه، وكما لا تعقل الواسطة بين الثابت والمنفي، فكذا بين الموجود والمعدوم والمنازع مكابر وجعل الوجود أخص من الثبوت والعدم من المنفي)، وجعل الموجود ذاتاً لها الوجود والمعدوم ذات لها العدم، لتكون الصفة واسطة اصطلاح (٦) لا مشاحة فيه . قال : فاستدل بوجوه .
We hold: the negation of the affirmation of the non-existent and the negation of the intermediate between it and existence in both respects is necessity. For nothing is understood from affirmation except existence mentally or externally, and from non-existence except the negation of that, and no intermediate between them is conceivable. That is, regarding the negation of the affirmation of the non-existent and its thingness, and the negation of the intermediate between the existent and the non-existent, necessity judges that. For nothing is conceived from affirmation except existence mentally or externally, and from non-existence except the negation of that, and thingness is coextensive with existence. So what is affirmed in the mind or externally is existent therein. And just as no intermediate is conceivable between the affirmed and the negated, so too between the existent and the non-existent. The opponent is obstinate. And they made existence more specific than affirmation, and non-existence more specific than the negated thing. They made the existent a essence having existence, and the non-existent an essence having non-existence, so that the attribute becomes an intermediate in terminology—no quibbling there. He said: then he adduced proofs.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position regarding the non-existent and its relation to existence. The author begins by stating the view: we deny that the non-existent has any affirmation or existence, and we deny any intermediate between existence and non-existence. This is claimed to be a matter of necessity. The author explains that 'affirmation' (thubut) is only understood as existence (wujud), either mentally or externally, and 'non-existence' ('adam) is the negation of that existence. There is no third state between them. He then addresses a terminological objection: some opponents distinguish between existence and affirmation, and non-existence and negation, making existence a quality of an essence. The author dismisses this as a mere terminological convention that does not affect the argument, and he notes that those who disagree are simply obstinate. The passage concludes by stating that the author will now adduce further proofs for those who do not accept the necessity argument.

Key Terms

الشيئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'affirmation' (thubut) can be something other than existence, such as a neutral state.
Correction: Sanusi insists that thubut is exactly existence—either mental or external. There is no third state.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between affirmation (thubut) and existence (wujud)?
A) Affirmation is more general than existence.
B) Affirmation is equivalent to existence.
C) Existence is an attribute of affirmation.
D) Affirmation is a mental concept only.

Key Takeaway
The non-existent has no existence—this is a necessary truth because thubut is nothing but existence, and there is no intermediate state between existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the non-existent has no existence—it is simply nothing. Your own existence right now is a fact, not a middle ground. Do you live as if your existence is a gift from the One who is Existence Itself, or do you take it for granted? How would your prayer, your work, and your relationships change if you truly recognized that you are nothing without His constant bringing you into existence?
?
The passage says 'thingness is coextensive with existence.' That means anything real is only real by existing. Is there anything in your life that you consider 'real' but separate from the Existence of Allah—like your money, your status, or your health? Will you today affirm that all reality is from Him alone, and let go of the illusion of independent things?
?
Sanusi says the opponent is 'obstinate' (مكابر). Where in your heart have you been obstinate against clear truths—truths about Allah, about death, about your own dependency? What would it take for you to put down that obstinacy and submit to the One by whose will you exist at this very moment?

Fourth Proof: Non-Finite Non-Existents vs. Finite Existents and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents a proof and its refutation regarding the infinity of non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof regarding the infinity of non-existent essences and its refutation, particularly the logical error in assuming finitude after subtracting a finite part from an infinite whole.

Core Idea
Subtracting a finite part from an infinite set does not make it finite.

Sanusi continues examining proofs for the existence of non-existent essences. The fourth proof argues that if non-existents are infinite but existents finite, a contradiction arises. The refutation challenges the claim that subtracting a finite amount from an infinite yields finitude, and criticizes the method of 'tatbiq' (pairing) between infinite sets. This lesson unpacks both the argument and the rebuttal, clarifying how infinite quantities behave differently from finite ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the proof is stated first, then its refutation.
2
Understand the proof's logic
The proof claims that since existent non-existents are finite, the whole set of non-existents minus that finite part would be finite. Identify the assumption that subtracting a finite from an infinite yields a finite remainder.
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation denies that subtraction of a finite from an infinite necessarily yields finitude. It also questions the validity of using 'tatbiq' (pairing) between an infinite set and its subset.
The refutation highlights a key misunderstanding about infinity.
4
Reflect on the weakness of tatbiq
The method of 'tatbiq' attempts to compare infinite sets by pairing elements, but this is problematic for infinite sets. Think about why such pairing might be weak or inconclusive.
This connects to later developments in set theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنها غير متناهية مع أن الموجود منها متناه. فالكل أكثر من الباقية على العدم بمنتاه فتكون متناهية .
والرابع : يمنع تناهي ما يزيد على الغير بمتناه . بل إذا كان الغير متناهياً. وإثبات ذلك بالتطبيق بينه وبين الكل ضعيف.
Fourth: that they are non-finite while the existent among them is finite. So the whole is greater than the remainder in non-existence by a finite amount, so they become finite. And the fourth [refutation] denies the finitude of that which exceeds the other by a finite amount. Rather, if the other is finite, and proving that by applying it between it and the whole is weak.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This lesson presents the fourth proof and its refutation. The proof argues that if non-existent essences are infinite in number, but the existent ones among them are finite, then the total set of non-existent essences minus the finite existent ones would still be infinite, but the proof claims that the whole (all non-existents) is greater than the finite part (the existents) by a finite amount, implying finitude. The refutation denies the premise that subtracting a finite amount from an infinite leaves a finite remainder; it also says that using the method of 'tatbiq' (pairing) between an infinite set and its subset is weak.

Key Terms
غير متناهية
متناه
الكل
الباقية
التطبيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that subtracting a finite number from an infinite set necessarily results in a finite set.
Correction: In infinite sets, subtracting a finite subset leaves an infinite remainder. For example, subtracting 1 from the set of natural numbers still leaves an infinite set.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation of the fourth proof?
A) The whole is always greater than the part.
B) Subtracting a finite amount from an infinite does not necessarily yield finitude.
C) Non-existents are actually finite.
D) The existent is also infinite.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof fails because it assumes that subtracting a finite part from an infinite whole makes the whole finite, which is not true for infinite sets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that an infinite set can contain a finite subset without becoming finite. Do you ever try to limit Allah’s infinite knowledge or power by assuming He only deals with what fits your finite understanding? What would it mean for you to truly accept His infinity without adding mental restrictions?
?
The refutation in this passage rejects a flawed proof despite its apparent logic. In your own life, where have you clung to a logical argument that felt satisfying but actually limited your view of Allah’s attributes? Will you let go of that today?
?
The method of 'tatbiq' is called weak for measuring the divine. Where in your worship do you rely on mechanical ‘mapping’—thinking that certain practices or quantities guarantee closeness to Allah—rather than genuinely submitting to His limitless will? How can you break that pattern starting now?
