The Fifth Proof and Its Refutation: Generality and Specificity of Non-Existence
Sanusi presents a logical proof and its refutation concerning the categories of non-existent and negated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical relationship between 'non-existent' and 'negated thing' in the fifth proof, and why the refutation shows that not being pure negation does not entail pure affirmation.

Core Idea
Not all non-existents are pure negation; intermediate possibilities refute the proof.

In this kalam, Sanusi presents a proof that attempts to derive an impossible consequence from the relationship between the non-existent (ma'dum) and the negated thing (manfi). The proof argues that if the non-existent is equal to or more specific than the negated, it cannot be affirmed; but if it is more general, it cannot be pure negation—leading to a contradiction. The refutation, however, exposes a false dichotomy: not being pure negation does not require pure affirmation, thereby invalidating the proof's conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the proof (three cases), then the refutation, then a final objection and response.
The passage uses technical terms: madum, manfi, 'amm, khas.
2
Analyze the proof's three possibilities
Identify the three logical relationships: equality, specificity, and generality. For each, note what the proof claims follows.
The proof aims to show an impossibility.
3
Understand the refutation's key insight
Focus on the line: 'its not being pure negation does not entail its being pure affirmation'. This is the crucial move that blocks the conclusion.
The refutation allows for intermediate cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أن المعدوم كان مساوياً للمنفي أو أخص منه لم يكن ثابتاً، وإن كان أعم منه لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، والا لما بقي فرق بين العام والخاص. بل ثابتاً . وهو صادق على المنفي فيلزم ثبوته وهو محال .
والخامس : بأن عدم كونه نفياً صرفاً لا يستلزم كونه اثباتاً صرفاً بل قد وقد فلا يصدق إلا بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم ثبوت المنفي . فإن قيل : المراد أنه لو كان أعم لكان متميزاً عن الخاص، فيكون ثابتاً إلزاماً قلنا فيلغوا أكثر المقدمات .
Fifth: that the non-existent either equals the negated or is more specific: if so, it would not be affirmed; and if it is more general, then it would not be pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between general and specific. Rather, it is affirmed. And it is true of the negated, so it necessitates its affirmation, which is impossible. And the fifth [refutation] is that its not being pure negation does not entail its being pure affirmation; rather it may be such and such. So only some of the non-existent is affirmed, so the negation of the negated thing is not entailed. If it is said: the intended meaning is that if it were more general, it would be distinguished from the specific, so it would be affirmed by implication—we say: then many of the premises are invalid.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This lesson presents the fifth proof and its refutation. The proof examines the logical relationship between 'non-existent' (ma'dum) and 'negated thing' (manfi). If they are coextensive or non-existent is more specific, then non-existent would not be affirmed. If non-existent is more general, then it is not pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between general and specific. So it would be affirmed, and since it applies to the negated, the negated would be affirmed, which is absurd. The refutation denies the implication: not being pure negation does not mean it is pure affirmation; it could be something in between. Therefore, only some non-existents are affirmed (in the sense of having thubut), not the entire category of the negated. The final objection-response addresses that if the opponent insists that being more general implies a distinction that amounts to affirmation, the refutation says that would invalidate many of the premises.

Key Terms
المعدوم
المنفي
العام
الخاص
نفي صرف
إثبات صرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that if something is not purely negated, it must be purely affirmed.
Correction: The refutation shows that there are intermediate states between pure negation and pure affirmation, so the dichotomy is false.

Checkpoint
What is the key insight in the refutation of the fifth proof?
A) Non-existent is always more general than negated.
B) Not being pure negation does not imply pure affirmation.
C) The non-existent is never affirmed.
D) The negated thing is always affirmed.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the fifth proof teaches that between pure negation and pure affirmation there is a middle ground, so the non-existent can be more general without being purely affirmed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a theological issue where you have assumed a binary outcome: either something is entirely negated of Allah or entirely affirmed. What intermediate possibility (affirmed in some sense but not purely) might you have overlooked? How does this lesson's refutation demand you reconsider that assumption?
?
Think of a personal habit or sin you have rationalized by saying 'since I am not completely denying this duty, I must be fulfilling it.' Does this lesson reveal a logical flaw in that reasoning? What concrete step will you take today to honestly examine where you fall on the spectrum between pure negation and pure affirmation?

The Absolute Non-Existent and the Tautology Argument
Al-Taftazani explains the logical flaw in the argument from the absolute non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical flaw in arguing that the non-existent is fixed, and why distinguishing between the attribute and the described is crucial.

Core Idea
Confusing the attribute and the described turns the argument into a tautology.

Al-Taftazani dissects an opponent's argument about the non-existent, exposing a hidden tautology. He shows that the argument only works if we treat the word 'non-existent' as an attribute distinct from the entity it describes. This lesson unpacks that logical subtlety.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense logical style.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent tries to prove that the non-existent is fixed. Find in the passage where al-Taftazani questions this approach and suggests a simpler formulation.
3
Understand the tautology
Al-Taftazani shows that the opponent's reasoning reduces to 'if the non-existent is fixed, then the non-existent is fixed'. Ask yourself why this is a problem and how distinguishing attribute from described resolves it.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعدوم : فأي حاجة للمستدل إلى إثبات ذلك بالتكلف، ليفرع عليه ثبوت المنفي؟ . وهلا قال من أول الأمر - لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً وهو صادق على المنفي لزم ثبوته . قلنا : زعمه أن الذوات المعدومة الممكنة ثابتة ومقصود المستدل إثبات : أن الوصف الذي هو المعدوم المطلق ثابت منه ثبوت موصوفه. وإلى هذا يشير قول المواقف : لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً كان المعدوم أعم بإعادة لفظ المعدوم دون ضميره . ألا بری أن ما آل إليه كلامه أنه لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً. لكان المعدوم ثابتاً فلو لم يرد الموصوف وبالثاني الوصف لكان لغواً. ومما يجب التنبه له : أن المراد بالأعم في تقرير الإمام ، ما يشمل العموم المطلق، والعموم من وجه ليتم الحسر، وفي تقرير المواقف يجوز أن يحمل على المطلق ويبين الملازمة بأنه صادق على كل منفي ) .
The non-existent: So what need does the arguer have to prove that with difficulty, to base upon it the fixity of the negated? And why did he not say from the beginning: 'If the non-existent were fixed and it is truthfully said of the negated, then its fixity would follow.' We said: His claim that the possible non-existent essences are fixed, and the intention of the arguer is to prove that the attribute which is the absolute non-existent is fixed, from it follows the fixity of its described. And to this refers the saying of al-Mawaqif: 'If the non-existent were fixed, then the non-existent would be more general, by repeating the word 'non-existent' without its pronoun.' Do you not see that what his statement comes down to is that if the non-existent were fixed, then the non-existent would be fixed, so if he did not intend the described by the first and the attribute by the second, it would be nonsense. And among what must be noticed is that what is meant by the more general (al-aʿamm) in the exposition of al-Imam is that which includes absolute generality and generality from one aspect to complete the investigation, and in the exposition of al-Mawaqif, it is permissible to interpret it as absolute and to show the entailment by saying it is true of every negated.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument. The author (al-Taftazani) begins by pointing out a redundancy in the opponent's reasoning. The opponent tries to prove that the non-existent (المعدوم) is fixed (ثابت) by arguing that the term 'non-existent' applies to the negated (المنفي), and if it were fixed, then its fixity would follow. The author asks why the opponent did not simply state from the beginning that if the non-existent is fixed and it applies to the negated, then the negated is fixed. Then the author explains that the opponent's real claim is that possible non-existent essences are fixed, and the arguer intends to prove that the attribute 'absolute non-existent' is fixed, and from that follows the fixity of its described (i.e., the entity that is non-existent). The author cites al-Mawaqif's statement that if the non-existent were fixed, then the non-existent would be more general (أعم) by repeating the word 'non-existent' without using a pronoun, which leads to a tautology. The author then clarifies that the intended meaning of 'more general' in al-Imam's formulation is that it includes both absolute generality and generality from one aspect, while in al-Mawaqif's formulation it can be taken as absolute generality, and the entailment is shown by the fact that the predicate 'non-existent' applies to every negated thing. Thus, the passage highlights the logical subtlety in distinguishing between the attribute and the described.

Key Terms
المعدوم
المطلق
المنفي
ثبوت
أعم
الموصوف
الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is a simple tautology with no further significance.
Correction: The tautology arises only if one fails to distinguish between the attribute ('non-existent' as an attribute) and the described (the entity). The argument is about the relationship between them.

Checkpoint
What does al-Taftazani identify as the real point in the argument about the non-existent?
A) That the non-existent is fixed in the mind
B) That the argument is a tautology if it does not distinguish between the attribute and the described
C) That the non-existent is a mental concept
D) That the opponent's argument is invalid because it relies on an unclear premise

Key Takeaway
The argument about the non-existent collapses into a tautology unless we carefully distinguish between the attribute (absolute non-existent) and the described (the entity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allah is All-Knowing,' do you ever pause to ask whether you treat that attribute as a living reality or just a familiar phrase that you repeat? Where in your daily life do you need to connect that attribute to your actions?
?
This lesson shows how easily an argument becomes a tautology when we confuse the attribute with the described. Is there any belief you hold about Allah that is merely a verbal repetition—something you say but have not truly integrated into your understanding? Will you examine one such belief today?
?
The refutation here required careful attention to language. Are you willing to examine your own statements of faith with that same precision, even if it means humbling yourself to admit you were merely repeating words without real insight? What will you do with that admission?

Two Arguments for the Thingness of the Non-Existent and Their Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents two arguments and a combined refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two arguments for the thingness of the non-existent and Al-Taftazani's combined refutation based on the mental nature of distinction and possibility.

Core Idea
Distinction and possibility are mental, not external, so they don't prove the non-existent is a thing.

Al-Taftazani introduces the first two rational arguments used by those who claim that the non-existent has thingness (شيئية). The first argument relies on the distinction of the non-existent in the mind, and the second on its possibility. He then refutes both together by showing that distinction and possibility are merely mental considerations that do not entail external existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation once to grasp the flow of argument and refutation.
Notice the structure: two arguments introduced by 'الأول' and 'الثاني', followed by 'قلنا' for the refutation.
2
Identify the first argument (distinction)
The opponent argues that the non-existent is distinct because it is known, willed, and an object of power. Every distinct thing must be fixed because distinction implies mental pointing, and pointing to pure negation is impossible.
The key term is 'تميز' (distinction).
3
Identify the second argument (possibility)
The opponent argues that the non-existent is possible, and every possible thing must be fixed because possibility is a positive attribute implying a subject.
The key term is 'إمكان' (possibility).
4
Understand the refutation
Al-Taftazani responds that both distinction and possibility are mental; they only require fixity in the mind, not in external existence. If they required external fixity, impossible things and imaginary compounds would also have to exist externally, which is absurd.
Notice the reduction ad absurdum: if mental attributes necessitated external existence, impossible things like a partner of God would exist.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutation is crucial for the debate on the non-existent. It shows that mental attributes are insufficient to prove external existence, preserving the distinction between mental and external reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة القائلين بشيئية المعدوم والرد عليهم (قال): تمسك المخالف بوجوه. الأول: أن المعدوم متميز، لأنه معلوم ومراد ومقدور وكل متميز ثابت لأن التميز إنما يكون بالإشارة العقلية، والإشارة إلى النفي الصرف محال . الثاني : أنه ممكن وكل ممكن ثابت لأن الإمكان ثبوتي. قلنا: كل من التميز والإمكان ،عقلي يكفيه ثبوت المتميز والممكن في الذهن، ولو اقتضيا الثبوت عيناً لزم ثبوت الممتنعات لتميزها، والمركبات الخياليات (۳) التميزها وإمكانها.
Proofs of those who affirm the thingness of the non-existent and the refutation against them. (He said): The opponent clung to several aspects. First: That the non-existent is distinct, because it is known, willed, and powerful over it, and every distinct thing is fixed because distinction only occurs by mental pointing, and pointing to pure negation is impossible. Second: That it is possible, and every possible is fixed because possibility is a positive attribute. We said: Both distinction and possibility are mental; the fixity of the distinct and the possible in the mind suffices for them. Had they required external fixity, then the fixity of impossible things would follow because of their distinction, and of imaginary compounds because of their distinction and possibility.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments used by the opponents who affirm the thingness (شيئية) of the non-existent, along with a combined refutation. The first argument is based on distinction (تميز): the non-existent is distinct because it is known, willed, and can be an object of power. Every distinct thing must be fixed (ثابت) because distinction is only possible through mental pointing (إشارة عقلية), and pointing to pure non-existence is impossible. The second argument is based on possibility (إمكان): the non-existent is possible, and every possible thing is fixed because possibility (الإمكان) is a positive attribute, so its subject must be fixed. The refutation (قلنا) responds to both: distinction and possibility are merely mental considerations; they only require the distinct or possible to be fixed in the mind, not in external existence. If they required external fixity, then impossible things like the partner of God would also have to be fixed externally because they are distinct in the mind, and likewise imaginary compounds would have to be fixed because they are distinct and possible in imagination.

B) Impossible things and imaginary compounds would have to be fixed externally
C) The possible would be non-existent
D) The mind would be unable to conceive them

Key Takeaway
Distinction and possibility are mental attributes that do not require external existence; otherwise, impossible things would also have to exist.

What would change if you accepted that such concepts may only be mental?

The refutation says: if distinction implied external existence, even impossible things would exist. Consider a seemingly noble idea you are certain about—could it be that you are treating a mental construct as if it were an external necessity? Are you willing to honestly examine that today?

Refuting the Claim of Intrinsic Essence for Non-Existent Things
Al-Taftazani refutes the third argument for thingness using negation and privation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the argument that non-existent things have an essence independent of existence, using the distinction between negation and privation.

Core Idea
Essence and existence are not separable in reality; the non-existent has no essence of its own.

This passage presents a crucial debate in Islamic theology: do non-existent things have a fixed essence? Al-Taftazani takes on the third argument of those who affirm thingness, showing how the claim leads to contradictions and is based on a misunderstanding of the relationship between essence and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument flow. Note the back-and-forth structure.
Pay attention to the distinction between negation and privation.
2
Identify the opponent's core claim
Find where the opponent asserts that non-existent blackness is black in itself by itself. This is the premise being refuted.
3
Understand the refutation strategy
Al-Taftazani uses two key moves: first, that as blackness is removed, existence is also removed; second, a distinction between two meanings of impossibility (negation vs. privation).
4
Apply the distinction to the argument
See how the refutation works by considering the two possible meanings of the opponent's impossibility claim. This shows the opponent's argument is not valid.
Finally, the opponent's argument about essence independent of others is countered by noting that disregarding a thing does not negate it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن معنى ثبوت المعدوم أن السواد المعدوم مثلاً سواد في نفسه به إذ لو كان ذلك بالغير لزم ارتفاعه بارتفاع الغير، فلا يبقى السواد الموجود سواداً حينئذ (٤) قلنا: ممنوع إذ كما ترتفع سواديته يرتفع وجوده . فإن قيل : فلا يكون السواد سواداً وهو محال . قلنا : بمعنى السلب فيمتنع الاستحالة، أو بمعنى العدول بأن (۱) ماهية السواد مع كونها لا سواداً فتمتنع الملازمة. فإن قيل : السواد سواد، وإن لم يوجد الغير ضرورة أن لكل شيء ماهية هو بها مع قطع النظر عن كل ما عداه قلنا قطع النظر عن الشيء (٢) لا يوجب انتفاءه) .
Third: That the meaning of the fixity of the non-existent is that the non-existent blackness, for example, is blackness in itself by itself, because if it were by another, then the blackness of existing blackness would cease; then the existing blackness would not remain black. We said: It is prevented, for as its blackness is removed, its existence is removed. If it is said: 'Then it would not be black, which is impossible,' we say: In the sense of negation, the impossibility is impossible, or in the sense of privation, that the essence of blackness while being not black is nonsense, so the entailment is impossible. If it is said: 'Blackness is blackness even if the other does not exist, necessarily because everything has an essence by which it is what it is, regardless of everything else,' we say: Disregarding a thing does not necessitate its non-existence.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage deals with the third argument of those who affirm the thingness of the non-existent. The argument states that the fixity of the non-existent means that a non-existent blackness, for instance, is blackness in itself and by itself (سواد في نفسه به). If its blackness were derived from another (i.e., from existence), then when that other is removed, the blackness would cease to be blackness, so an existent blackness would not remain black. The refutation (قلنا) begins by denying this: as its blackness disappears, so does its existence. If an objector says that then it would not be black, which is impossible, the author responds that this impossibility is either in the sense of pure negation (سلب) or privation (عدول). If it is negation, then the impossibility of the thing being not black is itself impossible (since a non-existent is not actually anything). If it is privation, then the essence of blackness while being not black is a contradiction, so the entailment that it must be black in itself fails. The opponent further argues that everything has an essence independent of others, so blackness is blackness regardless of existence. The author replies that disregarding a thing does not entail its non-existence; the essence is only considered in the mind, not externally.

ماهية
سلب
عدول
الغير
ارتفاع
قطع النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existent things have an essence that is independent of existence.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that such a claim leads to contradictions and that essence is mentally considered, not externally real without existence.

Checkpoint
How does al-Taftazani respond to the claim that a non-existent blackness is black in itself?
A) He agrees but says it is only in the mind
B) He argues that as blackness is removed, existence is also removed, so the question is moot
C) He says that blackness is always black even if non-existent
D) He uses the concept of possibility to refute it

Key Takeaway
The non-existent has no intrinsic essence; its consideration is only mental, not external.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the non-existent has no essence of its own. How many of your fears or desires are actually attached to things that do not yet exist—and how do you treat them as if they already have a fixed reality? What would change if you truly internalised that they are nothing until Allah brings them into existence?
?
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that an essence can be considered in itself, independent of all else. In your life, do you ever think you are 'something' in yourself, without relying on Allah? When you succeed, do you attribute it to your own essence or to the One who gives existence? Write down one area where you habitually forget your dependence on Allah.

The opponent insisted that blackness must be blackness even if it doesn't exist. How might you change your perspective to see all goodness as contingent on Allah's will and existence?

The Distinction Argument for the Non-Existent and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the distinction-based argument for the external fixity of the possible non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the possible non-existent is externally fixed because it is distinct, and al-Taftazani's refutation of that argument.

Core Idea
Mental distinction does not imply external existence.

Al-Taftazani presents an opponent's argument: the possible non-existent must be externally fixed because it can be distinguished from other things. He then refutes it by showing that distinction is a mental operation and only requires mental fixity. This lesson unpacks the logic of the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get the flow of the argument.
Note the footnote marker (٤) after 'النفي الصرف'—it's a scholarly notation.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The opponent's argument has a minor premise (the non-existent is distinct) and a major premise (distinct things are fixed). Identify each part.
The minor premise is supported by examples: known, willed, powerful over.
3
Understand the pointing principle
The major premise relies on the idea that distinction requires mental pointing, and pointing requires the pointed-to to exist. Grasp this link.
The opponent claims pointing to pure non-existence is impossible.
4
Study the refutation
Al-Taftazani's answer distinguishes between external and mental distinction. If by 'distinct' you mean external, it's denied; if mental, it only proves mental fixity.
This refutation shows the argument equivocates on 'distinction'.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this refutation is important: it blocks a key 'proof' for the external existence of non-existents, reinforcing that mental existence suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن المعدوم الممكن ثابت في الخارج تمسكوا بوجوه : الأول : أنه متميز وكل متميز ،ثابت أما الصغرى فلأنه قد يكون معلوماً فيتميز عن غير المعلوم، ومراداً فيتميز عن غير المراد، ومقدوراً فيتميز عن غير المقدور. وأما الكبرى فلأن التميز عند العقل لا يتصور إلا بالإشارة العقلية بهذا وذاك، والإشارة تقتضي ثبوت المشار إليه ضرورة امتناع الإشارة إلى النفي الصرف (٤) . والجواب عن الأول : أنه إن أريد أن التميز يقتضي الثبوت في الخارج فممنوع، وإنما يلزم لو كان التميز بحسب الخارج وإن أريد في الذهن فلا يفيد .
Those who say that the possible non-existent is fixed externally clung to several aspects: First: That it is distinct, and every distinct thing is fixed. As for the minor premise, because it may be known, so it is distinct from the unknown; willed, so distinct from the unwilled; and powerful over, so distinct from that which is not an object of power. As for the major premise, because distinction in the intellect cannot be conceived except by a mental pointing to this and that, and pointing requires the fixity of the pointed-to, necessarily because pointing to pure non-existence is impossible. The answer to the first: That if what is meant is that distinction necessitates external fixity, it is prevented; it would only be necessary if distinction were external, but if it is in the mind, it does not benefit.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of a second group of opponents who claim that the possible non-existent is fixed externally (ثابت في الخارج). The argument is based on distinction (تميز): the possible non-existent is distinct because it can be known, willed, and be an object of power; therefore, it must be fixed in reality. The major premise is that distinction can only occur through mental pointing, and pointing to pure non-existence is impossible, so the object pointed to must be fixed. The refutation is given in the answer: if distinction requires external fixity, it is denied; rather, it only requires mental fixity. Distinction is a mental operation, and its object need only be mentally present, not externally existent.

Key Terms

إشارة عقلية
ثبوت
خارج
ذهن
مقدور
مراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is distinct in the mind, it must also exist in external reality.
Correction: Distinction is a mental operation; it only proves mental fixity, not external existence. The non-existent can be mentally distinct without being externally real.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw in the argument that the possible non-existent is externally fixed because it is distinct?
A) Distinction does not exist for non-existents
B) Distinction only proves mental fixity, not external fixity
C) The minor premise is false because the non-existent cannot be known
D) The major premise is false because pointing is possible

Key Takeaway
The distinction argument fails because distinction is a mental operation and only proves mental fixity, not external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Possibility as Mental Consideration
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that possibility is an external attribute requiring external fixity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that possibility is a mental consideration, not an external attribute, and how this refutes the claim that the possible non-existent must be externally fixed.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental tool, not a real property.

This passage presents the second argument of a group claiming that the possible non-existent must be externally fixed because possibility is a positive attribute. Al-Taftazani refutes this by clarifying that possibility is merely a mental consideration. He then points out the absurd consequence that impossibilities would also have to be externally fixed if possibility were an external attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the dialectical structure: first an argument, then its refutation. The passage is incomplete at the end.
2
Identify the main claim
The opponent claims that possibility is a positive attribute (وصف ثبوتي) requiring external fixity. This is the basis for arguing that the possible non-existent exists externally.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Taftazani denies that possibility is external; it is a mental consideration (اعتبار عقلي). Therefore, the subject only needs to be fixed in the mind, not in external reality.
4
Reflect on the absurd consequence
If possibility required external fixity, the same logic would force impossibilities like the partner of God or the conjunction of contradictories to be externally fixed, which is absurd. This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه ممكن، وكل ممكن ثابت لأن الإمكان وصف ثبوتي على ما سيأتي ، فيكون الموصوف به ثابتاً بالضرورة. وعن الثاني : أنا لا نسلم (٥) كون الإمكان ثبـوتيـاً بمعنى كـونـه ثـابتـاً في الخارج، بل هو اعتبار عقلي يكفي ثبوت الموصوف به في العقل، ثم لا خفاء أن الممتنعات كشريك الباري ، واجتماع النقيضين وكون الجسم في آن في
Second: That it is possible, and every possible is fixed because possibility is a positive attribute, as will come, so the subject qualified by it is necessarily fixed. And the answer to the second: We do not grant that possibility is positive in the sense of being fixed externally; rather, it is a mental consideration sufficient for the fixity of the subject in the mind. Then it is no secret that impossible things such as the partner of God, the conjunction of contradictories, and the body being in an instant in...
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument of the same group: the possible non-existent is fixed because possibility (الإمكان) is a positive attribute (وصف ثبوتي), and every attribute requires a subject that is fixed. Therefore, the possible must be fixed externally. The refutation denies that possibility is a positive attribute in the sense of existing externally. Instead, possibility is a mental consideration (اعتبار عقلي); it only requires the subject to be mentally fixed, not externally existent. The author then points out that if possibility required external fixity, then impossible things would also have to be fixed externally, which is absurd. The argument is cut off but the point is clear: the same reasoning would force the fixity of impossibilities.

Key Terms

وصف ثبوتي
اعتبار عقلي
ممتنعات
شريك الباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is a real, external attribute that requires its subject to exist externally.
Correction: Possibility is a mental consideration, not an external attribute. It only requires the subject to be mentally fixed, not externally existent.

Checkpoint

A) Because possibility is a negative attribute
B) Because possibility is a mental consideration that only requires mental fixity
C) Because the possible is not fixed at all
D) Because possibility implies non-existence

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a mental consideration, not an external attribute, so the possible non-existent is only mentally fixed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If all possibilities are merely mental considerations, then your fears about future events are also mental constructs. Will you commit to treating your anxieties as passing thoughts rather than fixed realities? What concrete step can you take today to shift your focus from possible outcomes to trust in Allah?

Objection and Response on Non-Existent Blackness
Author responds to objections about blackness remaining blackness when non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the view that non-existent things do not retain their properties by distinguishing between negation and alteration and by clarifying the role of mental abstraction.

Core Idea
Non-existence does not strip an essence of its identity; it simply means the property is absent.

This passage presents a debate about whether a non-existent thing can still be called by its essence. The opponent argues that blackness must always be blackness, even when non-existent. The author responds with two key distinctions: between pure negation and alteration, and between disregarding something and negating its existence. By mastering these distinctions, you will see how logic protects theology from false conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the two exchanges: first an objection and answer, then a second objection and answer.
The passage uses technical terms 'salb' and 'ʿudūl'.
2
Identify the first objection and response
The opponent says if blackness is not blackness, that's impossible. The author splits into two meanings. Learn what 'salb' and 'ʿudūl' mean in this context.
Negation (salb) simply denies the property; alteration (ʿudūl) attributes the opposite.
3
Analyze the second objection and response
The opponent argues that every thing has an essence independent of other things. The author replies that disregarding something does not negate its existence.
Consider how 'qaṭʿ al-naẓar' (disregarding) is different from actual non-existence.
4
Connect to larger theological issues
Ask yourself why this matters for theology. How does the author's distinction preserve the principle that non-existent attributes do not cause impossibility?
This reasoning is used in arguments about divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فلا يكون السواد سواداً وهو محال . قلنا : بمعنى السلب فيمتنع الاستحالة، أو بمعنى العدول بأن ماهية السواد مع كونها لا سواداً فتمتنع الملازمة. فإن قيل : السواد سواد، وإن لم يوجد الغير ضرورة أن لكل شيء ماهية هو بها مع قطع النظر عن كل ما عداه قلنا قطع النظر عن الشيء لا يوجب انتفاءه .
If it is said: 'Then blackness would not be blackness, and that is impossible.' We say: In the sense of negation (salb), then impossibility does not occur; or in the sense of alteration (ʿudūl), in that the essence (māhiyya) of blackness while being not-blackness, then the entailment is impossible. If it is said: 'Blackness is blackness, even if the other does not exist, necessarily because every thing has an essence by which it is, disregarding everything else.' We say: Disregarding a thing does not necessitate its non-existence.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange regarding the status of non-existent blackness. The opponent first objects that if blackness loses its blackness, it would be impossible for it to be blackness. The author responds by clarifying two possible meanings of 'blackness is not blackness': either it is pure negation (salb), in which case no impossibility arises because the property is simply absent; or it is alteration (ʿudūl), meaning the essence of blackness is considered as having the attribute of non-blackness, which also does not lead to a logical impossibility. The opponent then raises a second objection: everything has an essence independent of other things, so blackness must be blackness even when non-existent. The author counters that disregarding (qaṭʿ al-naẓar) something does not negate its existence—i.e., mental abstraction does not eliminate external reality. The author thus defends the view that non-existent things do not retain their properties in external existence.

Key Terms
السواد
السلب
العدول
الماهية
قطع النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that if blackness is not blackness when non-existent, then it is impossible for it to be blackness at all.
Correction: The author shows that 'not blackness' can mean pure negation (the property is simply absent) which does not lead to impossibility, or alteration (the essence takes on the opposite attribute) which also does not lead to impossibility because the entailment is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the objection that if blackness loses its blackness it would be impossible?
A) He argues that blackness cannot lose its blackness.
B) He distinguishes between negation and alteration.
C) He concedes the objection.
D) He denies the existence of blackness.

Key Takeaway
The author uses the distinction between negation (salb) and alteration (ʿudūl) to refute the claim that non-existent blackness must remain blackness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you mentally set aside a difficult aspect of your faith (like a troubling prohibition or a complex doctrine), do you sometimes begin to treat that abstraction as if the difficulty is truly gone? How might the author’s warning—that 'disregarding does not necessitate non-existence'—expose a hidden escape you rely on? What would it cost you to honestly confront that reality instead?
?
Consider a belief you hold about your own nature—perhaps that deep down you are fundamentally 'good' or 'worthy' regardless of your actions. Is this belief based on your essence (māhiyya) or on a convenient disregard of evidence? Would you still cling to it if you applied the author's same logical precision? What would you have to change if you didn’t?
?
The opponent assumed that blackness must remain blackness even when non-existent. Have you ever assumed that your identity or character remains unchanged even when the relevant qualities are absent? What would it mean for you to accept that some of your 'blacknesses' might simply be negated or altered, rather than eternally fixed?

Argument from Distinction and Its Refutation
First argument from distinction and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from distinction for the external stability of non-existent possible things is constructed and refuted.

Core Idea
Mental distinction does not entail external existence.

Proponents of the external stability of non-existent possible things argue that such things are distinct in the intellect, and everything distinct must have external stability. But this confuses two kinds of distinction. Let us examine their reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the proponents' first argument
The argument is based on distinction: non-existent possibles are known, willed, etc., and distinction necessitates external stability via mental indication.
2
Analyse the minor premise
Notice that distinction is asserted from being known, willed, and capable. This is a mental distinction.
The minor premise: it is distinct.
3
Analyse the major premise
The major premise is that every distinct thing is fixed because indication points to a referent. This is the key claim.
Major premise: distinction requires external stability.
4
Understand the refutation
The author distinguishes: if the distinction is mental, it only proves mental stability; if external, it's denied. The argument therefore fails.
5
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this distinction prevents conflating mental concepts with external realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن المعدوم الممكن ثابت في الخارج تمسكوا بوجوه : الأول : أنه متميز وكل متميز ثابت أما الصغرى فلأنه قد يكون معلوماً فيتميز عن غير المعلوم، ومراداً فيتميز عن غير المراد، ومقدوراً فيتميز عن غير المقدور. وأما الكبرى فلأن التميز عند العقل لا يتصور إلا بالإشارة العقلية بهذا وذاك، والإشارة تقتضي ثبوت المشار إليه ضرورة امتناع الإشارة إلى النفي الصرف . الثاني : أنه ممكن، وكل ممكن ثابت لأن الإمكان وصف ثبوتي على ما سيأتي، فيكون الموصوف به ثابتاً بالضرورة. والجواب عن الأول : أنه إن أريد أن التميز يقتضي الثبوت في الخارج فممنوع، وإنما يلزم لو كان التميز بحسب الخارج وإن أريد في الذهن فلا يفيد . وعن الثاني : أنا لا نسلم كون الإمكان ثبوتياً بمعنى كونه ثابتاً في الخارج، بل هو اعتبار عقلي يكفي ثبوت الموصوف به في العقل، ثم لا خفاء أن الممتنعات كشريك الباري، واجتماع النقيضين وكون الجسم في آن واحد في حيزين بعضها متميز عن البعض وعن الأمور الموجودة مع أنها منتفية قطعاً، وإن مثل جبل من الياقوت وبحر من الزئبق من المركبات الخيالية، متميز مع أنها غير ثابتة، وفاقاً، فيورد بالأول معارضة أو نقض على الوجه الأولى، وبالتالي على الوجهين وقد يورد النقض بالأحوال من الوجود لما وغيره، فإنها مع تميزها ليست بثابتة في العدم، إذ لا عدم لها ولا وجود أن الحال صفة للموجود لا موجودة ولا معدومة، وفيه نظر لأن قاعدة الخصم ليست سوى أن كل متميز ثابت في الخارج، فإن كان موجوداً، ففي الوجود، أو معدوماً ففي العدم، أو لا موجوداً ولا معدوماً ففي تلك الحال، والوجود وغيره من الأحوال ليس لها حالة العدم أصلاً فمن أين يلزم ثبوتها في العدم .
Those who hold that the non-existent possible (al-maʿdūm al-mumkin) is fixed in external existence have adhered to arguments: The first: that it is distinct (mutamayyiz), and every distinct thing is fixed. As for the minor premise, because it may be known (maʿlūm) and thus distinct from the unknown, and willed (murād) and thus distinct from the unwilled, and capable (maqdūr) and thus distinct from the incapable. As for the major premise, because distinction in the intellect is not conceived except by mental indication (al-ishāra al-ʿaqliyya) of 'this' and 'that', and indication necessitates the stability (thubūt) of the indicated thing necessarily because indication towards pure negation is impossible. The second: that it is possible (mumkin), and every possible thing is fixed, because possibility (imkān) is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī) as will come, so that which is described by it is necessarily fixed. The answer to the first: if it is meant that distinction necessitates stability in external existence, it is denied; rather it would only follow if the distinction were according to external [existence], and if it is meant in the mind (dhihn) it is of no avail. And as for the second: we do not grant that possibility is positive in the sense of being fixed in external existence; rather it is a mental consideration (iʿtibār ʿaqlī) and it suffices that the thing described by it is fixed in the mind. Then it is not hidden that impossibilities (al-mumtaniʿāt) like the partner of God (sharīk al-bārī), the conjunction of opposites, and the body being in one moment in two places, some of them are distinct from some and from existing things while they are absolutely non-existent; and likewise, a mountain of ruby and a sea of mercury from imaginary compounds are distinct while they are not fixed, by agreement. So the first [argument] is opposed by counterexample (muʿāraḍa) or refutation (naqḍ) in the first way, and thus in both ways. And a refutation may be adduced from the states (al-aḥwāl) of existence and non-existence and others, for they, with their distinctness, are not fixed in non-existence, since they have neither non-existence nor existence — that the state (ḥāl) is an attribute of the existent, neither existent nor non-existent. And there is consideration in this because the opponent's principle is only that every distinct thing is fixed in external existence: if it is existent, then in existence; if non-existent, then in non-existence; if neither existent nor non-existent, then in that state. But existence and other states have no state of non-existence at all, so how does their stability in non-existence follow?
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from proponents who claim that a non-existent possible thing (al-maʿdūm al-mumkin) has fixed existence (thubūt) in the external world, followed by the author's refutations. The first argument is based on distinction (tamayyuz): the non-existent possible is distinct in the mind (as known, willed, etc.), and everything distinct must be fixed in external existence because mental indication points to a stable referent. The author refutes this by distinguishing between mental and external distinction: if the proponent means external distinction, it is denied; if mental, it does not prove external stability. The second argument appeals to possibility (imkān) as a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī) that requires its subject to be fixed. The author denies that imkān is positive in an external sense; it is merely a mental consideration (iʿtibār ʿaqlī) that requires only mental stability. The author then provides additional counterexamples: impossible entities (like a partner of God) and imaginary composites (like a mountain of ruby) are distinct but not externally fixed. Finally, the author discusses 'states' (aḥwāl) — attributes of existents that are neither existent nor non-existent — and argues that the opponent's own principle, if applied consistently, does not force them to be fixed in non-existence because they have no state of non-existence.

Key Terms
المعدوم الممكن
التميز
الإشارة العقلية
الثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distinction in the mind necessarily implies external existence.
Correction: Mental distinction only implies mental stability; external existence requires additional proof.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise of the first argument?
A) Non-existent possibles are known.
B) Every distinct thing is fixed.
C) Mental indication is impossible for negations.
D) Everything possible is fixed.

Key Takeaway
Mental distinction alone does not prove external existence; the refutation hinges on distinguishing between mental and external distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you assumed something was true just because you could clearly imagine it. How does this lesson challenge that habit? Will you examine your mental assumptions before accepting them as reality?
?
In your study of aqeedah, how often do you distinguish between what exists in your mind and what exists in external reality? What practical step can you take today to apply this distinction when learning about unseen matters?

Argument from Possibility and Counterexamples
Second argument from possibility and counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for external stability based on possibility and how the author refutes it with counterexamples.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental consideration, not an external attribute.

The second argument claims that possibility is a positive attribute requiring its subject to be externally fixed. The author counters that possibility is merely a mental consideration and provides powerful counterexamples from impossibilities and imaginary entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
The proponents argue that every possible thing is fixed because possibility is a positive attribute.
2
Understand the refutation
The author denies that possibility is positive in an external sense; it is a mental consideration.
3
Examine the counterexamples
Impossibilities and imaginary compounds are distinct but not externally fixed, providing a counterexample to the claim that distinctness implies external stability.
4
Consider the argument from states
The author addresses a potential objection from 'states' (aḥwāl), showing that the opponent's principle does not force stability in non-existence.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
This lesson shows that neither distinction nor possibility proves external stability; only mental stability is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن المعدوم الممكن ثابت في الخارج تمسكوا بوجوه : الأول : أنه متميز وكل متميز ثابت أما الصغرى فلأنه قد يكون معلوماً فيتميز عن غير المعلوم، ومراداً فيتميز عن غير المراد، ومقدوراً فيتميز عن غير المقدور. وأما الكبرى فلأن التميز عند العقل لا يتصور إلا بالإشارة العقلية بهذا وذاك، والإشارة تقتضي ثبوت المشار إليه ضرورة امتناع الإشارة إلى النفي الصرف . الثاني : أنه ممكن، وكل ممكن ثابت لأن الإمكان وصف ثبوتي على ما سيأتي، فيكون الموصوف به ثابتاً بالضرورة. والجواب عن الأول : أنه إن أريد أن التميز يقتضي الثبوت في الخارج فممنوع، وإنما يلزم لو كان التميز بحسب الخارج وإن أريد في الذهن فلا يفيد . وعن الثاني : أنا لا نسلم كون الإمكان ثبوتياً بمعنى كونه ثابتاً في الخارج، بل هو اعتبار عقلي يكفي ثبوت الموصوف به في العقل، ثم لا خفاء أن الممتنعات كشريك الباري، واجتماع النقيضين وكون الجسم في آن واحد في حيزين بعضها متميز عن البعض وعن الأمور الموجودة مع أنها منتفية قطعاً، وإن مثل جبل من الياقوت وبحر من الزئبق من المركبات الخيالية، متميز مع أنها غير ثابتة، وفاقاً، فيورد بالأول معارضة أو نقض على الوجه الأولى، وبالتالي على الوجهين وقد يورد النقض بالأحوال من الوجود لما وغيره، فإنها مع تميزها ليست بثابتة في العدم، إذ لا عدم لها ولا وجود أن الحال صفة للموجود لا موجودة ولا معدومة، وفيه نظر لأن قاعدة الخصم ليست سوى أن كل متميز ثابت في الخارج، فإن كان موجوداً، ففي الوجود، أو معدوماً ففي العدم، أو لا موجوداً ولا معدوماً ففي تلك الحال، والوجود وغيره من الأحوال ليس لها حالة العدم أصلاً فمن أين يلزم ثبوتها في العدم .
Those who hold that the non-existent possible (al-maʿdūm al-mumkin) is fixed in external existence have adhered to arguments: The first: that it is distinct (mutamayyiz), and every distinct thing is fixed. The second: that it is possible (mumkin), and every possible thing is fixed, because possibility (imkān) is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī) as will come, so that which is described by it is necessarily fixed. The answer to the first: if it is meant that distinction necessitates stability in external existence, it is denied; rather it would only follow if the distinction were according to external [existence], and if it is meant in the mind (dhihn) it is of no avail. And as for the second: we do not grant that possibility is positive in the sense of being fixed in external existence; rather it is a mental consideration (iʿtibār ʿaqlī) and it suffices that the thing described by it is fixed in the mind. Then it is not hidden that impossibilities (al-mumtaniʿāt) like the partner of God (sharīk al-bārī), the conjunction of opposites, and the body being in one moment in two places, some of them are distinct from some and from existing things while they are absolutely non-existent; and likewise, a mountain of ruby and a sea of mercury from imaginary compounds are distinct while they are not fixed, by agreement. Commentary

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that possibility is a positive attribute requiring external stability?
A) By denying that possibility exists.
B) By stating possibility is a mental consideration only.
C) By proving possibility is impossible.
D) By appealing to scripture.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a mental consideration; external stability is not required for mental concepts.

The Third Argument: Non-Existent Blackness and Its Refutation
Proponents argue non-existent blackness is blackness in itself; author refutes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the argument that non-existent blackness is blackness in itself, and how the author denies the entailment that removing the cause of blackness would remove its blackness without removing its existence.

Core Idea
A thing's essence is tied to its cause; when the cause is removed, both cease.

In this passage, the proponents of fixed essences in non-existence argue that non-existent blackness is blackness in itself, independent of any cause. The author refutes this by denying that the entailment of the argument holds; instead, the cause that gives blackness its property also gives it existence, so when the cause is absent, both are absent. This lesson unpacks the argument and its refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: claim, proof, refutation, objection, response.
The Arabic ends mid-sentence but the meaning is complete from the translation.
2
Identify the Proponent's Claim
Find the proponents' assertion that non-existent blackness is blackness in itself. Note how they reason: if its blackness were by another, removing that other would make it lose blackness, which they consider absurd.
This is the core of their argument.
3
Understand the Author's Refutation
The author does not grant the entailment. He argues that if the cause of blackness is also the cause of existence, removing the cause removes both blackness and existence, so no paradox arises.
This is the key move.
4
Examine the Objection and Final Response
The opponent objects that if blackness loses its blackness, it would not be blackness—a self-evident falsehood. The author responds that this only entails a negation (that non-existent blackness is not blackness), which is not a problem.
This clarifies the logical point.
5
Reflect on the Misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding: that the essence of a thing remains unchanged regardless of its cause. The author shows that the cause is essential to both the property and existence.
This reinforces the lesson's core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن معنى كون المعدوم الممكن ثابتاً في الخارج، أن السواد المعدوم مثلاً سواد في نفسه، سواء وجد الغير أو لم يوجد، وبيانه ظاهر، لأنه لو كان كونه سواداً بالغير لزم ارتفاع كون السواد سواداً عند ارتفاع الغير واللازم باطل. لأنه يستلزم أن لا يبقى السواد الموجود سواداً عند ارتفاع ذلك الغير، الذي هو الموجب لكونه سواداً وهو محال . والجواب : أنا لا نسلم استلزامه لذلك، وإنما يلزم لو كان وجود السواد باقياً عند ارتفاع موجب السوادية وهو ممنوع، لم لا يجوز أن يكون ارتفاع ذلك الغير كما يوجب ارتفاع سواديته، يوجب ارتفاع وجوده لكونه العلة للوجود أو لازمها . فإن قيل : لو ارتفع عند ارتفاع ذلك الغير سوادية السواد لزم أن لا يكون السواد سوادا، وهو بديهي . قلنا : إن أريد به أنه يلزم السلب أي ليس السواد المعدوم سواداً، فلا
The third: that the meaning of the non-existent possible being fixed in external existence is that non-existent blackness, for example, is blackness in itself, whether the other exists or not. Its clarification is apparent, because if its being blackness were by another, then when that other is removed, the blackness's being blackness would cease, and the consequent is false. Because it entails that existing blackness would not remain blackness when that other which is the cause of its being blackness is removed, and that is impossible. The answer: we do not grant its entailment; rather it would only follow if the existence of blackness remained when the cause of blackness is removed, which is denied. Why is it not permissible that the removal of that other, just as it causes the removal of its blackness, also causes the removal of its existence, because it is the cause of existence or its concomitant? If it is said: 'If the blackness of blackness is removed with the removal of that other, then blackness would not be blackness, and that is self-evident.' We say: If it is meant by that that negation (salb) follows, i.e., that non-existent blackness is not blackness, then no [impossibility follows].
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument by proponents that a non-existent possible thing is fixed in external existence. Specifically, they argue that non-existent blackness is blackness in itself (fī nafsihi) regardless of whether its cause exists. If its blackness were dependent on another, then when that other ceases, the blackness would cease to be blackness, which is absurd because even existing blackness would lose its blackness if the cause is removed—an impossibility. The author refutes by denying the entailment: it would only follow if the existence of blackness remained after the cause of blackness is removed, but the opponent does not grant that. Instead, the removal of the cause could remove both the blackness and the existence of blackness, since the cause is the cause of existence or its concomitant. The opponent then objects that if blackness loses its blackness, it would not be blackness, which is self-evident. The author responds that this only entails a negation (salb)—that non-existent blackness is not blackness—which is not a problem, as it does not imply alteration. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is complete in intent.

Key Terms
السواد المعدوم
السوادية
العلة
لازم
السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that the essence of a thing remains unchanged regardless of the existence of its cause.
Correction: The author argues that the cause of a property (blackness) may also be the cause of existence; when the cause is removed, both the property and the existence cease, so no stable essence remains to be considered.

Checkpoint
What is the author's objection to the claim that non-existent blackness is blackness in itself?
A) That it contradicts the definition of blackness.
B) That the cause of blackness also causes existence, so when the cause is absent, both blackness and existence are absent.
C) That blackness is not a real property.
D) That non-existent things cannot have properties.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that the cause of a property is also the cause of existence, so removal of the cause removes both, preventing the absurdity posed by the proponents.

The Two Proofs for the Existence of al-Hal
Presents two proofs for al-hal and their rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments that proponents of al-hal use to establish an intermediate state between existence and non-existence, and the standard immediate responses to these arguments.

Core Idea
Al-hal is argued to be necessary for concepts like existence and universals that cannot be classified as existent or non-existent.

In kalam, the concept of al-hal (the state) is proposed to account for entities that are neither fully existent nor non-existent. The proponents of al-hal present two logical proofs: one concerning existence itself, and another concerning universals. This lesson introduces these foundational proofs and the initial refutations offered by opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the two proofs
Identify the structure of each proof: they argue that a certain concept (existence or universal) cannot be existent (to avoid regress) nor non-existent (to avoid contradiction), therefore it must be an intermediate state.
Notice the phrase 'وإلا تسلسل' indicating infinite regress.
2
Examine the responses
The author responds to each proof with a choice: either the concept is existent (self-identical) or non-existent (and the contradiction is avoided by specifying the nature of the opposite).
The responses use the method of 'takhayyur' (choosing one horn).
3
Understand the second proof's response
The second proof involves universals; the response is that composition is mental, not external, so no real problem arises.
The key phrase: 'إنما التمايز في الذهن'.
4
Reflect on the common misconception
Many think al-hal is a separate entity, but it is a conceptual device. This passage clarifies its logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المثبتون للحال بوجوده: الأول: أن الوجود ليس بموجود وإلا تسلسل، ولا معدوم وإلا اتصف بنقيضه، والقول بأنه لا يرد عليه القسمة اعتراف بالواسطة. قلنا: موجود ووجوده عينه، أو معدوم وإنما يلزم الاتصاف بالنقيض لو كان الوجود عدما أو الموجد معدوما. الثاني: الكلي ليس بموجود وإلا لكان مشخصا ولا معدوم وإلا لما كان جزءا للموجود، وكذلك حال كل جنس أو فصل مع نوعه، على أنه لو وجد يلزم في الأعراض قيام العرض بالعرض. قلنا: لا تركيب في الخارج، إذ ليس هنا شيء هو إنسان وآخر خصوصية زيد، ولا في السواد شيء هو لون وآخر قابض للبصر، وآخر مركب منهما يقوم واحد منهما بآخر، على أن مثل هذا القيام ليس من قيام العرض بالمحل في شيء، وإنما التمايز في الذهن، فثبت فيه الكلي والجنس. لكان له وجود وتسلسل ولا معدوم وإلا لا تصف بنقيضه، أي بما يصدق على نقيضه وذلك لأن العدم على تقدير الواسطة ليس نقيضا للوجود، بل أخص منه، وإنما نقيضه اللاوجود. وأجاب صاحب التجريد بأن الوجود لا يرد عليه القسمة إلى الموجود والمعدوم، فلا يكون أحدهما، ولا يخفى ما فيه من تسليم المدعي والاعتراف بالواسطة. فإن قيل: الواسطة يجب أن تكون قسما من الثابت، والوجود ليس بثابت كما أنه ليس بمنفي، وإنما هو ثبوت، وهذا كما أن كلا من الثبوت والنفي ليس ثابتا ولا منفيا ولم يلزم من ذلك كونه واسطة بينهما. قلنا: العذر أشد من الجزم، لأن ما ذكرنا قول بالواسطة بين الثابت والمنفي بارتفاع النقيضين. وأجاب الإمام: بأنا نختار أن الوجود موجود، ووجوده عينه لا زائد ليلزم تسلسل الوجودات فامتيازه عن سائر الموجودات يكون بقيد سلبي، هو أن لا ماهية له وراء الوجود. وقد يجاب بأنا نختار أنه معدوم واتصاف الشيء بنقيضه إنما يمتنع بطريق المواطأة مثل أن الموجود عدم، والموجود معدوم وإما بطريق الاشتقاق مثل أن الوجود ذو عدم، فلا نسلم استحالته، فإنه بمنزلة اللاوجود هو العدم ويرادفه لفظ (ليس) وهو العدم أو المعدوم بخلاف (أيس) فهو يدل على الوجود أو الموجود.
The affirmers of the state (al-hal) argued for its existence: First: that existence (al-wujud) is not existent (mawjud), otherwise there would be infinite regress, nor is it non-existent (ma'dum), otherwise it would be qualified by its opposite. And the claim that the division does not apply to it is an admission of the intermediate (al-wasita). We said: It is existent and its existence is identical to it, or it is non-existent, and qualification by the opposite would only follow if existence were non-existence or the existent were non-existent. Second: The universal (al-kulli) is not existent, otherwise it would be individuated, nor non-existent, otherwise it would not be a part of the existent. Likewise the case of every genus (jins) or differentia (fasl) with its species (naw'), and if it existed, it would necessitate in accidents the subsistence of an accident by an accident. We said: There is no composition in the external world, for there is not here something that is 'human' and another that is 'Zayd's particularity', nor in blackness is there something that is 'color' and another that is 'contracting of the pupil', and another composite of them where one subsists by the other. Moreover, such subsistence is not at all like the subsistence of an accident in a substrate, rather differentiation is in the mind, so the universal and genus are established in it. It would have existence and infinite regress, nor non-existent, otherwise it would not be described by its opposite, i.e., by what is true of its opposite, because non-existence (al-'adam) on the assumption of the intermediate is not the opposite of existence, but more specific, rather its opposite is non-existence (al-la-wujud). And the author of al-Tajrid answered: that existence is not subject to the division into existent and non-existent, so it is neither. And it is not hidden what is in this of conceding the claim and admitting the intermediate. If it is said: The intermediate must be a part of what is established (al-thabit), and existence is not established just as it is not negated, rather it is establishment (thubut), just as both establishment and negation are neither established nor negated, and that does not necessitate it being an intermediate between them. We said: The excuse is worse than the sin, because what we mentioned is a statement of an intermediate between the established and the negated by the removal of the two opposites. And the Imam answered: We choose that existence is existent, and its existence is identical to it, not additional, so there is no infinite regress of existences, and its distinction from other existents is by a negative condition, that it has no quiddity (mahiyya) beyond existence. And it may be answered: We choose that it is non-existent, and the qualification of a thing by its opposite is only impossible in the way of agreement (al-muwata'a), such as 'the existent is non-existence' and 'the existent is non-existent', or in the way of derivation (al-ishtiqaq), such as 'existence is possessed of non-existence', so we do not concede its impossibility, for it is like non-existence (al-la-wujud), which is non-existence, and the word (laysa) is synonymous with it, meaning non-existence or non-existent, unlike (aysa), which indicates existence or existent.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the existence of 'al-hal' (the state), a concept in kalam that is an intermediate between existence and non-existence. The author first states that the proponents of al-hal argue for it based on two proofs.

The first proof: Existence (al-wujud) cannot be existent, otherwise there would be infinite regress, nor can it be non-existent, otherwise it would be qualified by its opposite (non-existence). Therefore, existence must be an intermediate state. The author responds (qulna) with a choice: either existence is existent and identical to its existence, or it is non-existent, and the objection about being qualified by its opposite only holds if existence were the same as non-existence.

The second proof: The universal (al-kulli) cannot be existent, otherwise it would be individuated (which universals are not), nor non-existent, otherwise it could not be part of existent things. Similarly for genus and species. If the universal existed, it would require accidents subsisting in accidents. The author responds that there is no real composition in the external world; these distinctions (e.g., human and Zayd's particularity) are mental, not external. Hence the universal and genus are only mental.

Then the text continues with an additional objection (apparently from a critic) that if the universal or existence were intermediates, they would have existence and cause infinite regress, and they cannot be non-existent because they would then be described by their opposite. The author clarifies that non-existence on the assumption of an intermediate is not the opposite of existence but more specific; the true opposite is 'non-existence' (al-la-wujud).

The author of al-Tajrid (Nasir al-Din al-Tusi) answers that existence is not subject to the division into existent and non-existent, so it is neither. But the author notes that this is a concession of the claim and admission of an intermediate.

Another objection is raised: the intermediate must be a part of what is established (thabit), but existence is not established just as it is not negated; it is establishment itself. Just as establishment and negation are neither established nor negated, so existence need not be an intermediate. The author responds that this excuse is worse because it affirms an intermediate between established and negated by removing both opposites.

The Imam (likely al-Ghazali or Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) then gives two possible answers: first, we choose that existence is existent, and its existence is identical to it, so no infinite regress; its distinction from other existents is by a negative condition (no quiddity). Second, we choose that it is non-existent, and the impossibility of being qualified by its opposite applies only in cases of agreement (e.g., 'the existent is non-existence') or derivation (e.g., 'existence is possessed of non-existence'), not here. So the impossibility is not conceded, as it is like the word 'laysa' meaning non-existence, unlike 'aysa' meaning existence.

Key Terms
وجود (al-wujud)
عدم (al-'adam)
واسطة (al-wasita)
كلي (al-kulli)
جنس (jins)
فصل (fasl)
عرض ('arad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-hal is an actually existing entity separate from both existence and non-existence.
Correction: Al-hal is a conceptual intermediary, not a real ontological category. The proofs show that it is a logical necessity for certain concepts, but the responses argue that those concepts can be understood without a third ontological status.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof for al-hal, why can't existence be existent?
A) Because it would cause infinite regress.
B) Because existence is not a thing.
C) Because existence is necessarily non-existent.
D) Because the proponents of al-hal say so.

Key Takeaway
The concept of al-hal arises from the apparent impossibility of classifying certain fundamental concepts (like existence and universals) as either existent or non-existent, but the standard responses show that these concepts can be explained without positing a third state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that existence itself cannot be reduced to a simple binary. You have likely done the same with your faith—treating iman as simply present or absent. When in your life have you ignored the subtle 'state' of your heart because it was easier to think in extremes? What will you do this week to examine your iman beyond binaries?
?
The rebuttal explains that mental composition (like universal and particular) does not reflect external reality. Consider the mental constructs you have about yourself: which of them is preventing you from seeing your true state before Allah? What concrete step will you take today to align your self-perception with reality?

Refutations of the Proofs for al-Hal
Continues the dialectical exchange with further objections and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the advanced dialectical responses to the proofs for al-hal, including the objection from infinite regress, al-Tajrid's response, and the Imam's two answers.

Core Idea
The existence of an intermediate state is contested through logical analysis that shows the division into existent and non-existent does not apply to existence itself.

After the initial proofs and responses, the debate deepens. Opponents raise further objections, and scholars like al-Tajrid and the Imam offer refined arguments. This lesson examines these higher-level dialectics, including the nature of opposites and the concept of 'thubut' (establishment).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about infinite regress
The opponent argues that if existence or universal were an intermediate, it would still cause problems. Identify the specific charge.
The text says: 'لكان له وجود وتسلسل'.
2
Understand al-Tajrid's answer
Al-Tajrid claims existence is not subject to the division into existent and non-existent. But the author notes this concedes the point.
Notice the phrase 'ولا يخفى ما فيه من تسليم المدعي'.
3
Analyze the objection about the intermediate
The opponent argues that existence is not 'thabit' (established) but rather thubut itself, so it cannot be an intermediate. The author responds that this still affirms an intermediate.
The key: 'العذر أشد من الجزم'.
4
Examine the Imam's two answers
The Imam presents two coherent answers: either existence is existent (self-identical) with a negative condition, or it is non-existent (and the impossibility of being qualified by its opposite is not absolute).
The second answer uses the distinction between 'muwata'a' and 'ishtiqaq'.
