The Contingent Necessarily Needs a Cause
The author defines contingent and establishes the necessity of its needing a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the judgment that a contingent thing needs a cause is considered rationally necessary and self-evident.

Core Idea
A contingent thing cannot exist or not exist without a cause – this is a rational necessity.

Before we can prove the existence of God, we must first establish a foundational principle: every contingent thing requires an efficient cause for its existence. In this lesson, we examine why the majority of theologians consider this judgment to be necessary and self-evident, requiring no proof once the meanings of 'contingent' and 'need' are clarified.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own progression come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author first states the necessity, then lists properties, then gives the majority view.
2
Identify the definitions
The passage defines both 'contingent' and 'need'. Find these definitions and note how they relate to each other.
Pay attention to the phrase: 'the meaning of the contingent is that its essence does not necessitate its existence nor its non-existence'.
3
Understand the necessity claim
The author says the majority hold this judgment to be necessary. Ask yourself: why is it considered necessary without proof?
The key is that after clarifying the terms, the judgment becomes self-evident.
4
Reflect on conceptual clarity
The author mentions that obscurity of assent due to obscurity of conception is not detrimental. Think about why conceptual clarity is important in theology.
This caveat prepares for the objection about different opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس
احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر
قال : المبحث الخامس : الضرورة قاضية باحتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر ، وامتناع ترجح أحد طرفيه بلا مرجح. وخفاء التصديق بخفاء التصور غير قادح .
من خواص الممكن : أنه يحتاج في وجوده وعـدمـه إلى سبب، وأنه لا أحد طرفيه إلا لمرجح ، ولتلازم هذين المعنيين، بل لتقارب مفهوميهما جداً قد يجعل الثاني تفسيراً للأول، والجمهور على أن هذا الحكم ضروري بعد تلخيص معنى الموضوع والمحمول، من غير أن يفتقر إلى برهان. فإن معنى الممكن ما لا يقتضي ذاته وجوده ولا عـدمـه ومعنى الاحتياج أن كلاً من وجوده وعدمه، يكون لا لذاته، بل لأمر خارج .
فإن قيل : يحتمل أن لا يكون لذاته ولا لأمر خارج بل لمجرد الاتفاق . قلنا هذا مما يظهر بطلانه بأدنى التفات، ولهذا يحكم به من لا يتأتى منه النظر والاستدلال، ثم اختلاف البعض في نفس الحكم أو في بداهته، والتفاوات بينه وبين قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين لا ينافي البداهة على ما سبق، وأما ماذهب إليه الكثيرون من أن الله تعالى خلق العالم في وقت دون سائر الأوقات من غير مرجح ، وخصص أفعال المكلفين بأحكام مخصوصة من غير أن يكون فيها ما يقتضي ذلك، وأن قدرة القادر قد تتعلق بالفعل أو الترك من غير مرجح ، فليس من ترجح الممكن بلا مرجح، بل من ترجح المختار أحد المتساويين من غير مرجح ، ونحن لا نقول بامتناعه فضلاً عن أن يكون ضرورياً، وإلى هذا يستند عندنا اختلاف حركات الكواكب، ومواضعها، وأوضاعها.
وأما الفلاسفة القائلون بالإيجاب دون الاختيار فلا يلتزمون وقوع تلك الاختلافات والاختصاصات بلا سبب، بل يعترفون باستنادها إلى أسباب فاعلية، لا اطلاع على تفاصيلها. ففي الجملة لم يقل أحد ممن يعتد به بوقوع الممكن بلا سبب .
The fifth section: The need of the contingent for the efficient cause. He said: The fifth section: Necessity judges the need of the contingent for the efficient cause, and the impossibility of the preponderance of one of its two sides without a preponderator. And the obscurity of assent due to the obscurity of conception is not detrimental. Among the properties of the contingent: that it needs in its existence and non-existence a cause, and that neither of its two sides occurs except by a preponderator. And due to the inseparability of these two meanings, rather due to the close proximity of their concepts, the second may be made an interpretation of the first. And the majority hold that this judgment is necessary after summarizing the meaning of the subject and predicate, without needing a proof. For the meaning of the contingent is that its essence does not necessitate its existence nor its non-existence, and the meaning of need is that each of its existence and non-existence is not due to itself but due to an external matter. For if it is said: It is possible that it is neither due to itself nor due to an external matter, but merely by chance. We say: This is something whose falsity is apparent with the slightest attention, and therefore one who is not able to engage in reflection and inference judges it. Then the disagreement of some concerning the judgment itself or its self-evidence, and the difference between it and our saying 'one is half of two' does not contradict self-evidence as previously. As for what many have held that God created the world at a time rather than other times without a preponderator, and specified the actions of the morally responsible with particular rulings without there being in them what necessitates that, and that the power of the capable may attach to action or inaction without a preponderator, that is not from the preponderance of the contingent without a preponderator, but from the preponderance of the free agent of one of two equals without a preponderator. And we do not say it is impossible, let alone necessary. And upon this is based with us the variation of the motions of celestial bodies, their positions, and their states. As for the philosophers who affirm necessitation without freedom, they do not commit to the occurrence of those variations and specifications without a cause, but rather they acknowledge that they are based on efficient causes, of whose details we have no knowledge. In summary, no one of those who are considered has said that the contingent occurs without a cause.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the necessity of the judgment that the contingent needs a cause. The author begins by stating that rational necessity judges that the contingent needs an efficient cause and that it is impossible for one of its two states (existence or non-existence) to preponderate without a preponderator. He notes that obscurity of assent due to conceptual obscurity does not undermine this necessity. Then he explains the properties of the contingent: it requires a cause for both existence and non-existence, and no state occurs without a preponderator. These two meanings are so close that the second may be considered an interpretation of the first. The majority consider this judgment necessary after clarifying the subject and predicate, without need for proof. The meaning of 'contingent' is that its essence does not necessitate either existence or non-existence; the meaning of 'need' is that both states come from an external cause, not from itself. He then anticipates an objection: perhaps there is no external cause but mere chance. He refutes this by saying its falsity is apparent with minimal reflection, and even those incapable of reasoning judge it. He addresses two potential challenges: differences of opinion about the judgment or its self-evidence, and the analogy to mathematical axioms. He explains that such differences do not negate self-evidence. He then distinguishes the preponderance of the contingent without a cause from the free choice among equals without a cause, affirming that the latter is not impossible. He further notes that even philosophers who deny freedom acknowledge that variations and specifications have efficient causes. Thus, no one of consequence claims that a contingent occurs without a cause.

Key Terms
الممكن
المؤثر
الضرورة
الاحتياج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The judgment that a contingent needs a cause requires a logical proof and is not self-evident.
Correction: The author states that the majority hold this judgment to be necessary after clarifying the meanings, without needing a proof. It is rationally self-evident once the terms are understood.

Checkpoint
What do the majority of theologians believe about the judgment that the contingent needs a cause?
A) It requires a logical proof from revelation.
B) It is necessary and self-evident after clarifying the meanings of subject and predicate.
C) It is only known through personal experience.
D) It is derived from philosophical speculation.

Key Takeaway
The judgment that a contingent needs a cause is a necessary, self-evident truth once the terms 'contingent' and 'need' are properly understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are contingent—your existence is not from yourself. If you were left alone, you would neither exist nor continue to exist. Does this truth cause you to turn to the One who gives you being every moment? How will you remind yourself of your dependency today?
?
The passage says obscurity of conception can obscure assent. What areas of your understanding about God and your own existence are you reluctant to think clearly about, perhaps because clarity would demand a response? Commit to studying one unclear concept this week.
?
If the contingent requires a cause, then your very existence points to a Necessary Being. When you look in the mirror, do you see a mark of dependency? How will you let this awareness shape your next act of worship?

Objections and Clarifications on Causality
The author refutes objections and clarifies the scope of the principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection of chance, defends the self-evidence of the necessity, and distinguishes between a contingent needing a cause and a free agent choosing among equals.

Core Idea
Even apparent disagreements and chance hypotheses fail to undermine the necessary truth that every contingent requires a cause.

Having established the foundational principle, the author now anticipates and answers objections. He dismisses the possibility that a contingent could happen by chance, addresses why differing opinions do not negate self-evidence, and draws a critical distinction: while a contingent cannot occur without a cause, a free agent (like God) can choose among equals without an external preponderator. This distinction is key to reconciling divine omnipotence with the principle of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the passage starting from 'For if it is said...' and see how the author immediately dismisses the chance hypothesis.
Notice the phrase 'its falsity is apparent with the slightest attention'.
2
Examine the response to disagreements
The author addresses that some disagree about the judgment or its self-evidence. How does he show that this does not invalidate the necessity?
He compares it to mathematical axioms where similar disagreements occur.
3
Understand the crucial distinction
The author distinguishes between the preponderance of a contingent without a cause and the free choice of a free agent. Find this key distinction and reflect on its importance.
This is a key theological point: God's free choice does not violate causality.
4
Consider the philosophers' view
The author mentions that even philosophers who deny free will acknowledge efficient causes for variations.
This shows that no one of consequence rejects causality altogether.

Critiquing a Weak Proof and Introducing Two Proofs
The author critiques a weak proof and introduces the two proofs used by those who consider the judgment theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why one common proof for the contingent's need for a cause is considered weak, and how scholars who view the judgment as theoretical have adduced two alternative proofs.

Core Idea
Not every proof for the contingent's need for a cause is valid; scholars distinguish weak from sound proofs.

The author begins by presenting a proof that attempts to show that a contingent event cannot occur without a preponderating factor. He then evaluates this proof as weak. This sets the stage for the two stronger proofs that are introduced—proofs used by those who consider the judgment of impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator to be acquired (theoretical) rather than self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the English translation to get a sense of the argument.
Notice that the passage is short and ends abruptly.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has two parts: (1) the weak proof and (2) the introduction of two proofs used by those who consider the judgment acquired.
The author explicitly states the weak proof is weak (ضعيف).
3
Understand the weak proof's structure
The weak proof is based on two premises: (a) that occurrence of one of two equals without a cause would necessitate preponderance, contradicting equality, and (b) that there must be a preponderator before existence, an existent attribute in the agent.
The author does not explain why it is weak; the commentary suggests it may be flawed.
4
Anticipate the two proofs
The passage ends with the claim that those who view the judgment as acquired have two proofs, but they are not elaborated here. This creates anticipation for the next lesson.
These two proofs are likely stronger than the weak one.

الاستدلال على احتياج الممكن إلى المؤثر
قال: والاستدلال بأن وقوع أحدهما بلا سبب يقتضي رجحانه فينافي التساوي، وبأنه لا بد من مرجح قبل الوجود، وهو وجودي يقوم بالمؤثر ضرورة تأخر الأثر (ضعيف).
القائلون بأن الحكم بامتناع الترجح بلا مرجح كسبي، استدلوا عليه بوجهين :
The proof for the need of the contingent for the efficient cause. He said: And the proof that the occurrence of one of them without a cause necessitates its preponderance, thus contradicting equality, and that there must be a preponderator before existence, which is an existent attribute subsisting in the agent due to the necessary delay of the effect (is weak). Those who say that the judgment of the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator is acquired have adduced two proofs for it:
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage presents the discussion about the proof for the contingent's need for a cause. The author first mentions a weak proof that is based on two premises: (1) if one of the two states occurs without a cause, that would imply its preponderance, which contradicts the supposed equality of the states; and (2) there must be a preponderator prior to existence, which is an existent attribute in the agent, because the effect necessarily comes after. The author marks this proof as weak. Then he transitions to those who consider the judgment itself to be acquired (theoretical) rather than necessary. They adduce two proofs to support their position. However, the text ends before those proofs are elaborated, only introducing them. This leaves the reader anticipating the details of the two proofs.

Correction: Actually, there are two stronger proofs that are introduced here (though not elaborated) used by those who view the judgment as acquired.

How much of your belief in Allah's existence is based on your own rational reflection versus blind acceptance? Mental Existence and Ignorance: Objection and Answer
Objection and answer about mental existence of causal relations leading to ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that mental concepts of relations are not inherently ignorance; ignorance only arises when the mind falsely judges external reality.

Core Idea
Mental concepts are valid unless the mind wrongly judges them as external.

A common objection against the mental existence of relations is that if something exists only in the mind and not externally, it leads to ignorance (jahl) because of lack of correspondence. Additionally, if causality qualities were real, they would exist before minds, so they cannot be mental. In this passage, the author answers by clarifying the precise condition for ignorance and by reframing what preexists minds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection has two parts: (1) if something is only in the mind without external reality, it is ignorance; (2) if causality qualities are real, they preexist minds, so they cannot be mental. Note the words 'جهلاً لانتفاء المطابقة' and 'صفة حاصلة قبل الأذهان'.
2
Identify the answer's key condition
The answer states that lack of correspondence is only ignorance when the mind judges the thing to be externally real while it is not ('إذا حكم العقل بالثبوت في الخارج ولم يثبت'). This is the crucial restriction.
The answer also explains what preexists minds: the disposition of the thing, not the mental concept itself.
3
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this answer preserves the validity of mental concepts about relations and causes, as long as we do not mistakenly attribute external existence to them. This resolves the objection without denying mental existence.
Notice that the author uses 'المؤثرية أو الحاجة' (effecting or need) as examples of relational concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يقال من أنه لو حصل في العقل دون الخارج كان جهلاً لانتفاء المطابقة وأن كلا منهما صفة حاصلة قبل الأذهان فيستحيل قيامها بالذهن فجوابه أن عدم المطابقة للخارج إنما يكون جهلاً إذا حكم العقل بالثبوت في الخارج ولم يثبت وأن الحاصل قبل الأذهان هو كون الشيء بحيث إذا تعقله الذهن حصل فيه معقول هو المؤثرية أو الحاجة
And what is said: That if it occurs in the mind without the external, it would be ignorance due to lack of correspondence, and that each of them is a quality existing before minds, so their subsistence in the mind is impossible. Its answer: That lack of correspondence with the external is only ignorance if the mind judges it to be established in the external while it is not established, and that what exists before minds is that the thing is such that when the mind conceives it, a concept occurs in it which is the effecting or the need.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that if effecting and need are merely mental, they would not correspond to external reality, causing ignorance. Also, if they were qualities existing before minds, they cannot subsist in the mind. The author answers that ignorance only follows when the mind judges something to be real externally but it is not. What exists before minds is the disposition (such that when the mind considers it, it forms the concepts of effecting or need). Thus, mental concepts do not require external reality as long as the judgment is not about external existence.

Key Terms
عقل (ʿaql)
خارج (khārij)
مطابقة (muṭābaqa)
جهل (jahl)
معقول (maʿqūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All mental concepts that lack external correspondence are acts of ignorance.
Correction: Ignorance only occurs when the mind judges a concept to be externally real when it is not. Mental concepts without such judgment are valid.

Checkpoint
When does lack of correspondence with the external become ignorance according to the author?
A) Whenever a concept occurs in the mind.
B) Only when the mind judges the thing to be external but it is not.
C) When the concept is relational.
D) When the concept is of non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Mental concepts of relations (like effecting and need) are valid as long as the mind does not falsely judge them to be external realities; ignorance only follows from a false judgment, not from mere mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are absolutely certain about a cause-and-effect relationship in your life (e.g., why a relationship failed or why you succeeded), have you ever paused to ask whether your certainty is based on a mental concept or a false judgment about external reality? Which of your deeply held beliefs might collapse if you honestly examined this distinction?
?
Think of a time you were convinced that something was true because it 'made sense in your mind,' only to later discover you were wrong. How does this lesson challenge you to separate the act of conceiving an idea from the act of judging it to be real? Will you now be more careful before declaring something as certain?
?
If ignorance is not in having ideas but in judging them to be externally true without evidence, what does that say about the ideas you are most defensive about? Are you ready to treat your most cherished mental concepts as concepts—not as external realities—until you have verified them?

Equality of the Possible's Two Sides in Essence
Al-Taftazani states the correct doctrine and clarifies 'priority'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the possible has no inherent priority for existence or non-existence, and how to interpret claims of priority.

Core Idea
The possible is neutral: neither existence nor non-existence is naturally preferred.

Al-Taftazani opens the Seventh Investigation with a clear assertion: the possible (al-mumkin) does not have an inherent priority (awlawiyya) for one of its two sides over the other. This is a foundational principle in kalam for understanding why the world needs a Creator, since if the possible had a natural inclination, it would either be necessary or its occurrence would need no external cause. In this passage, the author states the correct position, mentions two opposing views, and then clarifies what 'priority' could possibly mean.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the first sentence: 'لا أولوية لأحد طرفي الممكن نظراً إلى ذاته'. This is the thesis: no inherent priority.
The word 'ذات' means essence.
2
Identify the two other opinions
Notice the phrases 'وقيل بأولوية العدم مطلقاً' and 'وقيل في الأعراض السيالة'. These are alternative views.
'الأعراض السيالة' are flowing accidents like motion.
3
Understand the three interpretations
The author distinguishes three possible meanings of 'priority'. See how each is evaluated.
The third interpretation (disposition) is deemed possible but not inherent.
4
Connect to the big picture
Ask: Why is this important? Because if the possible had an inherent priority, it would undermine the need for a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع : لا أولوية لأحد طرفي الممكن نظراً إلى ذاته (۱) ، وقيل بأولوية العدم مطلقاً(۲) ، وقيل في الأعراض السيالة (۳)، والظاهر أنه إن أريد فلأولوية بحيث يستغني الوقوع عن سبب، فضروري البطلان، وإن أريد القرب إلى الوقوع لقلة الشروط والموانع ، وكثرة اتفاق الأسباب فعائد (٤) إلى الغير، وإن أريد اقتضاء ما للوجود أو العدم لا إلى حد الوجوب فمحتمل () والاستدلال على امتناعه بأنه إن امتنع مع تلك الأولوية، وقوع الطرف الآخر، وجب هذا، وإن أمكن فإما بلا سبب فيترجح المرجوح، أو بسبب فيتوقف هذا على عدمه فلا يكون أولى لذاته، وبأن اقتضاء التساوي ينافي اقتضاء أولوية ،أحدهما، وبأنه إن أمكن زوالها بسبب لم تكن ذاتية، بل متوقفة على عدمه، وإن لم يمكن كانت الماهية واجبة أو ممتنعة ضعيف لأن المتوقف على عدم ذلك السبب هو الوقوع لا الأولوية، ولأن (٦) عدم اقتضاء
The Seventh Investigation: There is no priority (awlawiyya) for one of the two sides of the possible (al-mumkin) in respect to its essence (dhāt). And it was said [that there is] priority of non-existence absolutely. And it was said [that there is priority] in the flowing accidents (al-aʿrāḍ al-sayyāla). And apparently, if by priority is meant [a priority] such that occurrence dispenses with a cause, then it is necessarily false. And if is meant nearness to occurrence due to fewness of conditions and obstacles and abundance of happening of causes, then it is referred to something else (i.e., not to the essence). And if is meant [a] disposition (iqtiḍāʾ) for existence or non-existence not to the extent of necessity, then it is possible. And the proof (al-istidlāl) against its being [i.e., against the existence of such an inherent priority] is: if with that priority the occurrence of the other side is impossible, then this side would be necessary; and if it is possible, then either without a cause, then the weaker would be preponderant, or by a cause, then it depends on the absence of that cause, so it would not be a priority in itself. And because the disposition for equality (musāwāt) contradicts the disposition for priority of one of them. And because if its removal by a cause were possible, then it would not be essential, but rather dependent on its absence, and if it were not possible, then the essence would be necessary or impossible. [All this] is weak, because what depends on the absence of that cause is the occurrence, not the priority. And because (the footnote): non-disposition
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage begins the Seventh Investigation, which concerns the equality of the two sides (existence and non-existence) of the possible with respect to its essence. The author states the correct doctrine: there is no inherent priority for either side. He then mentions two other opinions: one says non-existence has absolute priority; another says priority exists only in the case of flowing accidents (like motion, sound, time). Then the author clarifies what could be meant by 'priority'. If it means that with priority occurrence needs no cause, that is obviously false. If it means nearness to occurrence due to fewer conditions and obstacles, then this priority comes from external factors, not from the essence. If it means a disposition for existence or non-existence short of necessity, then it is possible (i.e., not absurd). Following this, the author presents an argument (istidlāl) against the possibility of inherent priority. The argument runs: if priority were inherent, then either the other side's occurrence is impossible (so this side becomes necessary), or it is possible—and then either it occurs without cause (so the weaker preponderates) or with a cause (so the priority is not essential). Also, the disposition for equality (which the possible has by definition) contradicts a disposition for priority. Also, if the priority could be removed by a cause, it is not essential; if it cannot be removed, the essence becomes necessary or impossible. The author then declares this proof weak (ḍaʿīf) because what depends on the absence of the cause is the actual occurrence, not the priority itself. The passage ends with the beginning of a second objection ('because') and the footnote content: 'Possibility does not contain a disposition for equality; rather it contains non-disposition for existence and non-existence, so the combination of opposites (equality and priority) does not follow.'

Key Terms
أولوية
الممكن
ذات
اقتضاء
مساواة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that possibility implies one side is naturally preferred (e.g., non-existence).
Correction: The author clarifies that any such priority is either impossible, external, or merely a disposition not from the essence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, which meaning of 'priority' (أولوية) is considered possible?
A) Priority that makes occurrence need no cause
B) Priority that gives nearness to occurrence due to few conditions
C) Priority as a disposition for existence or non-existence not leading to necessity
D) Priority is impossible in all cases

Key Takeaway
The possible has no inherent priority; any claimed priority is either impossible, external, or merely a non-necessary disposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a morally difficult choice you face right now. Do you believe that your own nature inclines you more toward one option (e.g., sin) than the other? How does the truth that you are a 'possible'—with no inherent priority—expose the lie of that belief? What cause will you deliberately seek today to tip the balance toward righteousness?
?
When you fail in a spiritual duty, do you ever comfort yourself with the thought 'it was bound to happen' or 'that's just how I am'? This lesson says the possible has no inherent priority—so your failure was not inevitable. Will you take full responsibility for your actions by tracing them to real causes (negligence, environment) rather than a false 'nature'?
?
You claim to believe that your salvation or ruin depends on your choices and God's grace. But if you secretly assume your spiritual state is 'fixed' (as if you have an inherent priority toward good or bad), then you have denied the very neutrality of the possible that makes moral effort meaningful. Will you reject that false assumption today and actively seek the causes of faith—knowledge, repentance, good company—as if your next step truly hangs in the balance?

The Argument Against Inherent Priority and Its Weakness
Al-Taftazani presents and critiques a proof against inherent priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that attempts to prove the impossibility of inherent priority in the possible, and why al-Taftazani considers it weak.

Core Idea
An argument against inherent priority is itself weak: the priority is not refuted by external causes.

After establishing that the possible has no inherent priority, al-Taftazani presents a proof (istidlal) that argues against the very possibility of such priority. He then evaluates this proof and finds it weak. The argument uses a dilemma: if priority existed, either the other side is impossible (leading to necessity) or possible, and then either it occurs without cause or by cause. The author's critique focuses on what depends on the absence of the cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument's dilemma
Read the first part of the proof: 'إن امتنع مع تلك الأولوية وقوع الطرف الآخر، وجب هذا، وإن أمكن فإما بلا سبب فيترجح المرجوح، أو بسبب...'.
This is a classic two-case dilemma.
2
Identify the two additional points
Look for the phrases 'وبأن اقتضاء التساوي ينافي اقتضاء أولوية' and 'وبأنه إن أمكن زوالها بسبب...'.
These are further sub-arguments.
3
Find the criticism
The author says 'ضعيف لأن المتوقف على عدم ذلك السبب هو الوقوع لا الأولوية'.
The critique distinguishes priority from occurrence.
4
Consider the footnote
The footnote explains that the possible does not have a disposition for equality, so no contradiction arises.
This strengthens the author's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع : لا أولوية لأحد طرفي الممكن نظراً إلى ذاته (۱) ، وقيل بأولوية العدم مطلقاً(۲) ، وقيل في الأعراض السيالة (۳)، والظاهر أنه إن أريد فلأولوية بحيث يستغني الوقوع عن سبب، فضروري البطلان، وإن أريد القرب إلى الوقوع لقلة الشروط والموانع ، وكثرة اتفاق الأسباب فعائد (٤) إلى الغير، وإن أريد اقتضاء ما للوجود أو العدم لا إلى حد الوجوب فمحتمل () والاستدلال على امتناعه بأنه إن امتنع مع تلك الأولوية، وقوع الطرف الآخر، وجب هذا، وإن أمكن فإما بلا سبب فيترجح المرجوح، أو بسبب فيتوقف هذا على عدمه فلا يكون أولى لذاته، وبأن اقتضاء التساوي ينافي اقتضاء أولوية ،أحدهما، وبأنه إن أمكن زوالها بسبب لم تكن ذاتية، بل متوقفة على عدمه، وإن لم يمكن كانت الماهية واجبة أو ممتنعة ضعيف لأن المتوقف على عدم ذلك السبب هو الوقوع لا الأولوية، ولأن (٦) عدم اقتضاء
The Seventh Investigation: There is no priority (awlawiyya) for one of the two sides of the possible (al-mumkin) in respect to its essence (dhāt). And if is meant nearness to occurrence due to fewness of conditions and obstacles and abundance of happening of causes, then it is referred to something else (i.e., not to the essence). And because the disposition for equality (musāwāt) contradicts the disposition for priority of one of them. And because if its removal by a cause were possible, then it would not be essential, but rather dependent on its absence, and if it were not possible, then the essence would be necessary or impossible. [All this] is weak, because what depends on the absence of that cause is the occurrence, not the priority. And because (the footnote): non-disposition

What is al-Taftazani's main criticism of the argument against inherent priority?
A) The argument is logically contradictory
B) The argument confuses priority with occurrence
C) The argument relies on an invalid premise
D) The argument is correct and he accepts it

Key Takeaway
The proof against inherent priority is weak because it confuses the priority itself with the actual occurrence; priority can be a non-essential disposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes measure the reality of your faith by its consistency in external outcomes? How can you separate the inherent value of your intentions from their actual fruition, as the source prioritizes priority over occurrence?
?
Think of a conviction you hold deeply but have seen shaken by external events. Does this lesson give you a way to preserve the priority of that belief even when its actualization seems blocked? What change in your thinking is required to act on that distinction?

Equality of Existence and Non-Existence for the Possible
Majority view of equality and refutation of non-existence preponderance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority Ash'ari view that existence and non-existence of a possible thing are equal in essence, and how the claim of preponderance of non-existence is refuted.

Core Idea
For possible beings, existence and non-existence are equally possible — neither has intrinsic priority.

In this passage, Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani presents the position of the majority (jumhur) that, considered in itself, a possible thing has no inherent leaning towards existence or non-existence. He then reports the opposing view that non-existence is preponderant, along with its arguments, and provides a refutation. This lesson will walk through the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority view
Read the first sentence to see what the majority (jumhur) claim about existence and non-existence of the possible.
Note the phrase 'ala sawā': equal.
2
Understand the opposing argument
The next part presents a claim that non-existence is preponderant, with two reasons. Make sure you grasp those reasons.
First reason: occurs without cause. Second: occurs by missing part of complete cause.
3
Follow the refutation
The refutation begins with 'wa rudda bi-anna...' and shows that the argument does not prove preponderance. Note how it explains the dependency of non-existence on the absence of the cause.
The key point: non-existence also depends on cause (its absence).
4
Check the conclusion
The last part clarifies that missing parts does not imply a disposition for non-existence.
The possible has no inherent inclination to non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجمهور على أن وجود الممكن وعدمه بالنظر إلى ذاته على السواء، لا أولوية لأحدهما عن الآخر، وقيل العدم أولى بالممكن جوهراً كان أو عرضاً زائلاً أو باقياً لتحققه بدون تحقق سبب مؤثر، ولحصوله بانتفاء شيء من أجزاء العلة التامة للوجود المفترق إلى تحقق جميعها. ورد بأن الممكن كما يستند وجوده إلى وجود العلة، يستند عدمه إلى عدمها، ولا معنى لعدم المركب سوى أن لا يتحقق جميع أجزائه، سواء تحقق البعض أو لم يتحقق، وهذا القدر لا يقتضي أولوية العدم بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن بمعنى أن يكون له نوع اقتضاء للعدم.
The majority hold that the existence and non-existence of the possible with respect to its essence are equal; there is no preponderance of one over the other. And it was said: non-existence is more preponderant for the possible, whether it is a substance or an accident, transient or permanent, because it [non-existence] occurs without the occurrence of an effective cause, and because it occurs by the non-existence of some part of the complete cause of existence which is dependent on the realization of all its parts. And it is refuted that the possible, just as its existence depends on the existence of the cause, so its non-existence depends on its non-existence; and there is no meaning to the non-existence of a composite except that not all its parts are realized, whether some parts are realized or not; and this much does not imply preponderance of non-existence with respect to the essence of the possible in the sense that it has a kind of disposition for non-existence.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage presents the majority view: existence and non-existence of the possible are equal with respect to its essence. Then it introduces a contrary view: non-existence is preponderant for all possible things, based on two reasons. First, non-existence occurs without an effective cause. Second, it occurs when any part of the complete cause of existence is missing. The refutation: just as existence depends on the cause, non-existence depends on the absence of the cause. The non-existence of a composite simply means not all its parts are present; that does not imply any intrinsic disposition for non-existence. Thus, the argument for preponderance fails. The structure is thesis, counter-thesis, and refutation.

Key Terms
الجمهور
التساوي
الوجود
العدم
الممكن
العلة التامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence is preponderant because it occurs without a cause.
Correction: Non-existence also depends on the absence of the cause, so it is not intrinsically preponderant; existence and non-existence are equally dependent on the cause or its absence.

Checkpoint
What is the majority view regarding existence and non-existence of the possible?
A) Existence is preponderant.
B) Non-existence is preponderant.
C) They are equal with respect to its essence.
D) Existence is preponderant for substances, non-existence for accidents.

Key Takeaway
For possible beings, existence and non-existence are equally possible; the claim that non-existence is preponderant is refuted by showing its dependence on the absence of the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence at this very moment is only as likely as your non-existence, what does that mean for your sense of security? Do you truly live with the awareness that your continued existence depends entirely on the constant presence of a cause beyond yourself?
?
The passage refutes the claim that non-existence is the default. When you consider your own death, do you treat it as something that merely happens on its own, or do you recognize it as an event that also requires a cause? How should this shape your preparation for what follows?
?
If your existence and non-existence are equal in themselves, what stops you from being annihilated right now? How does this direct you to gratitude and reliance on the One who continuously grants you existence?

The Equality of Existence and Non-Existence for the Contingent
Al-Taftazani states the majority view and refutes the claim that non-existence is prior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the majority holds that existence and non-existence are equal for the contingent, and how the argument for priority of non-existence is refuted.

Core Idea
Existence and non-existence are equally possible for the contingent.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic theology: whether non-existence is prior to existence for contingent things. After stating the majority view, al-Taftazani presents and refutes the claim that non-existence has priority. Understanding this argument clarifies the contingency argument for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the debate settle in your mind.
The passage is a self-contained argument: majority view, opposing claim with two reasons, rebuttal.
2
Identify the three sections
Note where the majority view ends, where the opposing view begins, and where the rebuttal starts. The passage is structured as a micro-argument.
The key transitional phrase is 'وقيل العدم أولى' (And it was said: non-existence is prior).
3
Analyze the rebuttal
Focus on the rebuttal: it argues that non-existence of a composite simply means not all parts are realized, and that non-existence depends on the absence of the cause, just as existence depends on the cause. This undercuts the claim of priority.
The rebuttal does not deny that non-existence occurs without a cause; it denies that this gives non-existence priority in the essence of the contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجمهور على أن وجود الممكن وعدمه بالنظر إلى ذاته على السواء، لا أولوية لأحدهما عن الآخر، وقيل العدم أولى بالممكن جوهراً كان أو عرضاً زائلاً أو باقياً لتحققه بدون تحقق سبب مؤثر، ولحصوله بانتفاء شيء من أجزاء العلة التامة للوجود المفترق إلى تحقق جميعها . ورد بأن الممكن كما يستند وجوده إلى وجود العلة، يستند عدمه إلى عدمها، ولا معنى لعدم المركب سوى أن لا يتحقق جميع أجزائه، سواء تحقق البعض أو لم يتحقق، وهذا القدر لا يقتضي أولوية العدم بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن بمعنى أن يكون له نوع اقتضاء للعدم .
The majority hold that the existence of the contingent and its non-existence, with respect to its essence, are equal, no priority of one over the other. And it was said: non-existence is prior for the contingent, whether substance or accident, transient or permanent, because it occurs without the occurrence of an efficient cause, and because it occurs by the non-existence of any part of the complete cause of existence, which requires the realization of all its parts. The reply is that just as the existence of the contingent depends on the existence of the cause, so its non-existence depends on its non-existence. There is no meaning to the non-existence of a composite except that not all its parts are realized, whether some parts are realized or not. This does not necessitate the priority of non-existence with respect to the essence of the contingent, in the sense that it has a kind of inclination for non-existence.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage sets out the position of the majority (al-jumhūr) regarding the contingent (al-mumkin). The majority holds that existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the essence of the contingent; neither has priority over the other. Then the passage presents an opposing view: some claim that non-existence is prior to the contingent, giving two reasons: first, non-existence occurs without an efficient cause, and second, it occurs by the mere absence of any part of the complete cause. The passage then gives the rebuttal: just as existence depends on the cause, so does non-existence depend on the absence of the cause. The non-existence of a composite merely means that not all its parts are realized; this does not imply that non-existence is inherently prior to existence. The rebuttal shows that the claimed priority does not follow from the premises.

Key Terms
الممكن
العدَم
أولوية
العلة التامة
اقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because non-existence occurs without a cause, it is inherently prior to existence for contingent things.
Correction: The passage shows that non-existence also depends on the absence of the cause; it is not a positive state that can be prior. The majority holds that existence and non-existence are equally possible.

Checkpoint
According to the majority (al-jumhūr), what is the relationship between existence and non-existence with respect to the essence of the contingent?
A) Existence is prior
B) Non-existence is prior
C) They are equal
D) Neither can be said

Key Takeaway
The contingent has no inherent priority of existence or non-existence; both depend on an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that your existence or circumstances are 'unfair' or 'lacking,' do you secretly assume that non-existence or a different state would be inherently better? How does this lesson's equal possibility challenge that assumption?
?
If your own contingent existence depends entirely on an external cause, what does that imply about your self-sufficiency? In what areas of your life do you still act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The rebuttal shows that non-existence is not a positive property. How often do you attach positive qualities to 'nothingness' or 'absence' (e.g., thinking that if something were gone, it would be better)? Can you name one thing you need to stop prioritizing over existence as Allah has given it?

Three Meanings of Priority and the Refutation of Accidents
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim of priority for accidents and distinguishes three meanings of priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible meanings of 'priority' in the context of contingent existence and why only one of them is rationally defensible.

Core Idea
Priority can mean essence-driven, external-driven, or inclination — only one avoids contradiction.

Al-Taftazani responds to the claim that non-existence is prior for transitory accidents. He argues that the meaning of 'priority' must be clarified. By distinguishing three senses, he shows that the objection either collapses into impossibility or is harmless.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The passage begins with an objection: non-existence is prior for transitory accidents like motion and sound because they cannot persist.
This sets up the problem.
2
First meaning of priority
If priority means preponderance by essence without external cause, this would turn a contingent into necessary or impossible, which is impossible.
This is the key refutation.
3
Second meaning of priority
If priority means being closer to occurrence due to fewer conditions or more causes, then it is priority by another, not by itself, and is harmless.
4
Third meaning of priority
If priority means the intellect finds an inclination toward existence or non-existence short of necessity, its impossibility is not evident.
This leaves room for such a possibility.
5
Synthesize the lesson
The objection fails because it does not specify which meaning of priority it intends; only the first meaning leads to a contradiction, but it is not what the objector likely means.
Al-Taftazani successfully defends contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : العدم أولى بالأعراض السيالة كالحركة والزمان والصوت وصفاتها، بدليل امتناع البقاء عليها، والذي يقتضيه النظر الصائب أنه أريد بأولوية الوجود أو العدم ترجحه بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن، بحيث يقع بلا سبب خارج، فبطلانه ضروري ، لأنه حينئذ يكون واجباً، أو ممتنعاً، لا ممكناً. فإن قيل : هذا إنما يلزم لو لم يكن وقوع الطرف الآخر بمرجح خارجي . قلنا : فيتوقف وقوع الطرف الأولى إلى عدم المرجح الخارجي، وإن أريد بالأولوية كونه أقرب إلى الوقوع لقلة شروطه وموانعه، وكثرة اتفاق أسبابه، فهذه أولوية بالغير لا بالذات وهو ظاهر. وإن أريد أن الممكن قد يكون بحيث إذ لاحظه العقل وجد فيه نوع اقتضاء للوجود أو للعدم لا إلى حد الوجوب ليلزم كونه واجباً أو ممتنعاً، فلا يظهر امتناعه.
And it was said: Non-existence is prior for transitory accidents such as motion, time, sound, and their attributes, due to the impossibility of their persistence. What sound consideration requires is that by priority of existence or non-existence is meant its preponderance with respect to the essence of the contingent, such that it occurs without an external cause. Its falsity is necessary, for then it would be necessary or impossible, not contingent. If it is said: This only follows if the occurrence of the other side is not by an external preponderator. We say: Then the occurrence of the first side depends on the absence of the external preponderator. And if by priority is meant its being closer to occurrence due to fewer conditions and obstacles and more frequent coincidences of its causes, then this is priority by another, not by essence, and this is apparent. And if it is meant that the contingent may be such that when the intellect observes it, it finds in it a kind of inclination for existence or non-existence not to the extent of necessity, so that it would be necessary or impossible, then its impossibility does not appear.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection: some claim that non-existence is prior for transitory accidents because they cannot persist. The author then refutes this by analyzing the meaning of priority. He distinguishes three possible meanings. First, if priority means preponderance by essence such that the contingent occurs without any external cause, then this is impossible because it would make the contingent necessary or impossible. To the objection that this would only be problematic if the other side occurs without an external preponderator, the author replies that then the occurrence of the first side would depend on the absence of that preponderator, so it is not inherently prior. Second, if priority means being closer to occurrence due to fewer conditions, etc., then this is priority by another, not by essence, and thus poses no problem. Third, if priority means that the intellect finds in the contingent some inclination for existence or non-existence short of necessity, then its impossibility is not evident. This response refutes the objection by clarifying what is at stake.

Key Terms
أعراض سيالة
أولوية
ترجح
مرجح خارجي
اقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-existence is inherently prior for transitory accidents, as a property of their essence.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that any priority is either due to external conditions or a non-necessary inclination, not an inherent property.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the first meaning of priority (preponderance by essence without external cause)?
A) It is necessary
B) It is impossible
C) It is possible
D) It is apparent

Key Takeaway
The apparent priority of non-existence for transitory accidents is not an inherent property but results from external conditions or a non-necessary inclination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own existence, do you ever feel it is 'natural' or 'self-sufficient'? How does this lesson's reminder that nothing occurs without an external preponderator shake that assumption, and what one thing will you do today to consciously attribute your existence to Allah?
?
The passage distinguishes priority by essence from priority by conditions. Are there beliefs or habits in your life that you treat as 'necessary' rather than contingent? What would change if you saw them as dependent on Allah's will and not on their own nature?
?
Al-Taftazani refutes the idea that non-existence is inherently prior for accidents. Do you ever subconsciously think that non-existence or failure is more 'natural' for you than success? How can you replace that assumption with a trust that Allah creates existence and goodness directly, without any inherent priority of non-existence?

First Proof on Inherent Priority
Al-Taftazani presents the first proof: inherent priority leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first proof against inherent priority and the counter-response that limits its scope.

Core Idea
Inherent priority leads to contradiction unless it does not imply necessity.

This passage presents the first argument against the claim that existence or non-existence is inherently prior for a contingent being. Al-Taftazani constructs a reductio ad absurdum, then offers a response that clarifies the intended meaning of priority. The proof is a classic example of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional reasoning: 'If one side is prior... then either the other side is impossible or possible.'
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Trace the two possibilities
The proof considers two cases: if the other side is impossible, the prior side becomes necessary. If the other side is possible, its occurrence either has no cause or depends on a cause's absence.
Both lead to contradiction.
3
Examine the answer
The answer argues that the proof only works if priority means necessity. If priority is merely preponderance without necessity, the conclusion does not follow.
This limits the proof's scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أنه لو كان أحد الطرفين أولى بالممكن نظر إلى ذاته، فمع تلك الأولوية، إما أن يمتنع وقوع الطرف الآخر فيكون الطرف الأولى واجباً لذات الممكن فلا يكون ممكناً، بل واجباً أو ممتنعاً هذا خلف وإنما يمكن، وحينئذ فوقوعه إما أن يكون بلا سبب يرجحه، فيلزم ترجيح المرجوح . أعني الطرف الغير الأولى، أو يكون بسبب بغير رجحانه فيكون وقوع الطرف الأولى متوقفاً على عدم ذلك السبب، فلا يكون أولى بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن بل مع عدم ذلك السبب هذا خلف . والجواب : أنه لا يلزم من توقف الوقوع على أمر توقف الأولوية عليه، حتى يلزم كونها غير ذاتية، وذلك لأن التقدير أن المراد بها رجحان ما لا إلى حد الوجوب .
First: If one of the two sides were prior for the contingent with respect to its essence, then with that priority, either the occurrence of the other side is impossible, making the first side necessary by the essence of the contingent, so it would not be contingent but necessary or impossible—this is contrary to the hypothesis — and then its occurrence would either be without a cause that preponderates it, leading to preponderance of the weaker, i.e., the non-prior side, or it would be due to a cause without its preponderance, so the occurrence of the prior side would depend on the absence of that cause, and thus it would not be prior with respect to the essence of the contingent but rather with the absence of that cause—this is a contradiction. The answer: It does not follow from the dependence of occurrence on something that the priority depends on it, such that it would be non-essential, because the assumption is that what is meant by priority is a preponderance not to the extent of necessity.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-awwal) against the claim that one side of existence or non-existence is inherently prior for the contingent. The proof argues by reductio: assume one side is prior by essence. Then two possibilities: either the other side is impossible (which would make the prior side necessary or impossible, contradicting contingency), or the other side is possible. If it is possible, then the occurrence of the prior side is either without any preponderating cause (which would be preponderance of the weaker, impossible) or due to a cause that does not make it preponderant (which then makes its priority dependent on the absence of that cause, so it is not essential priority). Therefore, the assumption leads to contradiction. The answer (al-Jawāb) is that this proof only works if priority means preponderance to the point of necessity, but the advocate of priority may mean a preponderance that is not to that extent, so the proof does not refute it.

Key Terms
أولوية ذاتية
تمتنع
وجوب
خلف
ترجيح المرجوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any inherent priority implies necessity to the point that the opposite is impossible.
Correction: The answer clarifies that inherent priority can be a preponderance that does not reach necessity, so the opposite remains possible.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, what does the opponent argue would happen if the other side is impossible?
A) The prior side becomes contingent
B) The prior side becomes necessary
C) The prior side becomes non-existent
D) The proof fails

Key Takeaway
The first proof against inherent priority shows that absolute priority leads to contradiction, but priority reinterpreted as mere preponderance remains tenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you assume that your own inclinations and priorities are inherently justified, or do you examine whether they are based on a cause? This lesson forces you to recognize that priority without a cause is a logical contradiction. Where in your life do you act on sheer preference without reason or cause, and how does that resemble the 'preponderance of the weaker'?
?
Consider a belief you hold strongly. Do you consider it necessary or merely preponderant? If it is not necessary, are you open to the possibility that it could be otherwise? This lesson challenges the assumption that strong conviction implies necessity.
?
The answer in the lesson reinterprets priority to avoid contradiction. Is there an area of your religious understanding where you are holding onto a position that seems logically contradictory? How can you reinterpret it without compromising truth, as al-Taftazani does?

Second Proof: Equality Implied by Contingents
Al-Taftazani presents the second proof and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that contingents imply equality and how al-Taftazani refutes it by distinguishing between implying equality and not implying occurrence.

Core Idea
Contingents do not imply equality; they simply do not determine either side.

Al-Taftazani presents a second argument that seems to force contingents into equality, but he then shows why this argument fails. The trick is to see that 'not implying occurrence' is not the same as 'implying equality'. This lesson unpacks that subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the proof statement carefully. Notice that it claims the contingent implies equality because neither existence nor non-existence comes from itself.
2
Identify the contradiction
The proof argues that if the contingent inherently implied one side, it would lead to a contradiction: both implying equality (by its nature) and not implying equality (by the supposed inherent preference).
3
Read the answer
Now read al-Taftazani's response. He denies that the contingent implies equality. Instead, he says it simply does not imply the occurrence of either side.
This is the key move.
4
Understand the distinction
Understand that not implying occurrence is different from implying equality. The former leaves open the possibility of an external priority (أولوية) without reaching necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الممكن يقتضي تساوي الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى ذاته، لما أن كلا منهما لا يكون إلا بالغير، فلو اقتضى أحدهما لذاته لزم اجتماع المتنافيين. أعني اقتضاء التساوي ولا اقتضاءه . والجواب : أنا لا نسلم أن الممكن يقتضي تساوي الطرفين، بل لا يقتضي وقوع أحدهما وهو لا ينافي اقتضاء أحدهما لا إلى حد الوجوب، والوقوع على ما هو المراد بالأولوية .
Second: The contingent implies the equality of existence and non-existence with respect to its essence, because each of them only occurs through another. So if it implied one of them by essence, the two contradictories would be combined: i.e., implication of equality and non-implication of it. The answer: We do not grant that the contingent implies equality of the two sides; rather, it does not imply the occurrence of either one, and that does not contradict the implication of one side not to the extent of necessity, which is what is meant by priority.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-thānī). It argues that the contingent, by its nature, requires (yaqtaḍī) the equality of existence and non-existence because both come from an external cause. If it inherently implied one side, then it would both imply equality (by its nature) and not imply equality (by the supposed inherent priority), which is a contradiction. The answer (al-Jawāb) denies the premise: the contingent does not imply equality, but rather does not imply the occurrence of either side. This non-implication does not contradict an inherent inclination (iqtiḍā') for one side as long as that inclination does not reach the level of necessity. Thus the proof fails because it assumes a stronger sense of 'implication' than is intended.

Key Terms
اقتضاء التساوي
المتنافيين
نفي الإقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof shows that contingents must be equal in terms of having no inherent preference.
Correction: The answer shows that contingents have no inherent determination, but they can have an inclination (priority) from an external cause without implying equality.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani's answer to the second proof, does the contingent imply equality of existence and non-existence?
A) Yes, by its essence
B) No, it does not imply equality
C) Yes, but only accidentally
D) No, it implies non-existence

Key Takeaway
The second proof fails because it conflates the contingent's lack of inherent determination with an implication of equality; in reality, the contingent simply does not determine itself, leaving room for priority from an external cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you rely on your own judgment as if you are self-sufficient, or do you actively seek Allah's priority through prayer and reflection? How does the lesson's emphasis on contingency challenge your sense of independence?
?
The proof wrongly assumed that not having a strong leaning means you are completely neutral. Are there areas of your faith where you have used 'balance' as an excuse to avoid taking a firm stance on what Allah has made clear? What will you do to align your priorities with His?
?
If every moment of your existence depends on Allah's constant will, does that fill you with awe or with the weight of accountability? How can you concretely remember this dependence in your daily actions starting today?

Third Proof: The Dilemma of Inherent Priority
Al-Taftazani presents the third proof as a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against inherent priority in contingents and why it poses a dilemma for its proponents.

Core Idea
Inherent priority cannot be both essential and removable.

This lesson introduces a key kalam argument that challenges the notion of inherent priority in contingent beings. Al-Taftazani presents a dilemma: if priority is essential, it cannot be removed; if it can be removed, it is not essential. This reasoning exposes the logical difficulty in maintaining that any side in a contingency has an inherent advantage. We will work through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's concise style and the structure of the dilemma become clear.
Notice that the answer is cut off; focus only on the proof as presented.
2
Analyze the two branches
Identify the two branches: (1) priority can be removed by a cause → it is not essential; (2) priority cannot be removed → the side would be necessary, contradicting contingency. Consider why each branch is problematic.
Essential properties (bi al-dhat) are those that cannot be removed by an external cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أنه لو كان أحد الطرفين أولى لذات الممكن، فإما أن يمكن زوال تلك الأولوية بسبب أو لا . فإن أمكن لم تكن الأولوية ذاتية لتوقفها على ذلك السبب، ولأن ما بالذات لا يزول بالغير، وإن لم يمكن كان الطرف . والجواب : ...
Third: If one of the two sides were prior for the essence of the contingent, then either it is possible for that priority to disappear by a cause or not. If it is possible, then the priority would not be essential, because it depends on that cause, and also because what is by essence does not disappear by another. If it is not possible, then the side (would be necessary?). The answer: ... [incomplete]
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-thālith). It is a dilemma: if one side is inherently prior, either that priority can be removed by an external cause or it cannot. If it can be removed, then it is not essential (dhātiyya) because essential properties cannot be removed by something else. If it cannot be removed, then the prior side would be necessary (since its priority is permanent and essential), contradicting its contingency. The answer, which is cut off in the text, presumably argues that the priority in question is not essential in the strong sense that it cannot be removed, or that it is not to the degree of necessity. The passage remains incomplete in this portion.

Key Terms
زوال الأولوية
ذاتية
بالغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Essential properties can be removed by external causes.
Correction: Essential properties (ma bi al-dhat) are defined as those that cannot be removed by an external cause. If a property can be removed, it is not essential.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, if the priority can be removed by a cause, what follows?
A) The priority is essential
B) The priority is not essential
C) The contingent becomes necessary
D) The priority disappears completely

Key Takeaway
The third proof uses a dilemma to show that inherent priority in contingents leads to contradiction, thereby supporting the Ash'ari position that all possibilities are equally balanced prior to divine determination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something you believe about yourself that you consider essential—part of your very nature. Can you honestly say it could never be changed by circumstances or effort? If it could be removed, does that mean it was never truly essential? How does this dilemma expose the risk of mistaking habit for essence?
?
The proof shows that if a priority cannot be removed, it becomes a necessity. Are there areas of your life—behaviors, beliefs, or commitments—that you treat as necessary when they are actually choices? What would it cost you to admit they are not inherent?
?
When have you clung to a priority as 'just the way you are' only to later find it was not essential? How does this logical dilemma challenge you to re-examine what you hold most firmly? Will you take time this week to test one of your deeply held assumptions?

The Second Objection: Possible Necessitates Equality — Refuted
Second objection and its refutation regarding possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that a possible being necessitates equality is flawed, and how the author defends preponderance without compromising the nature of the possible.

Core Idea
A possible does not necessitate equality; it simply does not necessitate either side, allowing preponderance.

This passage presents the second objection against the first position: if a possible being is inherently neutral, doesn't that force equality? The opponent argues yes, but the author refutes by clarifying what the possible does and does not necessitate. The key is that non-necessitation is not the same as necessitating equality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice the claim: the possible necessitates equality of existence and non-existence with respect to its essence.
The objection uses the concept of 'necessitating equality' (اقتضاء التساوي).
2
Identify the refutation's main point
The author denies the claim: 'We do not concede that the possible necessitates equality.' Instead, the possible does not necessitate the occurrence of either side. Understand this distinction.
3
Connect to the broader argument
The refutation allows preponderance (أولوية) from an external cause. The possible remains neutral in itself, so an external preponderance does not contradict its essence.
This preserves the rational principle that a possible requires a preponderant cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الممكن يقتضي تساوي الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى ذاته، لما أن كلا منهما لا يكون إلا بالغير، فلو اقتضى أحدهما لذاته لزم اجتماع المتنافيين أعني اقتضاء التساوي ولا اقتضاءه. والجواب : أنا لا نسلم أن الممكن يقتضي تساوي الطرفين، بل لا يقتضي وقوع أحدهما وهو لا ينافي اقتضاء أحدهما لا إلى حد الوجوب، والوقوع على ما هو المراد بالأولوية.
The second: That the possible necessitates the equality of existence and non-existence with respect to its essence, because each of them only occurs through another, so if it necessitated one of them by itself, the two contradictory things would come together—namely, necessitating equality and not necessitating it. And the answer: We do not concede that the possible necessitates the equality of the two sides; rather, it does not necessitate the occurrence of either, and that does not negate one of them being necessitated not to the point of necessity, and occurrence is what is meant by preponderance.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage contains the second objection and its refutation. The objection claims that a possible being inherently neutral necessitates equality of existence and non-existence, so any preponderance would contradict its essence. The author refutes by denying that the possible necessitates equality; it simply does not necessitate either side. This non-necessitation is compatible with an external preponderance that does not reach the point of necessity. The author reiterates that what is meant by preponderance here is occurrence, not necessity.

Key Terms
اقتضاء التساوي (necessitating equality)
متنافيان (contradictories)
اقتضاء (necessitation)
أولوية (preponderance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a possible does not necessitate either existence or non-existence, then both must be equally balanced.
Correction: Non-necessitation does not imply equality; it only means the possible itself does not determine an outcome. An external cause can tip the balance.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, what does the possible necessitate with respect to existence and non-existence?
A) It necessitates equality.
B) It necessitates one of them.
C) It does not necessitate either.
D) It necessitates both.

Key Takeaway
A possible being does not necessitate equality; it is neutral, allowing preponderance from an external cause without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult choice between two seemingly equal options, do you assume your own mind must be neutral, or do you seek preponderance from Allah? How does this lesson's distinction between 'necessitating equality' and 'not necessitating either side' affect your approach to seeking guidance?
?
Have you ever felt paralyzed by the assumption that because something is 'possible' it must be equally likely, causing you to hesitate? How can the truth that a possible being simply does not necessitate either side—yet preponderance can come from outside—free you to act with trust in Allah's will?
?
Reflect on a time you attributed your success or failure solely to your own effort, forgetting that preponderance comes from an external cause. How does this lesson challenge you to acknowledge that occurrence itself is from Allah, not from the possible's own nature?
